Fainting, Madness, and Intoxication as Nullifiers of Wudu
Expansion on three wudu nullifiers with definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions that nullify wudu and explain why imbecility does not nullify.

Core Idea
Loss of intellect or control nullifies wudu; imbecility is akin to childhood and does not.

In this lesson, we examine three states that remove a person's full faculties and thus invalidate wudu: fainting, madness, and intoxication. The definitions provided in the text help distinguish these from a condition like imbecility, which is treated similarly to a child's state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three nullifiers
Note that fainting (ighmā'), madness (junūn), and intoxication (sukr) are explicitly stated as nullifiers of wudu. Recognize each definition.
Fainting removes strength and veils intellect; madness removes intellect and increases strength; intoxication causes swaying and slurred speech.
2
Understand the rationale
All three conditions involve a loss of control or intellect. The Hanafi school considers this sufficient to break wudu because the person is no longer fully conscious.
Imbecility (atah) does not cause such loss, so it is not a nullifier.
3
Distinguish imbecility
Imbecility is not a nullifier; the text treats it like childhood. Even if the person's worship is valid, their wudu remains intact unless another nullifier occurs.
This is a key distinction: not all mental conditions nullify wudu.
4
Note the exception for intoxication
Intoxication nullifies wudu regardless of the substance (permissible or forbidden). However, it does not exempt the person from making up missed prayers because it is self-inflicted.
This differs from fainting, where prayers are not required during the unconscious state.
5
Recognize the dual definition of intoxication
The text notes that the definition of intoxication for the purpose of wudu is different from that used in the chapter on hadd (penalty). For wudu, the criterion is swaying and slurred speech; for hadd, it is incoherent speech or not knowing earth from sky.
This shows that a definition may vary according to legal context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ينقضه (إغماء) وهو مرض يزيل القوى ويستر العقل. و ينقضه (جنون) وهو مرض يزيل العقل ويزيد القوى. و ينقضه (سكر) وهو خفة يظهر أثرها بالتمايل وتلعثم الكلام لزوال القوة الماسكة بظلمة الصدر وعدم انتفاع القلب بالعقل. قوله: (وهو مرض يزيل القوى) بسبب امتلاء بطون الدماغ من البلغم البارد، وتعطل القوى المدركة والمحركة عن أفعالها . بقاء العقل مغلوباً، أو الغشي بفتح فسكون، أو بكسر الشين تشديد الياء نوع منه، مع وكلاهما ناقض وأما العته فهو غير ناقض لحكمهم على العبادة بالصحة معه، وإن لم يكن مكلفاً بها لإلحاقه بالصبي لا لأن عقله قد زال أفاده السيد. قوله: (وهو خفة الخ ) قال بعضهم : هو سرور يغلب على العقل بمباشرة بعض الأسباب الموجبة له، فيمنع الإنسان عن العمل بموجب عقله من غير أن يزيله، ولذا بقي أهلاً للخطاب وقيل يزيله، وتكليفه زجر له والتحقيق الأول كما في البحر، ولا فرق فيه بين السكر من محرم، أو مباح فهو كالإغماء إلا أنه لا يسقط عنه القضاء، وإن كان أكثر من يوم وليلة لأنه بصنعه بخلاف الإغماء. قوله : يظهر أثرها بالتمايل هذا التعريف باتفاق هنا كما في الحلبي كما أنه باتفاق في الإيمان أن يهذي ويخلط في أكثر كلامه كما صرح به الزيلعي في كتاب الحدود، واختلف في حده في باب الحد. فقال الإمام : هو أن لا يعرف الأرض من السماء، ولا الرجال من النساء لأنّ الحدّ عقوبة يحتال لدرئها فيعتبر نهاية السكر، وقالا : هو أن يهذي في كلامه لأنه هو السكر في العرف قال في النهر : وينبغي النقض بأكل الحشيشة إذ دخل في مشيته إختلال قوله: الزوال القوة الماسكة) علة للخفة الموصوفة بما بعدها، وقوله وعدم إنتفاع عطف على زوال قوله : (بالعقل) هو في الرأس، وشعاعه في الصدر، والقلب، أو بالقلب، فالقلب يهتدى بنوره لتدبير الأمور، وتمييز الحسن من القبيح قاله في الشرح
And it is nullified by fainting (ighmā'), which is a disease that removes strength and veils the intellect. And it is nullified by madness (junūn), which is a disease that removes intellect and increases strength. And it is nullified by intoxication (sukr), which is lightness whose effect appears in swaying and slurred speech due to the loss of the controlling strength by darkness of the chest and the heart not benefiting from the intellect. ... (commentary on definitions) ... The commentary explains that fainting occurs due to imbalance of phlegm in the brain, and both fainting and unconsciousness (ghashy) are nullifiers. However, imbecility ('atah) is not a nullifier because a person with it is treated like a child in terms of valid worship. For intoxication, it is defined as a lightness affecting control; it is like fainting but does not exempt from making up prayers because it is self-induced. The definition of intoxication in the chapter on hadd is different: Abu Hanifa says it is not knowing the earth from the sky, while the two disciples say it is incoherent speech. The text also notes that eating hashish requires nullification if it causes imbalance in walk.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage lists three additional nullifiers of wudu: fainting (ighmā'), madness (junūn), and intoxication (sukr). All are conditions that remove full control of one's faculties. Fainting is a disease that takes away strength and hides the intellect. Madness removes the intellect while increasing physical strength. Intoxication produces a lightness seen in swaying and slurred speech, due to loss of the controlling power. The commentary adds that fainting is caused by an imbalance of cold phlegm in the brain; both fainting and unconsciousness (ghashy) are nullifiers. However, simplemindedness ('atah) does not nullify because it is treated like childhood. For intoxication, the definition differs for hadd (penalty) but the nullification of wudu is clear. The text also states that eating hashish nullifies if it causes imbalance in one's walk.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighmā'): Fainting; a condition where strength and consciousness are temporarily lost.
جنون (junūn): Madness; loss of intellect with preservation or increase of physical strength.
سكر (sukr): Intoxication; a state of lightness leading to loss of motor control and coherent speech.
عته (atah): Imbecility or simplemindedness; not a nullifier because it is akin to childhood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following does NOT nullify wudu according to the Hanafi school?
A) Fainting (ighmā')
B) Madness (junūn)
C) Imbecility ('atah)
D) Intoxication (sukr)

Key Takeaway
Fainting, madness, and intoxication nullify wudu; imbecility does not.

Laughter During Prayer and Its Effect on Wudu
Ruling on loud laughter in prayer nullifying wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qahqaha in prayer, explain why it nullifies wudu despite not being a real hadath, and apply the ruling to distinguish between loud laughter and other sounds.

Core Idea
Loud laughter in prayer nullifies wudu, even though it is not a genuine impurity – a unique Hanafi dispensation.

The Hanafi school has a distinctive ruling regarding loud laughter (qahqaha) during prayer: it nullifies both the prayer and the wudu, whether intentional or forgetful. However, this nullification is not because laughter is considered a physical impurity (hadath). Instead, it is a special ruling based on analogy and juristic reasoning. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind this unique position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is loud laughter (qahqaha) occurring during prayer. The hukm: it nullifies wudu (and also the prayer).
This is specific to a mature (baligh) person praying.
2
Recognize the unique nature of the nullifier
The text explicitly states that loud laughter is not a real hadath (like urine or wind). If it were, it would nullify wudu in all states, not just prayer. This shows the ruling is exceptional.
Other nullifiers (e.g., breaking wind) nullify wudu regardless of context.
3
Trace the dalil: Qiyas Jaliy
The ruling is established by analogy (qiyas). The passage compares laughter to crying and speech – sounds that do not nullify wudu. Yet laughter is treated differently because of a specific dispensation.
The exact analogy chain is not fully given in the surviving text, but the principle is clear.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and practical implication
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) do not consider laughter a nullifier of wudu. The practical consequence: a Hanafi must repeat wudu after loud laughter in prayer; a Shafi'i would not.
This is a mukhtalaf fih issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ينقضه (قهقهة) مصل (بالغ) عمداً أو سهواً وهي ما . . . . هي ليست حدثاً حقيقة وإلا لاستوى فيها جميع الأحوال مع أنها مخصوصة ببعضها، وهو الموافق للقياس لأنها ليست بخارج نجس بل هي صوت كالبكاء، والكلام
And it is nullified by loud laughter of a praying mature person, intentionally or forgetfully, and it is that which... It is not a real hadath, otherwise it would be equal in all states, but it is specific to some, and this is in accordance with analogy because it is not an impure excretion, but rather a sound like crying and speech.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage discusses laughter during prayer as a nullifier of wudu. The Hanafi school holds that loud laughter (qahqaha) in prayer, whether intentional or by mistake, nullifies both the prayer and the wudu. The text explains that laughter is not a real hadath (like urine or wind), because if it were, it would nullify wudu in all situations, but it only does so in prayer. This is based on analogy (qiyas): laughter is a sound like crying or talking, not an impure substance, so it is not a genuine nullifier. However, the ruling is that it does nullify wudu due to a special dispensation (rukhsah). The significance is that this is specific to the Hanafi school; other schools may not consider laughter a nullifier of wudu.

Key Terms
قهقهة (qahqaha): Loud, exuberant laughter that can be heard by others, distinct from a slight smile (tabassum) or silent chuckle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person prays and lets out a loud laughter (qahqaha). According to Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling on his wudu?
A) His wudu remains intact because laughter is not a real hadath.
B) His wudu is nullified only if he laughed intentionally.
C) His wudu is nullified regardless of intention.
D) His wudu is nullified only if he laughed after the prayer.

Key Takeaway
Loud laughter (qahqaha) in prayer nullifies wudu regardless of intention, even though it is not a genuine hadath, due to a specific Hanafi dispensation.

Heavy Sleep Nullifies Wudu When Seat Lifts
Ruling on heavy sleep nullifying wudu with condition of seat lifting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on heavy sleep nullifying wudu and identify the condition (seat lifting) that determines nullification.

Core Idea
Heavy sleep nullifies wudu only when the sitting place lifts — nullification depends on loss of control, not sleep itself.

You may have heard that sleep nullifies wudu in the Hanafi school. But the ruling is more precise: it is not all sleep, but only heavy sleep that causes loss of control over the seat. This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya refines the condition, explaining when the nullification applies and when it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Heavy sleep (al-nawm al-thaqīl) is a nullifier of wudu. The ruling is that it nullifies wudu.
The nullification is not automatic; it depends on a condition.
2
Locate the condition
The nullification occurs when the seat of a person sitting on the ground is lifted before waking, indicating deep sleep and loss of control.
Even if the person does not fall to the ground, the lifting of the seat is sufficient.
3
Understand the refinement
If the person wakes momentarily each time they fall (e.g., nodding), then wudu is not nullified. If they remain continuously asleep and then wake, wudu is nullified because of reclining sleep.
This is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa.
4
Note the authoritative sources
Al-Tabyeen, al-Fath, al-Mudmarat, and al-Siraj affirm this position. It is the apparent view and the basis for fatwa.
Another narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad also supports it.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if a person is sitting and falls asleep such that their bottom lifts off the ground, wudu is nullified. If they merely nod off without seat lifting, it is not nullified.
This distinguishes heavy sleep from light sleep.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا فهو الثقيل ناقض (و) ينقضه (ارتفاع مقعدة) قاعد (نائم) على الأرض (قبل انتباهه وإن لم يسقط على الأرض (في الظاهر) من المذهب لزوال المقعدة ... قوله: (وينقضه إرتفاع مقعدة الخ) فقيل إن انتبه كلما سقط، فلا ينتقض وإن استقر نائماً، ثم انتبه إنتقض لوجود النوم مضطجعاً هذا قول الإمام. قال في التبيين، وهو الظاهر وفي الفتح، وعليه الفتوى وفي المضمرات عن الزاد وهو الصحيح في رواية الحسن وبه جزم في السراج
Otherwise it is heavy [sleep and] nullifying. And it [heavy sleep] is nullified by the rising of the sitting place of a person sitting [and] sleeping on the ground before his waking, even if he did not fall to the ground, in the apparent [view] of the school, because of the removal of the sitting place. ... His statement: 'And it is nullified by the rising of the sitting place etc.' So it is said: if he wakes up every time he falls, then it is not nullified; and if he remains sleeping then wakes up, it is nullified due to the occurrence of sleep while reclining. This is the saying of Imam [Abu Hanifa]. He said in al-Tabyeen: it is the apparent [view], and in al-Fath: and upon it is the fatwa, and in al-Mudmarat from al-Zad, it is the correct [view] in the narration of al-Hasan, and he affirmed it in al-Siraj.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of heavy sleep (al-nawm al-thaqīl) as a nullifier of wudū'. The base text from Maraqi al-Falah states that heavy sleep nullifies wudu when the sitting place (maq‘ada) of a person sitting on the ground is lifted before waking, even if he did not fall. This is the apparent position of the Hanafi school. The commentator, al-Tahtawi, elaborates on an intra-Hanafi dispute: If the person wakes momentarily upon each fall (e.g., nodding), then wudu is not nullified. If he remains continuously sleeping and then wakes, wudu is nullified due to the presence of reclining sleep. This is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa. Al-Tabyeen and al-Fath consider this the apparent ruling, and it is the basis for fatwa. Another narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad reports it as correct, and it is affirmed in al-Siraj. The discussion revolves around whether the sleep is heavy enough to cause loss of control. The key condition is the lifting of the sitting place, indicating deep sleep.

Key Terms
النوم الثقيل (al-nawm al-thaqīl): Sleep so deep that the seat (maq‘ada) lifts when sitting on the ground, indicating loss of control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does heavy sleep nullify wudu?
A) Whenever a person falls asleep, regardless of position.
B) Only when the person falls asleep in a reclining position and the seat lifts before waking.
C) Only if the person is lying on his side.
D) Only if the sleep lasts more than an hour.

Key Takeaway
Heavy sleep nullifies wudu only when the sitting place lifts before waking, as per the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Fainting, Insanity, and Intoxication Nullify Wudu
Three mental states nullifying wudu with definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three mental states that nullify wudu, define each according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish intoxication for wudu nullification from its definition for hadd punishment.

Core Idea
Fainting, insanity, and intoxication all nullify wudu, but the definition of intoxication differs for legal punishment.

This lesson examines three mental states—fainting (ighmā'), insanity (junūn), and intoxication (sukr)—that the Hanafi school classifies as nullifiers of wudu. The passage provides detailed definitions and discusses the nuanced difference between intoxication for wudu and for hadd punishment. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying the ruling in daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three nullifiers and their definitions
Read the first part of the passage that lists fainting, insanity, and intoxication. Note the concise definition for each: fainting removes strength and hides intellect; insanity removes intellect and increases strength; intoxication causes swaying and stammering due to loss of restraining power.
These definitions are operational for wudu nullification.
2
Understand the technical meaning of each term
The commentary expands on each: fainting involves cold phlegm in the brain; insanity is total loss of intellect; intoxication is a lightness that overcomes but does not remove intellect. Distinguish these from imbecility (ʿatah) which does not nullify wudu.
Intoxication leaves the person addressable (ahl al-khiṭāb) according to the verified view.
3
Distinguish intoxication for wudu from intoxication for hadd
The passage mentions a disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the definition of intoxication for the purpose of hadd (punishment). For wudu, the agreed definition is swaying and stammering; for hadd, there are stricter thresholds.
This shows that the same term can have different legal definitions in different contexts.
4
Apply the ruling to modern scenarios
Consider substances like hashish or strong medications: the passage explicitly states that eating hashish causing unsteadiness nullifies wudu. Intoxication from any source, permissible or prohibited, nullifies wudu.
The ruling is based on the condition (loss of control), not the sinfulness of the act.
5
Note the exception: imbecility (ʿatah) does not nullify
The commentary explains that a person with imbecility is likened to a child; their wudu remains valid even if they are not obligated to pray. This is because the intellect is not completely removed, and the condition is permanent, not a temporary state.
This exception highlights that the nullifiers are temporary states that overcome intellect or control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) ينقضه (إغماء) وهو مرض يزيل القوى ويستر العقل (و) ينقضه (جنون) وهو مرض يزيل العقل ويزيد القوى (و) ينقضه (سكر) وهو خفة يظهر أثرها بالتمايل وتلعثم الكلام لزوال القوة الماسكة بظلمة الصدر وعدم انتفاع القلب بالعقل ... قوله: (وهو مرض يزيل القوى) بسبب امتلاء بطون الدماغ من البلغم البارد، وتعطل القوى المدركة والمحركة عن أفعالها . بقاء العقل مغلوباً، أو الغشي بفتح فسكون، أو بكسر الشين تشديد الياء نوع منه، مع وكلاهما ناقض وأما العته فهو غير ناقض لحكمهم على العبادة بالصحة معه، وإن لم يكن مكلفاً بها لإلحاقه بالصبي لا لأن عقله قد زال أفاده السيد. قوله: (وهو خفة الخ ) قال بعضهم : هو سرور يغلب على العقل بمباشرة بعض الأسباب الموجبة له، فيمنع الإنسان عن العمل بموجب عقله من غير أن يزيله، ولذا بقي أهلاً للخطاب وقيل يزيله، وتكليفه زجر له والتحقيق الأول كما في البحر، ولا فرق فيه بين السكر من محرم، أو مباح فهو كالإغماء إلا أنه لا يسقط عنه القضاء، وإن كان أكثر من يوم وليلة لأنه بصنعه بخلاف الإغماء. قوله : يظهر أثرها بالتمايل هذا التعريف باتفاق هنا كما في الحلبي كما أنه باتفاق في الإيمان أن يهذي ويخلط في أكثر كلامه كما صرح به الزيلعي في كتاب الحدود، واختلف في حده في باب الحد. فقال الإمام : هو أن لا يعرف الأرض من ولا الرجال من النساء لأنّ الحدّ عقوبة يحتال لدرئها فيعتبر نهاية السكر، وقالا : هو أن يهذي في كلامه لأنه هو السكر في العرف قال في النهر : وينبغي النقض بأكل الحشيشة إذ دخل في مشيته إختلال
And it is nullified by fainting (ighmā'), which is a disease that removes strength and hides intellect. And it is nullified by insanity (junūn), which is a disease that removes intellect and increases strength. And it is nullified by intoxication (sukr), which is a lightness the effect of which appears by swaying and stammering of speech due to the removal of the restraining power by darkness of the chest and the heart's lack of benefit from intellect. ... His statement: 'It is a disease that removes strength' due to the filling of the brain's ventricles with cold phlegm and the suspension of the perceptive and motive powers from their actions, with the intellect remaining overcome. Or 'al-ghashī' is a type thereof, and both are nullifiers. As for 'al-'atah' (imbecility), it is not a nullifier because they rule the worship valid with it, even if the person is not obligated, due to its resemblance to a child, not because his intellect has left. His statement: 'It is lightness...' Some said: it is a joy that overcomes the intellect by directly engaging in some causes that necessitate it, preventing a person from acting according to his intellect without removing it, therefore he remains eligible for address. It is said it removes it, and his obligation is a deterrent. The verified view is the first, as in al-Bahr. There is no difference between intoxication from a prohibited or permissible substance; it is like fainting except that it does not drop the making up of prayer (qadā') even if more than a day and night, because it is by his own action, unlike fainting. His statement: 'its effect appears by swaying' – this definition is agreed upon here as in al-Halabi, just as it is agreed in faith that he raves and mixes in most of his speech, as stated by al-Zayla'i in the Book of Hudud. There is disagreement on its legal definition in the chapter of hadd. Imam [Abu Hanifa] said: it is that he does not know the ground from the sky, nor men from women, because the hadd is a punishment and one seeks to repel it, so the extreme of intoxication is considered. The two disciples said: it is that he raves in his speech, because that is intoxication in custom. He said in al-Nahr: it is appropriate to nullify wudu by eating hashish if it causes unsteadiness in gait.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage lists three states that nullify wudu: fainting (ighmā'), insanity (junūn), and intoxication (sukr). The commentator defines each: fainting is a disease that removes physical strength and conceals the intellect (though intellect remains overcome); insanity removes intellect entirely and increases strength; intoxication is a lightness causing swaying and stammering, due to the loss of the restraining power in the chest and the heart's inability to benefit from intellect. The commentary expands on the definition of intoxication: some define it as a joy overcoming intellect without removing it, while others say it removes intellect. The verified view is the former, as in al-Bahr. Intoxication from any source (prohibited or permissible) nullifies wudu, but unlike fainting, it does not drop the obligation to make up missed prayers if it lasts more than a day and night, because it is self-inflicted. There is also a discussion of the legal definition of intoxication for the purpose of hadd punishment: Abu Hanifa defines it as not knowing the ground from the sky or men from women, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad define it as raving speech. For wudu nullification, the definition of swaying and stammering is agreed upon. The passage also mentions that eating hashish causing unsteadiness nullifies wudu.

Key Terms
الإغماء (ighmā'): A disease that removes strength and conceals intellect (but intellect remains overcome).
الجنون (junūn): A disease that removes intellect entirely and increases physical strength.
السكر (sukr): A lightness causing swaying and stammering due to loss of restraining power; may be from lawful or unlawful substances.
العته (ʿatah): Imbecility; a permanent condition of mental deficiency that does not nullify wudu because it is likened to childhood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the agreed-upon definition of intoxication for the purpose of nullifying wudu?
A) Not knowing the ground from the sky.
B) Raving and mixing in most of one's speech.
C) Swaying and stammering of speech due to loss of restraining power.
D) Feeling lightheaded and happy.

Key Takeaway
Fainting, insanity, and intoxication all nullify wudu, with intoxication defined by physical swaying and stammering from any source, while imbecility does not nullify wudu.

Loud Laughter in Prayer: Nullification of Wudu and the Hadath vs Punishment Debate
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on qahqaha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qahqaha in prayer, identify its conditions, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between punishment and hadath, including its practical effect on touching the Mushaf.

Core Idea
Loud laughter in prayer nullifies wudu as a deterrent punishment, not as a ritual impurity.

This lesson examines the unique Hanafi ruling that loud laughter (qahqaha) during prayer nullifies both wudu and the prayer itself. The passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi reveals an important intra-Hanafi debate: is qahqaha a true hadath, or is the required wudu merely a punitive measure? Understanding this distinction affects the permissibility of touching the Mushaf and reflects the school's methodological use of qiyas along with the hadith evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Qahqaha is loud laughter audible to one's neighbors during prayer. It nullifies both wudu and the prayer for an adult who is praying a complete prayer, intentionally or forgetfully.
The nullification applies only to the person praying; it does not nullify wudu for non-prayer contexts.
2
Locate the dalil and its wajh al-dalalah
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'من ضحك منكم قهقهة فليعد الوضوء والصلاة' (Whoever laughs loudly, let him repeat wudu and prayer). The imperative 'فليعد' indicates obligation, implying that wudu and prayer are nullified and must be repeated.
The Hanafi school accepts sahih mursal hadith as a proof, and this hadith is narrated as mursal and musnad from several Companions.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute: punishment vs hadath
Scholars differ on whether qahqaha is a real hadath or a punishment. A group including al-Dabusi hold it is a punishment (zajr wa 'uquba) because qiyas shows it is not a filthy excretion but a sound like crying. Others consider it a hadath by implication of the wudu command.
The relied-upon view, supported by al-Bahr, is that it is a punishment. The hadith only commands repetition; it does not declare qahqaha a hadath.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical effect concerns touching the Mushaf. If qahqaha is a hadath, one may not touch the Mushaf after it (like any other hadath). If it is a punishment, one may touch the Mushaf, because the wudu performed is not for removing impurity but for fulfilling the command.
The mufta bihi position is the punishment view, so touching the Mushaf is permitted after wudu due to qahqaha.

And it is nullified by loud laughter (qahqaha) of a praying person (adult), intentionally or forgetfully. And it is [laughter] that is heard by his neighbors... His statement: 'And it is not a hadath in reality, otherwise it would be equal in all states, while it is specific to some. This is in accordance with qiyas because it is not a filthy excretion but a sound like crying and speech... Rather, wudu becomes obligatory from it as a deterrent and punishment. A group including al-Dabusi hold this. And it is said it is a hadath. The benefit of the difference appears in the permissibility of touching the Mushaf after it: whoever makes it a hadath forbids it like other hadaths; whoever makes wudu obligatory as a punishment permits it. He said in al-Bahr: it is preferable to side with qiyas due to the apparent (zahir) of the ahadith in this chapter, for there is nothing in them except the command to repeat wudu and prayer, and it does not necessitate it being a hadath. His statement: 'or forgetfully' – there is one of two narrations, and al-Zayla'i affirmed it because the state of prayer is a reminder, unlike sleep. His statement: 'it is what is heard by his neighbors' even if little, and what is meant is his neighbors in prayer and the like. His statement: 'and it is said it nullifies prayer but not wudu, and it is narrated from ... and from Abu Qasim that it nullifies both; in the latter case he should build upon his prayer. There is that qahqaha is not a heavenly hadath. His statement: 'not a sleeping person' on the correct view, because his action is not described as janabah like a child, but his prayer is invalidated for what we mentioned, and this is the school (madhhab) in al-Bahr. His statement: 'in a complete prayer', even if de jure (hukman) as if he laughed in forgetfulness or if the hadath preceded after wudu before he builds. His statement: 'or a person performing ghusl' on the correct view, and upon it the majority as in al-Dhakha'ir al-Ashrafiyya, and the general mashayikh said: it does not nullify because it is established within the ghusl; if the encompassing (with kasra) does not nullify, then the encompassed (with fatha) does not nullify. His statement: 'because it is a punishment' i.e. not because it is a real hadath, so the statement is not necessitated. He added in the sharh: 'the place of the text' is what is narrated as mursal and musnad: 'Whoever among you laughs loudly, let him repeat wudu and prayer.' Al-Kamal said: the people of hadith acknowledged its authenticity as mursal; as for its musnad narration, it is from several Companions including Ibn Umar, Ma'bad ibn Abi Ma'bad al-Khuza'i, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, Abu Hurairah, Anas, Jabir, and Imran ibn Husayn. The sahih mursal is a proof for us, so it must be acted upon as in al-Burhan and others. His statement: 'without a barrier that prevents the heat of the body' – it is true that there is no barrier at all, or that there is a thin barrier that does not prevent heat. And just as his wudu is nullified, her wudu is nullified as in al-Qunyah. Muhammad said: wudu is not nullified.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage deals with the nullification of wudu by loud laughter (qahqaha) during prayer. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute whether qahqaha is a true hadath or a punishment (zajr). The base text states that qahqaha nullifies wudu for an adult praying a complete prayer, intentionally or forgetfully. The commentary explains: If it were a real hadath, it would nullify wudu in all states, but it is specific to prayer. According to qiyas, it is not a filthy excretion but a sound like crying or speech. The obligation to repeat wudu is a deterrent punishment (zajr wa 'uquba). A group of scholars, including al-Dabusi, hold this view. Others say it is a hadath. The practical difference is touching the Mushaf: if a hadath, one may not touch the Qur'an; if a punishment, one may. Al-Bahr prefers the punishment view due to the ahadith that only command repetition of wudu and prayer without declaring it a hadath. The hadith proof is: 'Whoever laughs loudly, let him repeat wudu and prayer.' This is narrated as mursal and musnad from several Companions, and the sahih mursal is a proof in the Hanafi school. The commentary also discusses conditions: the prayer must be a complete prayer with ruku' and sujud; the person must be awake; the laughter must be audible to neighbors (in prayer); it does not nullify wudu if the person is sleeping or if it occurs in a funeral prayer or prostration of recitation. There is also a discussion about the effect of laughter on ghusl: if one laughs during ghusl, wudu is not nullified according to many mashayikh, because ghusl encompasses wudu. The passage also mentions that if the laughter occurs after the last sitting but before salam, the prayer is valid because all pillars are complete and the missing salam is wajib but does not invalidate. Finally, touching without a barrier that prevents heat of the body is considered direct contact that nullifies wudu (in the context of direct contact between a man and a woman).

Key Terms
القهقهة (qahqaha): Loud laughter with a sound that can be heard by neighbors in prayer; differs from ordinary laughter (dahk) which only invalidates prayer if intentional, and from smiling (tabassum) which does nothing.
الحدث (al-hadath): Ritual impurity that nullifies wudu; acts such as urination, defecation, etc. The dispute is whether qahqaha is a type of hadath.
الزجر والعقوبة (al-zajr wa al-'uquba): Deterrent punishment; the rationale for requiring wudu for qahqaha, according to many Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, why does qahqaha require repeating wudu?
A) Because it is a form of hadath (ritual impurity) like urine.
B) Because it is a deterrent punishment (zajr) prescribed by hadith.
C) Because it invalidates the prayer, and prayer requires wudu.
D) Because it causes physical uncleanliness.

Key Takeaway
Loud laughter (qahqaha) during a complete prayer nullifies both wudu and prayer, but the wudu is wajib as a punishment, not as a hadath, which allows touching the Mushaf after it.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
Ruling on Qahqaha (Loud Laughter) Nullifying Wudu and Prayer
Critical refinement on qahqaha nullifying wudu and prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qahqaha in prayer, explain its proof from the hadith, and distinguish it from other forms of laughter (dahk and tabassum).

Core Idea
Loud laughter (qahqaha) in a complete prayer nullifies both wudu and prayer as a disciplinary measure, not as a genuine hadath.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on qahqaha (loud laughter) during prayer. We will examine the source text from Hashiya al-Tahtawi, which defines qahqaha, distinguishes it from simpler laughter (dahk) and smiling (tabassum), and presents the proof from the hadith. You will also learn the key intra-madhhab dispute: is the nullification a punishment (uquba) or an actual hadath? Understanding this distinction affects post-prayer rulings like touching the Mushaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Qahqaha in a complete prayer (with ruku and sujud) nullifies both wudu and the prayer. Note that the ruling applies only to loud laughter audible to neighbors, and only in a complete prayer; funeral prayer and prostration of recitation are excluded.
The text explicitly defines qahqaha as 'audible to one's neighbors in prayer'.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith: 'Whoever among you laughs loudly, let him repeat wudu and prayer' (من ضحك منكم قهقهة فليعد الوضوء والصلاة). The hadith is transmitted as mursal and musnad, and the Hanafi school accepts sound mursal reports as evidence.
Al-Tahtawi cites al-Kamal's confirmation that hadith scholars authenticated it as mursal.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'falyu'id' (let him repeat) indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul. Therefore, both wudu and prayer must be repeated when qahqaha occurs in a complete prayer.
The imperative is from al-amr, which yields wujub unless there is a contrary indicator.
4
Understand the rationale: uquba vs hadath
The text states that wudu is required as a deterrent punishment (uquba), not because qahqaha is a genuine hadath (ritual impurity). Some Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Dabusi) hold the punishment view; others consider it a hadath. The practical difference: if hadath, one cannot touch the Mushaf after; if punishment, it is permissible.
The author, following al-Bahr, prefers the punishment view as more consistent with the apparent meaning of the hadith.
5
Distinguish from other forms of laughter
Mere smiling (tabassum) with no sound nullifies nothing. Laughter (dahk) heard only by the person himself nullifies only the prayer, not wudu. Only qahqaha nullifies both.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school; other schools may not differentiate.

Because it is audible to his neighbors. Laughter that he hears himself but not his neighbors invalidates only the prayer. Smiling does not invalidate anything, and it is that which has no sound even if teeth appear. The qahqaha of a child does not invalidate his wudu because he is not subject to deterrence; it is said it does invalidate. (Awake, not asleep is the sounder opinion.) We excluded by 'complete' the funeral prayer and the prostration of recitation because the text's context does not apply; thus it does not nullify in them even though they become invalid. Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from Al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah, discussing the ruling of qahqaha (loud laughter) in prayer. The main matn states that qahqaha in a complete prayer (with ruku and sujud) nullifies both wudu and the prayer itself, while mere smiling does not. The author explains that the laughter must be audible to neighbors (i.e., those nearby in prayer) to be considered qahqaha. Lesser laughter heard only by the person himself nullifies only the prayer, not wudu. The ruling applies to conscious adults; a laughing child's laughter does not break wudu because the child is not subject to deterrent punishment, though it does break the child's prayer. The reasoning given for this ruling is that it is a punishment (uquba), not a genuine hadath (ritual impurity). This distinction has practical consequences: if it is considered a hadath, one may not touch the Quran afterward; if merely a punishment, one may touch it after renewing wudu. The author then cites the hadith: 'Whoever among you laughs loudly, let him repeat both wudu and prayer,' which is considered sound as a mursal report and accepted in the Hanafi school. The commentary also discusses nuances such as laughter in forgetfulness, the case of one who laughs after the final tashahhud, and the difference between qahqaha and other forms of laughter.

Key Terms
القهقهة (al-qahqaha): Loud, audible laughter that can be heard by neighbors in prayer.
الضحك (al-dahk): Laughter that is heard by the person himself but not by neighbors; only nullifies prayer, not wudu.
التبسم (al-tabassum): Smiling without sound; does not nullify anything.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on wudu for a person who laughs loudly (qahqaha) during a complete prayer?
A) Wudu and prayer remain valid.
B) Only wudu is nullified.
C) Both wudu and prayer are nullified.
D) Only prayer is nullified, not wudu.

Key Takeaway
Loud laughter (qahqaha) in a complete prayer nullifies both wudu and the prayer, based on the hadith and Hanafi usul, as a disciplinary punishment.

Direct Genital Contact Nullifies Wudu: The Istihsan vs. Qiyas Debate
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on mubashara fahisha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi mufta bihi ruling on mubashara fahisha, distinguish between the proofs of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the maxim 'al-ghalib ka al-muhaqqaq' to a similar case.

Core Idea
Direct genital contact without barrier nullifies wudu — istihsan treats the likely as certain.

Wudu is nullified by a number of causes, among them direct contact between the genitals of a man and a woman without a barrier that prevents heat transfer. But what if no emission occurs? The Hanafi school has an important internal difference: Abu Hanifa, relying on istihsan (juristic preference), rules that such contact always nullifies wudu, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, restrict nullification to cases where madhy (pre-ejaculate) is actually emitted. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind both positions and identifies the relied-upon view for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Locate in the passage the act described: mubashara fahisha — direct skin-to-skin contact between the genitals without a barrier that prevents body heat. Note that the text explicitly includes contact between two men or two women as well.
The barrier must prevent thermal sensation; a thin barrier that does not block heat is still considered direct contact.
2
State the Hanafi Mufta Bihi Position
From the passage and the commentary, determine which position is the mufta bihi (operative for fatwa). Al-Tahtawi cites al-Bahr preferring Abu Hanifa's view, and the text presents istihsan as the basis.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's: wudu is nullified even without confirmed emission of madhy.
3
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
The passage presents two views: Abu Hanifa's istihsan and the Sahibayn's qiyas. Identify the ruling each would give in a case where contact occurs without any emission.
Abu Hanifa nullifies; the Sahibayn do not nullify unless madhy appears.
4
Examine the Proofs
The Sahibayn's argument is qiyas: wudu is only nullified by a known hadath; madhy is a hadath, but without its appearance, contact alone is not a hadath. Abu Hanifa's istihsan overrides this analogy: such contact rarely lacks madhy, so the majority case (ghalib) is treated as certain (ka al-muhaqqaq).
This exemplifies the Hanafi usul principle that istihsan can override qiyas when necessity and common practice demand.
5
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence (thamarah) is clear: if a husband touches his wife's vagina directly and no madhy is felt, according to Abu Hanifa his wudu is nullified; according to the Sahibayn it remains valid. For fatwa, the mufti follows the first.
This ikhtilaf also affects the permissibility of touching the Mushaf after such contact — a point mentioned in the passage.

و) ينقضه مباشرة فاحشة وهي مس (فرج) أو دبر بذكر منتصب بلا حائل) يمنع حرارة الجسد وكذا مباشرة الرجلين والمرأتين ناقضة. التي هي هو سلمة . منه شداد، وإنما وجب الوضوء منها زجراً وعقوبة وعليه جماعة منهم الدبوسي، وقيل، بل حدث، وتظهر فائدة الخلاف في جواز مس المصحف بعدها، فمن جعلها حدثاً منع كسائر الأحداث، ومن أوجب الوضوء عقوبة جوز قال في البحر : وينبغي ترجيح موافقة القياس لظاهر الأخبار الأصل في هذا الباب إذ ليس فيها إلا الأمر بإعادة الوضوء والصلاة ولا يلزم . كونها من الأحداث اهـ. قوله: (بلا حائل يمنع حرارة الجسد صادق بأن لا يكون حائل أصلاً، وبأن يكون حائل رقيق لا يمنع الحرارة، وكما ينتقض وضوءه ينتقض وضوءها كما في القنية، وقال محمد: لا ينتقض الوضوء إلا بخروج مذي، وهو القياس وجه الإستحسان أن المباشرة الفاحشة لا تخلو عن خروج مذي غالباً، والغالب كالمتحقق وفي مجمع الأنهر قوله : أقيس، وقولهما: أحوط.
And direct touching of the private parts nullifies it (wudu), which is touching the vagina or anus with an erect penis without a barrier that prevents body heat, and similarly direct touching between two men or two women is nullifying. Those are... It is... Salamah... From him... Shaddad... And wudu is only obligated from it as a deterrent and punishment, and a group including al-Dabusi hold this. It is said rather it is an event (hadath). The benefit of the difference appears in the permissibility of touching the Mushaf after it. Whoever considers it an event prevents it like other events, and whoever obligates wudu as punishment permits it. He said in al-Bahr: Preference should be given to analogical reasoning in agreement with the apparent ahadith in this chapter, for they only contain the command to repeat wudu and prayer, and it does not necessitate that it be from the events. He said: (Without a barrier that prevents body heat) applies to there being no barrier at all or a thin barrier that does not prevent heat. And just as his wudu is nullified, her wudu is nullified as in al-Qunyah. Muhammad said: Wudu is not nullified except with the emission of madhy, and this is the analogy. The reason for istihsan is that direct touching is rarely devoid of the emission of madhy, and the majority case is treated as certain. In Majma' al-Anhur: He said 'I analogize' and they said 'It is more precautionary.'
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of mubashara fahisha (direct genital contact) as a nullifier of wudu. The matn states that if a man's erect penis touches a vagina or anus without a barrier that prevents heat transfer, both his wudu and the woman's wudu are nullified. Similarly, direct contact between two men's genitals or two women's genitals also nullifies wudu. The commentary explains that the barrier must be one that prevents thermal sensation; a thin barrier that does not prevent heat is still considered direct contact. The author then presents the well-known ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Abu Hanifa (based on istihsan) holds that such direct contact nullifies wudu even without emission, because it typically leads to madhy (pre-ejaculate) which is itself a nullifier, and the majority (ghalib) is treated as certain. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (based on qiyas) argue that it does not nullify wudu unless madhy actually appears, because the ruling should be based on actual occurrence, not presumption. The text then notes that Abu Hanifa's view is based on istihsan (juristic preference) while the Sahibayn's view is based on strict analogy (qiyas). The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa, i.e., it nullifies wudu.

Key Terms
المباشرة الفاحشة (al-mubashara al-fahisha): Direct skin-to-skin contact between genitals without a barrier that prevents heat transfer.
المذي (al-madhy): Pre-ejaculate fluid that is a known nullifier of wudu.

A) Wudu is not nullified unless madhy is emitted.
B) Wudu is nullified regardless of emission.
C) Wudu is only nullified if the contact is with desire.
D) Wudu is not nullified at all.

Key Takeaway
Mubashara fahisha nullifies wudu by Hanafi mufta bihi, relying on istihsan that treats the likely occurrence of madhy as certain.

Taharah — Purification

Ten Things That Do Not Nullify Wudu: Hanafi Rulings
Lists and explains four of ten non-nullifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which substances and actions do not nullify wudu according to the Hanafi school and explain the reasoning and internal disagreements.

Core Idea
Only specific impurities nullify wudu; blood that doesn't flow, falling flesh, worms, and touching do not.

We often assume any body substance breaks wudu, but the Hanafi school has a detailed list. This lesson parsers four of the ten items that explicitly do NOT nullify wudu, with their proofs and internal disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four items
List the four items from the passage: blood that does not flow, flesh falling without bleeding, worms from wound/ear/nose, and touching genitals.
The full list of ten is in the title but only these are detailed.
2
Determine the ruling for each
For each item, state that it does not nullify wudu (لا تنقض الوضوء).
3
Understand the reasoning
For each, identify why: blood is not impure if not flowing; flesh is pure; worm is not impure and moisture minimal; touching is not inherently contaminating.
The reasoning uses principles of purity (tahara) and the lack of flowing impurity.
4
Note intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on blood
Abu Yusuf holds blood pure whether solid or liquid; Muhammad (in non-principle narration) considers it impure. The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf for solids and Muhammad for liquids.
This affects whether water remains pure if blood falls into it.
5
Recognize inter-madhhab ikhtilaf on touching
Hanafi school relies on hadith of Talq that touching does not nullify; Shafi'i uses hadith of Busra. Hanafi prefers Talq due to stronger narrators, but recommends wudu to avoid disagreement.
This demonstrates how the school handles conflicting evidence.

قوله : (لأنه لا ينجس جامداً ولا مائعاً ينجس بتشديد الجيم من التنجيس أي لا أصابه جامداً كان أو مائعاً عند أبي يوسف وهو الصحيح، فلو أخذ بقطن وألقى في الماء القليل لا يفسده، وعن محمد في غير رواية الأصول أنه نجس. قال الحدادي : والفتوى على قول الثاني فيما إذا أصاب الجامدات وعلى قول الثالث فيما إذا أصاب المائعات أفاده السيد قوله: (فلا يكون (ناقضاً لا يحسن ترتيبه على ما قبله بل يترتب ما قبله عليه لأنه إذا لم يكن ناقضاً فلا يكون نجساً قوله : (الطهارته) أي اللحم في حق نفسه أما في حق غيره فنجس لأن المنفصل من الحي ميتة قوله : ( كالعرق المدني نسبة إلى المدينة الشريفة لكثرته بها، وهي بثرة تظهر في سطح الجلد تنفجر عن عرق يخرج كالدودة شيئاً فشيئاً، وسببه فضول غليظة قاله السيد قوله : ( ولقلة الرطوبة التي معها لكنها تنجس ما وقعت فيه من المائعات. قوله: (مطلقاً) ولو من غير الماس، ولو كان الممسوس مشتهى، وسواء كان المس بباطن الكف، أو بغيره بشهوة أولا وفي السيد ويستحب غسل يده إن كان مستنجياً بغير الماء، وحديث بسرة ضعفه جماعه، وهو من مس ذكره فليتوضأ قال في الفتح والحق أن كلا من الحديثين لا ينزل عن درجة الحسن لكن يترجح حديث طلق، وهو الذي ذكره المصنف بأن أحاديث الرجال أقوى لأنهم أحفظ للعلم، وأضبط ، ولذا جعلت شهادة إمرأتين رجل واحد، وقال ابن أمير حاج : يمكن حمل حديث بسرة على غسل اليدين، وقد تقدم أنه يستحب الوضوء للخروج من خلاف العلماء، فإن العبادة المتفق عليها خير من العبادة المختلف فيها . قوله :
Ten things do not nullify wudu: among them, the appearance of blood that has not flowed from its place, because it is neither solid that renders impure nor liquid according to the correct view, so it is not a nullifier. And among them, the falling off of flesh without the flow of blood, because of its purity, and separation of the pure does not necessitate purification, like the urban boil (al-araq al-madani) which is called 'rishta' in Persian as in al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya. And among them, the emergence of a worm from a wound, ear, or nose because of its being not impure and the littleness of moisture that accompanies it, contrary to the one emerging from the anus. And among them, touching the penis, anus, or vulva absolutely; this is the school of great Companions like Umar, Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Zaid ibn Thabit, and the leaders of the Tabi'un. Except with the emission of madhy, and this is the analogy. The ground of istihsan is that direct touching is rarely devoid of the emission of madhy, and the majority is like the certain. In Majma' al-Anhur: He said 'I analogize' and they said 'It is more precautionary.' Ten things do not nullify wudu. نجس (impure). What... He said: (Because it does not render [a thing] impure when solid or liquid, with tashdid on the jim from tanjis, i.e., it does not affect it whether it is solid or liquid according to Abu Yusuf, and this is the correct view. So if one takes it with cotton and throws it into little water, it does not spoil it. From Muhammad, in non-principle narration, it is impure. Al-Haddadi said: The fatwa is on the second saying regarding solids, and on the third saying regarding liquids, as conveyed by al-Sayyid. He said: (So it is not a nullifier) its order is not proper upon what precedes it; rather what precedes it orders upon it because if it is not a nullifier then it is not impure. He said: (Its purity) meaning the flesh itself is pure, but regarding others it is impure because the part separated from a living being is dead. He said: (Like the urban boil) relative to the noble city (Medina) due to its abundance there, and it is a pimple that appears on the skin surface bursting forth from a vein that comes out like a worm gradually, caused by coarse residues, as said by al-Sayyid. He said: (And due to the littleness of moisture with them) but it renders impure the liquids it falls into. He said: (Absolutely) even if not from the one touching, even if the touched is desired, and whether the touching is with the inside of the palm or with other parts with or without desire. In al-Sayyid: It is recommended to wash his hand if he has cleaned himself with other than water. The hadith of Busra was weakened by a group: 'Whoever touches his penis, let him perform wudu.' He said in al-Fath: The truth is that neither of the two ahadith is below the level of hasan, but the hadith of Talq is preponderated, which the author mentioned, that the ahadith of men are stronger because they are more retentive of knowledge and more precise. Hence the testimony of two women is made equal to one man. Ibn Amir Haj said: The hadith of Busra can be interpreted as washing the hands. It has preceded that wudu is recommended to exit from the disagreement of scholars, for the agreed-upon act of worship is better than the disputed one. He said:
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage enumerates ten things that do not nullify wudu according to the Hanafi school, though only four are detailed in the preserved text. The first is blood that does not flow from its wound; it remains at the site without spreading and is considered pure. The second is a piece of flesh that falls off without bleeding, as the flesh itself is pure, and separation of a pure object does not necessitate wudu. The third is a worm emerging from a wound, ear, or nose, because it is not inherently impure and its moisture is minimal, unlike a worm from the anus. The fourth is touching one's own or another's penis, anus, or vulva, which does not nullify wudu according to many Companions. The commentary then digresses into the well-known ikhtilaf between the hadith of Busra (that touching the penis nullifies wudu) and the hadith of Talq (that it does not). The Hanafi school relies on the hadith of Talq, considering it stronger because male narrators are more reliable. However, it is recommended to perform wudu after touching to avoid the disagreement of other schools. The commentary also clarifies that the blood that does not flow is not impure and does not nullify wudu, citing Abu Yusuf's view that it is pure whether solid or liquid, though Muhammad has a different opinion. The flesh separated from a living body is considered pure in itself but impure concerning others (like a dead body). The urban boil (araq madani) is a pimple that exudes a worm-like liquid; it does not nullify wudu. Overall, these rulings show that only specific impurities and emissions nullify wudu, not every substance coming from the body.

Key Terms
الدم الذي لم يسل (al-dam alladhi lam yasil): Blood that appears but does not flow from its wound; considered pure and not a nullifier according to Abu Yusuf.
سقوط لحم (suqut lahm): A piece of flesh that falls off the body without bleeding; considered pure.
مس الذكر (mass al-dhakar): Touching the penis; by itself does not nullify wudu in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following does NOT nullify wudu?
A) Blood that flows from a cut.
B) Touching one's own penis.
C) Passing wind.
D) Vomiting a mouthful.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, only flowing blood, not merely appearing blood, nullifies wudu, and touching the private parts does not nullify by itself.

Taharah — Purification
beginner

Five Acts That Do Not Nullify Wudu (Part 1: Blood, Flesh, Worms, Touch)
List of first five things that do not break wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the first five items that do not break wudu according to the Hanafi school and explain the primary evidence for each.

Core Idea
Blood that does not flow, falling flesh, worms, touching private parts, and touching women do not nullify wudu in the Hanafi school.

The Hanafi school has a precise list of nullifiers of wudu. Many actions mistakenly thought to break wudu are actually not nullifiers. This lesson covers the first five: blood that does not flow from its site, a piece of flesh falling without blood, a worm exiting a wound/ear/nose, touching one's own private parts, and touching a non-mahram woman. Each ruling is supported by strong textual evidence from the Quran and Sunnah, and the intra-Hanafi discussions are noted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each of the five items, determine that the ruling is 'does not break wudu'. Note any conditions (e.g., blood that does not flow).
2
Locate the evidence cited
Find the Quranic verse or hadith used as proof. For touching private parts, the hadith 'It is but a piece of you.' For touching women, the hadith of the Prophet kissing his wives and the interpretation of 'lams' as intercourse.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
On blood on solids vs. liquids: the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf for solids and Muhammad for liquids. On touching private parts: the relied-upon view is no wudu, but some recommend wudu to avoid disagreement.
The ikhtilaf reflects differences in transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah vs. Nawadir) and methodological preference.
4
Understand the rationale
Blood that does not flow is not considered impure; flesh is pure in itself; worms from non-exit areas are not impure; touching private parts is not a hadath; touching women is not a nullifier because the Quranic term means intercourse.
5
Apply to a real-life scenario
Imagine a person accidentally touches his own private part while washing. According to this lesson, his wudu remains valid. Contrast with the Shafi'i ruling.

كتاب الطهارة

عشرة أشياء لا تنقض الوضوء منها ظهور دم لم يسل عن محله) لأنه لا جامداً ينجس ولا مائعاً على الصحيح فلا يكون ناقضاً (و) منها (سقوط لحم من غير سيلان (دم) لطهارته وانفصال الطاهر لا يوجب الطهارة كالعرق المدني الذي يقال له رشته) بالفارسية كما في الفتاوى البزازية (و) منها (خروج دودة من جرح وأذن وأنف لعدم نجاستها ولقلة الرطوبة التي معها بخلاف الخارجة من الدبر (و) منها (مس ذكر) ودبر وفرج مطلقاً، وهو مذهب كبار الصحابة كعمر وعلي وابن مسعود وابن عباس وزيد بن ثابت وصدور التابعين كالحسن وسعيد والثوري رضي الله تعالى عنهم لأن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جاءه رجل كأنه بدوي فقال يا رسول الله ما تقول في رجل مس ذكره في الصلاة فقال: (هل هو إلا بضعة منك أو مضغة منك قال الترمذي : وهذا الحديث أحسن شيء في هذا الباب وأصح (و) منها (مس (امرأة غير محرم لما في السنن الأربعة عن عائشة رضي الله عنها كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يقبل بعض أزواجه، ثم يصلي ولا يتوضأ، واللمس في الآية المراد به الجماع كقوله تعالى: (وإن طلقتموهن من قبل أن تمسوهن) (و) منها (قيء لا يملأ الفم) لأن من أعلى المعدة (و) منها (قيء بلغم ولو) كان كثيراً) لعدم تخلل النجاسة فيه وهو طاهر (و) منها تمايل نائم احتمل زوال مقعدته لما في سنن أبي داود كان أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ينتظرون العشاء حتى تخفق رؤوسهم ثم يصلون ولا يتوضؤن (و) منها (نوم متمكن من الأرض (ولو) كان (مستنداً إلى شيء) كحائط وسارية، ووسادة بحيث (لو أزيل) المستند إليه (سقط) الشخص فلا ينتقض وضوءه على الظاهر) من مذهب أبي حنيفة (فيهما) أي في المسئلتين هذه والتي قبلها لاستقراره بالأرض فيأمن خروج ناقض منه رواه أبو يوسف عن أبي حنيفة وهو الصحيح وبه أخذ عامة المشايخ وقال القدوري : ينتقض وهو مروي عن الطحاوي (و) منها (نوم مصل ولو) نام (راكعاً أو ساجداً) إذا كان على جهة أي صفة (السنة) في ظاهر المذهب بأن أبدى ضبعيه وجافي بطنه عن فخذيه لقوله : لا يجب الوضوء على من نام جالساً أو قائماً أو ساجداً حتى يضع جنبه فإذا اضطجع استرخت مفاصله وإذا نام كذلك خارج الصلاة لا ينتقض به وضوءه في
Book of Purification. Ten things do not break wudu: (1) appearance of blood that does not flow from its place, because it does not make solid nor liquid impure according to the correct view, so it is not a nullifier. (2) falling of flesh without blood flowing, due to its purity, and the separation of pure does not necessitate purity, like a 'madani sweat' which is called 'rishta' in Persian, as in al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya. (3) emergence of a worm from a wound, ear, or nose, due to its lack of impurity and the scant moisture accompanying it, contrary to one emerging from the anus. (4) touching the penis, anus, or vagina absolutely, which is the madhhab of senior Companions like Umar, Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Zayd ibn Thabit, and the leading Tabi'un like al-Hasan, Sa'id, and al-Thawri, may Allah be pleased with them, because a man who seemed like a Bedouin came to the Messenger of Allah and said: 'O Messenger of Allah, what do you say about a man who touches his penis in prayer?' He said: 'Is it but a piece of you?' or 'a lump of you?' al-Tirmidhi said: This hadith is the best and most authentic in this chapter. (5) touching a woman who is not mahram, due to what is in the four Sunan from A'isha: 'The Prophet used to kiss some of his wives then pray without performing wudu,' and 'al-lams' in the verse means intercourse, as in His saying: 'And if you divorce them before you have touched them' (2:237). (6) vomit that does not fill the mouth, because it comes from the upper stomach. (7) vomiting phlegm even if it is a lot, because impurity does not permeate it and it is pure. (8) swaying of a sleeping person whose seated position is likely to have shifted, due to what is in Sunan Abi Dawud: 'The Companions of the Messenger of Allah used to wait for 'Isha' until their heads nodded [from drowsiness], then they would pray without performing wudu.' (9) sleep while firmly seated on the ground even if leaning on something like a wall, pillar, or cushion such that if the support were removed the person would fall, then his wudu is not broken according to the apparent madhhab of Abu Hanifa on both issues (this one and the previous), due to his stability on the ground thus being safe from the emission of a nullifier; it was narrated by Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, and it is the correct view adopted by most mashayikh; al-Quduri said it breaks wudu, which is narrated from al-Tahawi. (10) sleep of a praying person even if he sleeps while bowing or prostrating, as long as it is in the manner of the Sunnah in the apparent madhhab, by exposing his armpits and separating his belly from his thighs, because of his saying: 'Wudu is not obligatory on one who sleeps sitting, standing, or prostrating until he lies down on his side, for when he lies down his joints relax.' And if he sleeps similarly outside of prayer, his wudu is not broken by it in...
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi 'ala Maraqi al-Falah presents a list of ten things that, according to the Hanafi school, do NOT nullify wudu. The commentator, al-Tahtawi, is glossing the original matn of Maraqi al-Falah. He begins by stating the list, then provides reasoning and evidences for each item. The ten items are: (1) blood that appears but does not flow from its site—Hanafis consider that if blood does not flow, it does not impurity nor nullify wudu, with a note that Abu Yusuf's view (solid or liquid matters) is the correct one, while Muhammad's view in non-relied transmissions holds it impure. (2) falling of a piece of flesh without blood flowing—since the flesh itself is pure (though separate flesh from a living being is considered dead and impure when touching other things? Actually the text says the flesh is pure in itself, but when it separates it becomes impure? The commentary clarifies that the flesh is pure in itself, but regarding others it is impure because separate from a living being is dead carcass. (3) a worm coming out of a wound, ear, or nose—because it is not impure and has little moisture, so it doesn't nullify wudu, unlike a worm from the anus. (4) touching one's own penis, anus, or vagina—the Hanafi position is that this does not break wudu, based on hadith that the penis is just a piece of you, and supported by many Companions. There is an intra-madhhab discussion: the hadith of Basra is weak; the preferable hadith is that of Talq. The commentator notes that it is recommended to perform wudu to leave the disagreement of scholars. (5) touching a non-mahram woman—does not break wudu, based on the Prophet kissing his wives and praying without wudu. The term 'lams' in the Quran is interpreted as intercourse, not mere touching. (6) vomiting less than a mouthful—because it comes from the upper stomach and does not nullify. (7) vomiting phlegm, even if a lot—because phlegm is pure and impurity does not penetrate it. (8) swaying of a sleeping person whose seat is likely to have moved—based on the practice of the Companions who slept while waiting for 'Isha and then prayed without wudu. (9) sleeping while firmly seated on the ground even if leaning on something—because the seat is stable, so one is safe from emitting a nullifier. This is the relied-upon view of Abu Hanifa as reported by Abu Yusuf, though al-Quduri and al-Tahawi hold it breaks wudu. (10) sleep of a praying person while in ruku' or sujud if done in the Sunnah manner—because the joints are not fully relaxed, unlike lying down. The passage ends abruptly but likely concludes that such sleep does not break wudu.

Key Terms
ناقض (naqid): That which nullifies wudu; in this passage, it refers to acts that invalidate the state of purity.
مذي (madhi): Pre-ejaculatory fluid; its emission breaks wudu, but simply touching does not.
اللمس (al-lams): In the Quranic verse, it is interpreted as sexual intercourse, not mere physical touch, so it does not break wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the evidence for touching a non-mahram woman not breaking wudu?
A) The Prophet said 'It is but a piece of you.'
B) The Prophet used to kiss his wives and then pray without wudu.
C) The Quran says 'Do not approach women during menstruation.'
D) A hadith about the Prophet wiping over socks.

Key Takeaway
Five common acts—non-flowing blood, falling flesh, worms from non-exit areas, touching private parts, and touching non-mahram women—do not break wudu in the Hanafi school.

Five Acts That Do Not Nullify Wudu (Part 2: Vomit, Phlegm, Sleep States)
List of last five things that do not break wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the last five items that do not break wudu according to the Hanafi school and explain the conditions for sleep-related non-nullifiers.

Core Idea
Vomit less than a mouthful, phlegm, swaying sleep, seated sleep, and sleep in prayer do not break wudu in the Hanafi school.

Continuing the list of ten things that do not nullify wudu, this lesson covers items six to ten: vomit that does not fill the mouth, vomiting phlegm, swaying sleep of one whose seat is stable, sleep while firmly seated even if leaning, and sleep of a praying person in ruku or sujud. The Hanafi school provides specific conditions for sleep not to break wudu, focusing on stability of the seat and absence of full relaxation of joints.

Guided Path
1
Identify each act and its ruling
For vomit: less than mouthful does not break wudu; phlegm does not break even if a lot. For sleep: three categories – swaying sleep (seat likely shifted), seated sleep (stable), and sleep in prayer (maintaining Sunnah posture). All do not break wudu under conditions.
2
Note the conditions and exceptions
Vomit from upper stomach or less than mouthful is pure. Phlegm is pure. Sleep must be with stable seat or in prayer posture; lying down and fully relaxed breaks wudu.
3
Locate the evidence
For sleep, the hadith of the Companions waiting for 'Isha with nodding heads and praying without wudu. For seated sleep, the narration of Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on sleep
Abu Hanifa's view (via Abu Yusuf) is that seated sleep does not break wudu if stable. Al-Quduri and al-Tahawi hold it breaks wudu. The relied view is that of Abu Hanifa.
5
Apply to a real-life scenario
A person dozes off while sitting in a chair, but his seat is stable. According to the Hanafi school, his wudu remains valid. If he lies down fully and sleeps, his wudu breaks.

Book of Purification. Ten things do not break wudu: (1) appearance of blood that does not flow from its place, because it does not make solid nor liquid impure according to the correct view, so it is not a nullifier. (2) falling of flesh without blood flowing, due to its purity, and the separation of pure does not necessitate purity, like a 'madani sweat' which is called 'rishta' in Persian, as in al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya. He said: 'Is it but a piece of you?' or 'a lump of you?' al-Tirmidhi said: This hadith is the best and most authentic in this chapter. The Hadith Proof that Touching the Private Part Does Not Nullify Wudu
Presents the hadith proof for non-nullification of wudu from touching the private part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hadith proof cited by early scholars that touching the private part does not nullify wudu, and explain the rhetorical implication of the Prophet's response.

Core Idea
The private part is like any other body part—this hadith is used to argue it does not nullify wudu.

This passage presents a key hadith used by early scholars (al-Hasan al-Basri, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, and Sufyan al-Thawri) to support their view that touching the private part does not break wudu. The Prophet's rhetorical question equates it to any other body part. Al-Tirmidhi considers this hadith the best and most authentic on the topic. Understanding this proof is essential to recognizing the basis of their position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponents
Note that the scholars are al-Hasan al-Basri, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, and Sufyan al-Thawri – all early authorities who held that touching the private part does not nullify wudu.
2
Locate the hadith
The hadith recounts a Bedouin asking the Prophet about a man touching his private part during prayer. The Prophet replied: 'Is it anything but a piece of you or a morsel of you?'
This is a hadith ahad, reported in Sunan al-Tirmidhi.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's rhetorical question implies that the private part is like any other body part, so touching it does not create any special impurity requiring renewed wudu.
The comparison to a 'piece' or 'morsel' suggests it is not a source of hadath.
4
Assess the source strength
Al-Tirmidhi states that this hadith is 'the best thing on this topic and the most authentic,' indicating its reliability among the relevant narrations.
Despite its strength, later Hanafi scholars override it with istihsan based on other evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said: 'Is it anything but a piece of you or a morsel of you?' al-Tirmidhi said: This hadith is the best thing on this topic and the most authentic.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the proof used by certain early scholars (al-Hasan al-Basri, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, and Sufyan al-Thawri) who held that touching one's own private part does not nullify wudu. They rely on the hadith where the Prophet called it 'a piece of you,' implying it has no special ruling. The Hanafi position, however, is that touching the private part with desire does nullify wudu, but this text only cites the opposition's proof.

Key Terms
مس الذكر (mass al-dhakar): The act of touching the male genitalia with the hand
بضعة (biḍ'ah): A piece or portion of flesh
مضغة (muzghah): A morsel or small piece of flesh

Key Takeaway

Touching a Woman and Wudu: The Hanafi Proof
Provides hadith and Quranic evidence against wudu nullification by touch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on touching a non-mahram woman regarding wudu and cite the primary proof from hadith and Quranic interpretation.

Core Idea
Touching a woman does not nullify wudu — 'lams' in the Quran means intercourse, not skin contact.

The nullifiers of wudu vary among schools. This lesson examines the Hanafi position that mere skin contact with a non-mahram woman does not invalidate wudu. We will analyze the proof cited from the Prophet's practice (hadith) and the Quranic reinterpretation of 'lams'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The widespread view that touching a woman nullifies wudu is challenged. The Hanafi school argues this is incorrect.
The nullifier is actually sexual contact, not mere touch.
2
Examine the hadith proof
A hadith from Aisha (ra) reports that the Prophet kissed his wives and then prayed without performing wudu. This shows that kissing, which involves touching, does not necessitate wudu renewal.
The hadith is found in the four Sunan (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Nasa'i, Ibn Majah).
3
Examine the Quranic interpretation
The verse of wudu (al-Ma'idah: 6) mentions 'lams' of women as a nullifier. The Hanafi school interprets 'lams' as sexual intercourse, citing a similar usage in al-Baqarah: 237 ('before you have touched them').
Thus, 'lams' is intercourse, not skin contact.
4
Note the Hanafi position
Therefore, touching a non-mahram woman without desire does not break wudu. The nullifier is limited to actual sexual contact or touching with desire (which causes minor hadath).
Touching with desire is makruh tahrimi and nullifies wudu according to some Hanafi authorities.
5
Compare with other schools
The Shafi'i school holds that any skin contact with a non-mahram woman nullifies wudu, based on a different reading of the verse and hadith. The Hanafi school responds by interpreting the evidence more narrowly.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (difference of opinion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و منها (مس (امرأة غير محرم لما في السنن الأربعة عن عائشة رضي الله عنها كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يقبل بعض أزواجه، ثم يصلي ولا يتوضأ، واللمس في الآية المراد به الجماع كقوله تعالى: (وإن طلقتموهن من قبل أن تمسوهن)
And among them (nullifiers) is touching a non-mahram woman, because of what is in the four Sunan from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): The Prophet would kiss some of his wives, then pray without performing wudu. And 'touching' in the verse means intercourse, as Allah says: 'And if you divorce them before you have touched them...'
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage argues that touching a non-mahram woman does not nullify wudu, citing the hadith that the Prophet kissed his wives and then prayed without renewing wudu. It further explains that the word 'lams' in the Quranic verse (al-Ma'idah: 6) refers to intercourse, not mere touching. This is the Hanafi position: mere skin contact without desire does not break wudu.

Key Terms
لمس (lams): Touching; in the context of the verse, it is interpreted as sexual intercourse
غير محرم (ghayr mahram): A woman who is not a mahram (permanently forbidden in marriage)

Key Takeaway
Touching a non-mahram woman does not nullify wudu; the nullifier is sexual contact, as proven by hadith and Quranic reinterpretation of 'lams'.

Vomiting and Phlegm: Exceptions to Wudu Nullification
States exceptions to vomiting as a nullifier of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which vomiting nullifies wudu in the Hanafi school and identify whether a given substance (stomach contents vs. phlegm) nullifies wudu.

Core Idea
Vomiting only nullifies wudu if it is from the stomach and fills the mouth; phlegm never nullifies.

You may have heard that vomiting breaks wudu, but the Hanafi school attaches two important conditions to that rule. In this lesson, we examine a short passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi that clarifies exactly when vomiting does and does not nullify wudu. Understanding these exceptions will save you from unnecessary worry and strengthen your grasp of Hanafi taharah fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act under discussion
The passage lists vomiting (qay') as a potential nullifier of wudu. Note that it is only a nullifier if specific conditions are met.
Other nullifiers include urination, defecation, etc.
2
Extract the first exception: less than mouthful
Vomiting that does not fill the mouth is excluded from nullification. The reason given is that such vomiting originates from the upper stomach and is not considered substantial.
The key measurement is 'filling the mouth' – a mouthful.
3
Extract the second exception: phlegm
Vomiting of phlegm (balgham) does not nullify wudu, even if the amount is large. The reason is that phlegm is pure and does not contain impurities from the stomach.
Phlegm originates from the chest, not the stomach.
4
Apply the conditions to a scenario
If a person vomits a mouthful of food from the stomach, wudu is nullified. If they bring up a small amount of stomach fluid or only phlegm, wudu remains valid.
The ruling is based on the substance and quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و منها (قيء لا يملأ الفم) لأن من أعلى المعدة (و) منها (قيء بلغم ولو) كان كثيراً) لعدم تخلل النجاسة فيه وهو طاهر
And among them (nullifiers): vomiting that does not fill the mouth, because it is from the upper stomach. And among them: vomiting of phlegm, even if it is much, because impurity does not permeate it, and it is pure.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage lists two conditions related to vomiting as a nullifier of wudu. First, vomiting only nullifies wudu if it fills the mouth; if it is less, it does not. Second, vomiting phlegm does not nullify wudu regardless of quantity, because phlegm originates from the chest and is not considered impure; it does not come from the stomach where najasah resides. The Hanafi school holds that the matter from the stomach is impure, but phlegm is not, so it does not break wudu.

Key Terms
قيء (qay'): Vomiting, the expulsion of stomach contents
بلغم (balgham): Phlegm, matter from the chest

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Only if it is phlegm
D) Only if it is from the stomach

Key Takeaway
Vomiting nullifies wudu only if it is from the stomach and fills the mouth; phlegm is not a nullifier.
