Sleep and Wudu: Scenarios and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains when sleep nullifies wudu, with different scenarios and a dispute within the Hanafi school

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between types of sleep that nullify wudu according to the Hanafi school and state the relied-upon position in the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Not all sleep nullifies wudu; only deep sleep relaxing the joints does.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi presents three scenarios of sleep and their effect on wudu. It also exposes a key dispute within the Hanafi school regarding sleep while seated. Understanding this helps clarify when renewal of wudu is truly necessary in daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sleep scenarios
Read the Arabic passage and translation to list the three cases: (1) swaying while seated on ground, (2) firmly seated even if leaning, (3) sleeping in sunnah posture during prayer.
2
Understand each ruling
For each scenario, determine whether wudu is nullified or not according to the 'apparent madhhab' (zahir al-riwayah). Note that cases (1) and (2) both hold wudu valid under Abu Hanifa's view.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
In case (2), al-Quduri and al-Tahawi hold that wudu is nullified. Identify the two opposing positions and their reasoning.
4
Identify the dalil
The hadith of the companions nodding during prayer and then praying without wudu is cited. Understand how this supports Abu Hanifa's position.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states 'al-sahih' and 'bi akh' (most mashayikh took it). Conclude that Abu Hanifa's non-nullification view is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و منها تمايل نائم احتمل زوال مقعدته لما في سنن أبي داود كان أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ينتظرون العشاء حتى تخفق رؤوسهم ثم يصلون ولا يتوضؤن (و) منها (نوم متمكن من الأرض (ولو) كان (مستنداً إلى شيء) كحائط وسارية، ووسادة بحيث (لو أزيل) المستند إليه (سقط) الشخص فلا ينتقض وضوءه على الظاهر) من مذهب أبي حنيفة (فيهما) أي في المسئلتين هذه والتي قبلها لاستقراره بالأرض فيأمن خروج ناقض منه رواه أبو يوسف عن أبي حنيفة وهو الصحيح وبه أخذ عامة المشايخ وقال القدوري : ينتقض وهو مروي عن الطحاو ي (و) منها (نوم مصل ولو) نام (راكعاً أو ساجداً) إذا كان على جهة أي صفة (السنة) في ظاهر المذهب بأن أبدى ضبعيه وجافي بطنه عن فخذيه لقوله : لا يجب الوضوء على من نام جالساً أو قائماً أو ساجداً حتى يضع جنبه فإذا اضطجع استرخت ،مفاصله وإذا نام كذلك خارج الصلاة لا ينتقض به وضوءه في الصحيح وإن لم يكن على صفة السجود والركوع المسنون انتقض وضوءه
And among them: swaying of a sleeper that may cause his seat to move, because of what is in Sunan Abi Dawud: The companions of the Messenger of Allah would wait for the night prayer while their heads were nodding, then they would pray without performing wudu. And among them: sleep while firmly seated on the ground, even if leaning on something like a wall, a pillar, or a cushion, such that if the support is removed he would fall, then his wudu is not nullified in the apparent madhhab of Abu Hanifa in both issues (this and the previous one) because he is settled on the ground and thus safe from the emission of a nullifier. Abu Yusuf narrated this from Abu Hanifa, and it is sound, and most mashayikh took it. al-Quduri said it is nullified, and it is narrated from al-Tahawi. And among them: sleep of a praying person, even if sleeping while bowing or prostrating, if it is in the manner of the sunnah in the apparent madhhab, by exposing his sides and keeping his belly away from his thighs, because it is said: Wudu is not obligatory on one who sleeps sitting, standing, or prostrating until he places his side (on the ground). When he lies down, his joints relax. And if he sleeps like that outside of prayer, his wudu is not nullified in the sound view. But if he is not in the sunnah posture of prostration and bowing, his wudu is nullified.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage discusses sleep as a nullifier of wudu, presenting three scenarios: (1) a light sleep where the person sways but remains seated, (2) a firm sleep while seated on the ground even if leaning, and (3) sleep during prayer in the sunnah posture. The Hanafi school generally holds that light sleep does not nullify wudu, but deep sleep where one lies down does. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that sleep while firmly seated does not nullify, even if leaning, because the person is secure. al-Quduri and al-Tahawi held that it does nullify. For a praying person, if he sleeps in the recommended posture (sunnah) of prostration or bowing (with proper form), his wudu remains valid; otherwise it is nullified.

Key Terms
نوم (nawm): Sleep; the state of unconscious rest
تمايل (tamayyul): Swaying or nodding due to drowsiness
مستنداً (mustanidan): Leaning on something for support

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) remains valid because he is settled
C) nullified only if he snores
D) must be renewed out of precaution

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, sleep that relaxes the joints nullifies wudu; light sleep while seated does not.

Introduction to Ghusl: Definitions and Requirements
Defines ghusl linguistically and legally, lists causes and components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ghusl linguistically and legally, list its causes (asbab), and identify its pillar (rukn) and conditions (shurut).

Core Idea
Ghusl is wajib for janabah, hayd, and nifas.

This lesson introduces the chapter on ghusl (ritual bath). The passage defines the term, explains its linguistic and legal usage, and outlines the components necessary to understand its application. We will break down the definition, causes, conditions, and pillar of ghusl as presented by the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic and legal definitions
Identify that 'ghusl' (with dammah) is the technical term used by Hanafi jurists, while 'ghasl' (with fatha) is more common in language. The legal meaning is the complete washing of the body from major impurity.
The passage notes that the dammah pronunciation is the technical choice, though fatha is more eloquent.
2
Identify the causes (asbab) of obligatory ghusl
From the text: ghusl becomes wajib due to janabah (semen emission with desire), hayd (menstruation), and nifas (postpartum bleeding). These are the three causes mentioned.
The passage also implies that the cause of janabah is the state, not the substance.
3
List conditions and pillar (rukn)
The text states that conditions of obligation and validity are the same as those for wudu (e.g., Islam, sanity, puberty, availability of water). The pillar (rukn) is the intention (niyyah) and washing the entire body.
The passage says 'conditions of obligation and conditions of validity that have preceded in wudu' – we apply the same.
4
Summarize the components of ghusl
The passage enumerates: linguistic and legal definition, cause, condition, ruling, pillar, sunnah, etiquette, and description. Master these to fully understand ghusl.
This step ties together the entire introduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter: What necessitates al-ightisal (ritual bath). That is ghusl, and it (with dammah) is a verbal noun from al-ightisal, and it is the complete washing of the body, and also a name for the water used for bathing. The dammah (vowel) is what the jurists (or most of them) have terminologically agreed upon, even though the fatha is more eloquent and more common in the language. They have specifically used it for washing the body from janabah, hayd, and nifas. Janabah is a state that occurs through the emission of semen with desire. One says 'januba' if he fulfills his desire with a woman. Know that one needs to explain ghusl linguistically and legally, its cause, condition, ruling, pillar, sunnah, etiquette, and description. Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on ghusl (ritual bath). It begins by defining the term linguistically: ghusl (with dammah) is the act of washing, and it can also refer to the water used. The Hanafi jurists have adopted the pronunciation 'ghusl' with dammah as their technical term, though 'ghasl' with fatha is more common in Arabic. In Islamic law, ghusl is the complete washing of the entire body required in cases of major impurity (janabah from sexual activity, hayd/menstruation, and nifas/postpartum bleeding). The passage then lists the components necessary to fully understand ghusl: its linguistic and legal definition, cause (semen emission with desire), conditions, ruling, pillar (rukn), sunnah elements, etiquette, and description. The ruling of ghusl is that it is wajib (obligatory) when one intends to perform acts forbidden during janabah, such as prayer. The conditions for obligation and validity are similar to those of wudu.

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl): The ritual washing of the entire body for purification from major impurity
الجنابة (al-janabah): The state of major ritual impurity caused by sexual discharge
ركن (rukn): An essential part of an act; without which the act is invalid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ghusl is wajib upon janabah, hayd, or nifas; its rukn is intention and complete washing of the body.

Definition of Ghusl and the Condition of Desire for Seminal Emission
Commentary on ghusl obligations, defining the act and detailing the first cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ghusl, identify the condition of desire for obligation, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on the timing of desire.

Core Idea
Janabah requires ghusl only if semen exits with desire; the dispute: when must desire be present?

This passage defines ghusl and its first cause—the exit of semen. It then clarifies a key intra-Hanafi dispute: whether desire must persist until the semen exits or only at the moment of separation. Understanding this nuance affects when ghusl becomes obligatory in ambiguous cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Ghusl is fard (obligatory) upon certain causes. The first cause is exit of semen.
2
Define janabah and the role of desire
Janabah is a state that occurs by the emission of semen with desire (shahwa).
3
State the condition for obligation
For ghusl to be wajib, the semen must exit with desire. If no desire, no ghusl.
4
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that desire is required only at the moment of separation from the spine. Abu Yusuf holds that desire must persist until the semen exits the body.
5
Determine the muftā bihi
The text declares that the fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf’s view.

Chapter of what necessitates ghusl: ghusl is, with dammah, a noun from ightisal, meaning complete washing of the body, also a name for the water used for ghusl. The dammah is the term used by most jurists, though the fatha is more eloquent and common in language. They restricted it to washing the body from janabah, menstruation, and post-natal bleeding. Janabah is a quality that occurs by the emission of semen with desire. Know that ghusl requires explanation in language and Shari'ah, its cause, condition, ruling, pillar, sunnah, manners, and description. Its interpretation and cause are known as intending what is forbidden due to janabah or its obligation. It has conditions of obligation and validity. Its pillar is ... Ghusl is obligatory by one of seven things: first, emission of semen, which is thick white water that breaks the penis upon exit, resembling the smell of pollen. Female semen is thin and yellow. It becomes apparent on the body. When it separates from its place (the spine) with desire, and if from other than intercourse (like wet dream, even the first time for puberty in the sound view, or thought, looking, or playing with the penis—permissible for an unmarried man to calm desire feared, not to incite it). The condition of desire suffices instead of gushing, so if no desire, no ghusl. The condition is presence at separation from spine, not persistence until exit, contrary to Abu Yusuf. The text discusses rulings on female discharge, masturbation, and the prohibition of seeking desire. The fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hāshiya) on Marāqī al-Falāh, defining ghusl and its first cause. The author begins with the linguistic and technical definitions of ghusl, noting that jurists use the form with dammah (غُسْل) to mean the act of washing, while the fatha (غَسْل) is the verbal noun. Ghusl is restricted to washing the entire body from states of major ritual impurity: janābah (sexual defilement), hayḍ (menstruation), and nifās (postpartum bleeding). Janābah is defined as a state resulting from the emission of semen with desire. The author then lists the aspects needed to explain ghusl: linguistic and shar‘ī meaning, cause, condition, ruling, pillar, sunnah, manners, and description. The cause is the intention to permit what was forbidden due to janābah. The conditions of obligation and validity are the same as for wuḍū’. The matn states that ghusl is fard by one of seven things, the first being the exit of semen (manī). The commentary details semen: male semen is thick, white, and smells like pollen when fresh; female semen is thin and yellow. The key conditions are: (1) the semen must appear on the body (not just internal), (2) it separates from its source (the spine) with desire (shahwa), and (3) the exit occurs without intercourse (e.g., wet dream, masturbation). If desire is absent, no ghusl is required. There is an intra-Hanafī dispute: Imām Abū Ḥanīfa holds that the condition of desire is required only at the moment of separation from the spine; Imām Abū Yūsuf requires desire to persist until the semen exits the body. The text declares that the fatwā is according to Abū Yūsuf. The commentary also addresses masturbation: it is permissible when there is fear of falling into sin (e.g., zinā or liwāṭ), but it is not rewarded—it is a 'head-to-head' escape (no sin, no reward). Seeking desire solely for pleasure is prohibited based on narrations like 'the hand-marrier is cursed' and stories of punished nations. Practical rulings: if a woman performs ghusl from janābah and later discharges yellow fluid without desire, she must repeat ghusl; otherwise not. The passage ends with a note that the fatwā is according to Abū Yūsuf's view.

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl (with dammah)): The act of washing the entire body with pure water, specifically for janabah, hayd, and nifas.
الجنابة (al-janabah): A state of major ritual impurity caused by the emission of semen with desire, whether from intercourse or otherwise.
الشهوة (al-shahwa): Sexual desire; a condition for the obligation of ghusl from seminal emission without intercourse.
المني (al-maniyy): Semen; male semen is thick and white, female is thin and yellow. Its exit with desire necessitates ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view regarding the condition of desire (shahwa) for ghusl from seminal emission?
A) Desire must be present only at the moment of separation from the spine.
B) Desire must persist until the semen exits the body.
C) Desire is not required if the semen exits without intercourse.
D) Desire is required only for women.

Key Takeaway
Ghusl from semen is fard only when desire accompanies its separation; the relied-upon view requires desire to last until exit.

Obligation of Ghusl Due to Semen Emission and Glans Concealment
First two causes of ghusl with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which ghusl becomes obligatory due to semen emission and glans concealment, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the timing of desire.

Core Idea
Ghusl is obligatory when semen exits with desire or when the glans is concealed; desire at separation from the spine suffices.

In this passage, the author identifies the first two of seven causes that necessitate ghusl: the emergence of semen and the concealment of the glans. He details the condition of desire for semen, citing a hadith as proof, and presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether desire must persist until exit. Understanding these conditions and the operative fatwa is essential for correctly applying the rules of janaba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two causes
List the first two of seven causes that make ghusl obligatory: (1) emergence of semen to the exterior with desire, and (2) concealment of the glans (hashafa) during intercourse.
The text uses 'yufarrad' to indicate the ruling level (fard).
2
Analyze the condition of desire for semen
Recognize that desire must be present at the moment semen separates from the spine (sulb). Desire is sufficient; gushing (dafq) is not required. The text clarifies scenarios where desire is absent, such as carrying a heavy load, and where it is present, such as wet dream or self-stimulation to calm desire.
The hadith about a woman's wet dream is used to prove that appearance of fluid triggers ghusl, not the force of ejaculation.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that desire at the moment of separation from the spine is sufficient. Abu Yusuf requires desire to continue until the semen exits the body. The text illustrates a case: holding the penis until desire subsides, then releasing semen.
The fatwa is given according to Abu Yusuf when one fears social harm, but for future acts, Abu Hanifa's view is followed.
4
Understand the second cause: glans concealment
Concealment of the entire glans (hashafa) during intercourse triggers janaba. The hashafa is defined as the head of the penis. Even partial concealment? The text implies full concealment is the condition, but later hashiya may elaborate.
This applies to the circumcised and uncircumcised; the measure is the glans of an uncircumcised male.
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
For past prayers where one followed Abu Yusuf's view due to necessity, they are valid. For future, the preferred position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
The text also mentions that a celibate man may use his hand to calm desire without sin or reward.

Ghusl becomes obligatory by one [of seven things] that occur to a person: first, the emergence of semen—a thick white fluid upon whose exit the penis softens, resembling the smell of pollen, while the woman's semen is thin and yellow—to the exterior of the body, because as long as it has not appeared it has no ruling—if it separates from its source (the spine) with desire, and its exit is without intercourse, such as a wet dream, and even if it is the first time for puberty in the soundest opinion, and [also] thinking, looking, and playing with his penis, and he may do that if he is unmarried, and thereby he escapes head to head to calm a desire he fears from, not to bring it. The condition of desire suffices over gushing due to its concomitance with it. So if desire is not found, there is no ghusl, as when he carries a heavy load or is struck on his spine and semen descends without desire. The condition is its existence at its separation from the spine, not its continuance until it emerges to the exterior, contrary to Abu Yusuf, and this applies equally to woman and man due to his statement, peace be upon him, when asked 'Is there ghusl upon a woman when she has a wet dream?', he said: 'Yes, when she sees water.' And the consequence of the difference appears in the case where one holds his penis until his desire subsides, then releases water: ghusl is obligatory upon him according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, not according to Abu Yusuf, and the fatwa is given according to Abu Yusuf if he fears [censure] because he is afraid of causing harm? And if he does not apprehend being caught? he should pretend to be in prayer without takbir and recitation. And the consequence appears when he performs ghusl in his place and prays, then the remainder of semen emerges: ghusl is upon him according to the two, not according to him, and his prayer is valid by agreement. And if it emerges after he urinates and his penis relaxes, or he sleeps, or walks many steps, ghusl is not obligatory by agreement. And making semen and what is conjoined with it a cause for ghusl is metaphorical for ease in teaching, because they are conditions. And among them is the concealment of the glans—which is the head of the male penis, covered?

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the first two causes that necessitate ghusl: the emergence of semen and the concealment of the glans. The author states that ghusl becomes obligatory when semen exits the body with desire, even without gushing, as desire is sufficient. The hadith from the Prophet (SAW) is cited: 'When a woman has a wet dream and sees water, she must perform ghusl.' This establishes the proof. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require desire only at the moment the semen separates from the spine (sulb), while Abu Yusuf requires desire to continue until the semen exits the body. The fatwa is given according to Abu Yusuf, especially when the person fears social harm (like being seen as a wrongdoer). The passage also discusses the ruling for a celibate man who uses his hand to calm desire: it is permissible without reward or sin. The second cause is the concealment of the glans (hashafa) during intercourse. The hashya then comments on details such as the definition of hashafa and the conditions for ghusl when only part of the glans is concealed.

Key Terms
المني (al-maniy): The thick, white fluid of a man and thin, yellow fluid of a woman whose exit with desire causes janaba.
الشهوة (al-shahwa): Sexual desire that accompanies the separation of semen from its source (the spine) and is required for ghusl obligation.
الحشفة (al-hashafa): The glans of the penis, the head that must be concealed (even partially) for ghusl to become obligatory in intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, according to all Hanafi imams.
B) No, according to Abu Yusuf, and fatwa is given on his view in this case of fear.
C) Yes, only according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
D) No, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
Ghusl is obligatory when semen exits with desire (even if desire subsides before exit) or when the glans is concealed during intercourse; the operative fatwa depends on the circumstances of fear or necessity.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Ghusl from Suppressed Emission
Explains the ikhtilaf and fatwa for a man suppressing emission then releasing water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf regarding ghusl after suppressed emission, and apply the fatwa rule for a guest who is embarrassed or fears suspicion.

Core Idea
When desire precedes, the emitted water is semen requiring ghusl; Abu Yusuf exempts the embarrassed guest.

This lesson delves into a subtle but important intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the obligation of ghusl (full body wash) when a man suppresses his sexual urge and then releases water. The disagreement between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani hinges on whether the fluid is considered semen (mani) requiring ghusl. The practical implications are especially relevant for a guest in an awkward situation, where following Abu Yusuf's opinion allows ease and avoids hardship.

Guided Path
1
State the Case
Identify the scenario: a man holds his penis until his desire subsides, then releases water (urine or fluid).
The key variable is the presence of prior desire.
2
Identify the Positions
Recall the rulings: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say ghusl is obligatory; Abu Yusuf says it is not.
This disagreement is within the Hanafi school itself.
3
Analyze the Evidence
Examine the proof: the hadith 'Yes, when she sees water' (for a woman's wet dream) is applied by analogy to men. The dispute focuses on whether the emission at release is considered water (semen) given the prior desire.
The two imams emphasize the prior desire; Abu Yusuf emphasizes the moment of exit.
4
Apply the Fatwa Hierarchy
Understand that for a guest who is embarrassed or fears suspicion, fatwa is given on Abu Yusuf's view to grant leniency. Some scholars differentiate between past and future prayers.
This shows the school's practical flexibility without abandoning the stronger opinion.

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi is a critical refinement of Maraqi al-Falah, focusing on the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ghusl (ritual bath) for a man who suppresses his sexual urge and then releases water (urine or semen). The passage begins by citing the hadith that a woman must perform ghusl after a wet dream when she sees water (semen). Then it presents the fruit of the dispute (thamarah) between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The case: a man holds his penis until his desire calms, then releases water. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, ghusl is obligatory because the water is considered semen (mani) expelled with a preceding desire. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the emission at the moment of release is not accompanied by desire, so it is not semen requiring ghusl. The fatwa is given according to Abu Yusuf's opinion specifically for a guest who is embarrassed (istaḥyā) or fears suspicion (khashiya ar-raybah) from the household, allowing him to avoid ghusl and perform wudu instead. If he does not immediately suppress and instead pretends to be praying to avoid suspicion, the practical consequence is that if he later bathes and prays, and then remaining semen exits, ghusl is obligatory according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two) but not according to Abu Hanifa; yet his prayer remains valid by consensus. Additional rulings clarify that ghusl is not obligatory if semen exits after urination, relaxation of the penis, sleeping, or many steps, by agreement. The passage also discusses the metaphorical use of 'cause' for semen, emphasizing that the actual factors are conditions for obligation. The commentary then explains specific phrases from the matn, such as the definition of 'suppressing' (tadāruk) and the details of pretending to pray, including what acts are permissible (like takbir) and what are not (like recitation). The overall aim is to delineate the precise conditions under which ghusl becomes obligatory and the scope of the ikhtilaf within the school.

Key Terms
مني (mani): Semen, the fluid emitted from the male reproductive organ due to sexual desire.
غسل (ghusl): Full body ritual washing required after major impurity (janabah).
حركة الشهوة (shahwah): Sexual desire or arousal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the intra-Hanafi dispute about a man who holds his penis until desire subsides and then releases water, on whose opinion is fatwa given for a guest who is embarrassed or fears suspicion?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the obligation of ghusl for suppressed emission depends on desire and the imam's view; fatwa grants ease to the embarrassed guest by following Abu Yusuf.

Condition of Ghusl: Concealment of the Glans (Hashafah)
Defines the condition for ghusl and its exceptions, including the hadith of circumcision

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for ghusl relating to the hashafah and identify which cases require ghusl and which do not.

Core Idea
Ghusl is wajib when the entire glans is concealed in a living human's private part, even without ejaculation.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi defines one of the key conditions for obligatory ghusl: the concealment (tawārī) of the entire glans (ḥashafah) in a living human's private part. It clarifies exceptions for animals, dead bodies, amputated organs, and artificial ones, and also discusses a controversial case where the penis is wrapped in cloth. Understanding this definition is essential for determining when ghusl becomes mandatory.

Guided Path
1
Define the key term
Identify the definition of hashafah: the head of the penis above the circumcision line, which must be completely concealed for ghusl to be obligatory.
The passage distinguishes the full glans from partial concealment.
2
Identify the condition of ghusl
State that ghusl becomes wajib when the entire hashafah is concealed in one of the two private parts (front or back) of a living human.
This is based on the hadith: 'When the two circumcised parts meet and the glans disappears, ghusl becomes obligatory.'
3
List the exceptions
Note which cases do not obligate ghusl: when the organ is from an animal, a dead body, amputated, artificial (leather, finger), or if the partner is not a living human who can cohabit.
In such cases, only washing the penis may be required, not full ghusl.
4
Analyze the cloth barrier case
Examine the ruling when the penis is wrapped in a cloth: the most correct opinion is that ghusl is obligatory only if both heat of the vulva and pleasure are felt; otherwise not, but the precaution is to perform ghusl in all cases.
This illustrates that direct skin contact is not always required; the condition is concealment plus sensation.
5
Understand the proof and cross-madhhab issue
Learn that the hadith is the primary proof, and note that the Shafi'i school considers circumcision obligatory for both genders, while Hanafi considers it sunnah.
This does not affect the ghusl condition directly but shows a related difference.

اللذة اقتصر في السراج على وجود الحرارة، وفي التنوير وشرحه على وجود وجمع بينهما المصنف لأن الظاهر تلازمهما غالباً قوله : (إذا التقى الختانان (الخ) ذكرهما بناء على عادة العرب من ختن نسائهم، وهو من الرجال دون حزة الحشفة، ومن المرأة موضع قطع جلدة كعرف الديك فوق مدخل الذكر، وهو مخرج الولد والمني والحيض، وتحت مخرج البول، ويقال له أيضاً خفاض قال في السراج وهو سنة عندنا للرجال، والنساء، وقال الشافعي: واجب عليهما وفي الفتح يجبر عليه أن تركه إلا إذا خاف الهلاك، وإن تركته هي لا اهـ. وذكر الاتقاني عن الخصاف بإسناده إلى شداد بن أوس مرفوعاً الختان للرجال سنة، وللنساء مكرمة.
His statement: 'and among them is concealment of the glans' i.e., covering the entire glans. If less than that is concealed, or less than its measure from the amputated one, ghusl is not obligatory as in Al-Qahastani. His statement: 'it is the head of the penis...' this definition the author had the context in mind, otherwise the glans as in Al-Qamus and similar in Al-Durr is what is above the circumcision, and in Al-Qahastani it is the head of the penis up to the cut, and that is not included in its concept. End. His statement: He guarded against by it the penis of animals, the dead, the amputated, the one made from leather, the finger, the penis of a boy who desires, and the adult woman makes obligatory upon her the concealment of the glans of the adolescent for ghusl. And (and) concealment of its measure (i.e., of the glans) from its amputated (organ) if the concealment is in one of the two paths of a living human, then ghusl is obligatory upon both if they are obligated, and the adolescent is commanded with it for moral training. And it is obligatory by sexual intercourse with a young girl who does not desire and whom he has not deflowered, because she became one who is cohabited with in the correct view. And if he wrapped his penis in a cloth and inserted and did not ejaculate, the most correct is that if he finds heat of the vulva and pleasure, ghusl is obligatory; otherwise not. And the most precautionary is the obligation of ghusl in both cases because of his saying: 'When the two circumcised parts meet and the glans disappears, ghusl becomes obligatory whether he ejaculated or not.' And the author did not express using 'meeting of the two circumcised parts' to include insertion in the anus, and because what is established in the vulva is their alignment, not their meeting. His statement: He guarded against by it the penis of animals as a guard for human, and his statement: 'the dead' excludes by the penis of the living, and his statement: 'the amputated' excludes by the desiring as also excludes his statement: 'and the penis of a boy' and his statement: 'the one made from leather' and 'the finger' excludes by his saying 'head of a penis' so it is from confused scattering. His statement: 'obligates upon her' – prayer with an event, i.e., not upon him, but it prevents him from prayer until he bathes as it prevents from ? until he performs wudu as in Al-Khulasa from Al-Asl. And in Al-Khaniyya, a ten-year-old is commanded with it habitually and morally as he is commanded with purification and prayer. His statement: 'in one of the two paths of a living human who cohabits like him' excludes non-human, the dead, and the young girl who does not cohabit, so ghusl is not obligatory by intercourse in these things, and wudu is not broken, and he is only required to wash his penis as in Al-Qahastani from the nullifiers. And in Al-Durr: moisture of the vulva is pure according to Abu Hanifa, end. i.e., so washing the penis is not required either. His statement: 'and is obligatory by intercourse with a young girl who does not desire and whom he has not deflowered' this is he said: the correct is, and some said absolutely, beneficial from Al-Sayyid. His statement: 'obligatory' so the most correct is that if he finds heat of the vulva and pleasure, ghusl is obligatory. And 'pleasure' is in the accusative conjuncted to 'heat'. Some restricted in Al-Siraj to the existence of heat, and in Al-Tanwir and its commentary to the existence of pleasure, and the author combined between them because the apparent is that they generally accompany each other. His statement: 'When the two circumcised parts meet' etc. – he mentioned them based on the custom of the Arabs of circumcising their women, and it is for men the line of the glans, and for women the place of cutting a piece of skin like the cock's comb above the entrance of the penis, and it is the exit of child, semen, and menses, and below the exit of urine, and it is also called 'khifad'. He said in Al-Siraj: It is sunnah for us for men and women, and Al-Shafi'i said: it is obligatory for both. In Al-Fath: he is compelled upon it if he leaves it unless he fears death, but if she leaves it she is not compelled. End. And Al-Itqani mentioned from Al-Khassaf with his chain to Shaddad ibn Aws marfu'an: 'Circumcision is sunnah for men and an honor for women.'
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines one of the conditions for ghusl: the concealment (tawārī) of the entire glans (ḥashafah) in one of the two private parts (front or back) of a living human. If less than the entire glans is concealed, or if the organ is amputated and only the equivalent part is concealed, ghusl is not obligatory. The text then defines the glans: it is the head of the penis of a living, desiring male – not of animals, dead persons, amputated organs, artificial ones (like leather), or fingers. For a boy who has reached the age of desire (but not necessarily puberty), concealment of his glans obligates ghusl for the partner if they are adults, and the boy is commanded to perform ghusl for moral training. The ruling also applies to a small girl who is not sexually desirable and not deflowered, because she is considered one who is cohabited with according to the correct view. The passage then discusses the case of wrapping the penis in a cloth before insertion. The most correct opinion is that ghusl is obligatory only if one feels both the heat of the vulva and pleasure; if only one is present, it is not obligatory, but the precaution is to perform ghusl in all cases. This is based on the hadith: 'When the two circumcised parts meet and the glans disappears, ghusl becomes obligatory whether he ejaculated or not.' The author explains that the condition of 'meeting of the two circumcised parts' is not limited to vaginal intercourse but also includes anal intercourse, since the same principle applies. The text also addresses that if a man has intercourse with a dead female or an animal, ghusl is not obligatory, but he must wash his penis. The vaginal moisture (rutūbah) is considered pure according to Abu Hanifa, so no washing of the penis is needed. The passage concludes with a discussion on circumcision: it is sunnah for both men and women in the Hanafi school, unlike the Shafi'i school which considers it obligatory. A hadith is cited that circumcision is sunnah for men and a noble practice (mukramah) for women.

Key Terms
حشفة (hashafah): The glans penis, the head of the penis above the circumcision line.
التقاء الختانين (iltiqa' al-khitanayn): The meeting of the two circumcised parts (of man and woman) indicating intercourse.
سبيل (sabil): A pathway; here refers to the front or back private parts (urethra and anus).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a man wraps his penis in a cloth and inserts it into his wife's vagina without ejaculating, which of the following is the most correct ruling?
A) Ghusl is always obligatory
B) Ghusl is obligatory only if he feels heat and pleasure
C) Ghusl is not obligatory under any circumstances
D) Ghusl is obligatory only if he ejaculates

Key Takeaway
The concealment of the entire glans in a living human's private part is the primary condition for obligatory ghusl, regardless of ejaculation.

Ghusl Becomes Obligatory Upon Insertion, Not Ejaculation
Explains when ghusl becomes obligatory due to intercourse with exclusions and a barrier case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that triggers ghusl from intercourse and apply the Hanafi ruling to cases involving barriers or non-adult partners.

Core Idea
Ghusl is wajib when the glans disappears, even without ejaculation.

This lesson explores a foundational ruling of taharah: when precisely does ghusl become obligatory after intercourse? The Hanafi school, relying on a clear hadith, holds that full insertion of the glans – regardless of ejaculation – makes ghusl fard. We will examine the text from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya, which refines the ruling by excluding certain cases and addressing an intra-madhhab difference regarding the use of a barrier. Understanding this ruling is essential for every Muslim's daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? What is the hukm level? The passage states that ghusl becomes obligatory (wajib/fard) when the glans is completely concealed in a living human's private part.
The ruling applies to both parties if they are legally responsible (mukallaf).
2
Locate the dalil
What evidence is cited? The hadith: 'When the two circumcised parts meet and the glans disappears, ghusl is obligatory, whether he ejaculated or not.'
This is a sunnah ahad hadith; its transmission is zanni but its meaning is clear.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The explicit statement 'wajaba al-ghusl' indicates obligation, and the phrase 'whether he ejaculated or not' negates the need for ejaculation.
The imperative form of 'wajaba' is interpreted as fard in Hanafi usul.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
What disagreement exists within the school? Regarding the case of wrapping the penis in a cloth: Abu Hanifa holds that if no heat/pleasure is felt, ghusl is not obligatory; the more precautionary view (Sahibayn or later scholars) says ghusl is obligatory in both cases.
The text marks 'al-ashahh' (most correct) as the view differentiating based on heat/pleasure, but the precautionary opinion is also cited.
5
State the mufta bihi
What is the relied-upon position for fatwa? Ghusl is obligatory upon full insertion; in the barrier case, the precautionary view (obligatory in both) is recommended.
The text does not explicitly give a tarjih authority, but the mention of 'al-ashahh' indicates the preferred view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احترز به عن ذكر البهائم والميت والمقطوع والمصنوع من جلد والأصبع وذكر صبي يشتهي والبالغة يوجب عليها تواري حشفة المراهق الغسل (و) تواري (قدرها) أي الحشفة (من مقطوعها) إذا كان التواري في أحد سبيلي آدمي حي) فيلزمهما الغسل لو مكلفين ويؤمر به المراهق تخلقاً ويلزم بوطء صغيرة لا تشتهي ولم يفضها لأنها صارت ممن يجامع في الصحيح ولو لف ذكره بخرقة، وأولجه ولم ينزل فالأصح أنه إن وجد حرارة الفرج، واللذة وجب الغسل وإلا فلا والأحوط وجوب الغسل في الوجهين لقوله : إذا التقى الختانان وغابت الحشفة وجب الغسل أنزل أو لم ينزل
He guarded against mentioning the penis of animals, dead, amputated, manufactured from skin, finger, and the penis of a boy who is desired, and the adult female obligates her the concealment of the glans of an adolescent boy necessitates ghusl (and) the concealment of its size, i.e. the glans, from its amputated part, if the concealment is in one of the two passages of a living human, then ghusl is obligatory on both if they are legally responsible, and the adolescent boy is commanded to do it as a matter of moral training, and it is obligatory by intercourse with a young girl who is not desired and he did not deflower her because she has become one who is cohabited with in the correct view. And if he wrapped his penis in a cloth and inserted it and did not ejaculate, then the most correct is that if he found the heat of the private part and pleasure, ghusl is obligatory, otherwise not. And the more precautionary is the obligation of ghusl in both cases due to his statement: 'When the two circumcised parts meet and the glans disappears, ghusl becomes obligatory, whether he ejaculates or not.'
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah deals with the ruling of ghusl becoming obligatory due to sexual intercourse. The author first clarifies the subject: the head of the penis, by excluding various cases (animals, dead, amputated, artificial, finger, and an undesired boy). For a living human, full concealment of the glans in either front or back passage makes ghusl obligatory on both parties if they are adults. The text then addresses special cases: intercourse with a young girl who is not desired but is capable of intercourse, and wrapping the penis in a cloth. In the latter case, if heat and pleasure are felt, ghusl is obligatory; otherwise, it is not, but the precautionary opinion favors doing ghusl. The proof is the hadith: 'When the two circumcised parts meet and the glans disappears, ghusl becomes obligatory, whether he ejaculates or not.' This hadith establishes that full contact, not ejaculation, triggers the obligation. The commentary also defines al-khitanan for males and females, noting it is a sunnah for both in the Hanafi school, while al-Shafi'i considers it obligatory.

Key Terms
حشفة (hashafa): The glans (head) of the penis; also defined as the part removed in circumcision.
الختانان (al-khitanan): The circumcised parts of the male and female; for males, the glans; for females, the clitoral hood.
تواري الحشفة (tawāri al-ḥashafa): The complete concealment of the glans within the partner's passage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ejaculation of semen
B) Full insertion of the glans into a living human's private part
C) Both partners being adult
D) Presence of pleasure

Key Takeaway
Ghusl becomes fard upon full insertion of the glans, regardless of ejaculation, as established by the hadith.

Ruling on Thin Fluid After Sleep: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ghusl for thin fluid after sleep

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl obligation when finding thin fluid after sleep and distinguish the positions of the Sahibayn and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
When thin fluid appears after sleep without a wet dream, ghusl becomes wajib due to precaution, but Abu Yusuf held it is only madhi.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi clarifies an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the obligation of ghusl for a thin fluid (mā’ raqīq) found after sleep without recollection of a wet dream. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it wajib based on a hadith and the principle of precaution in worship, while Abu Yusuf himself initially held it to be madhi, requiring only wudu. Understanding this ikhtilaf helps us apply the correct ruling in cases of doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the doubt
The act is finding thin fluid after sleep without recollection. The doubt is whether it is semen (mani) or pre-ejaculatory fluid (madhi).
The fluid is described as 'thin' (raqīq), which can be either.
2
State the position of the Sahibayn
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two Sahibayn), ghusl is wajib. Their evidence includes a hadith commanding ghusl and the reasoning that sleep arouses desire and semen may become thin.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
3
State Abu Yusuf's dissenting position
Abu Yusuf held that such fluid is madhi, requiring only wudu. His evidence is qiyas: the fluid is thin like madhi, so the ruling of madhi applies.
Ibn Ayyub and Abu al-Layth followed this view.
4
Explain the Hanafi response
The Sahibayn respond that the hadith is explicit and cannot be overridden by qiyas. Additionally, precaution (ihtiyāt) is obligatory in worship when doubt exists.
The hadith is a primary dalil; qiyas is used only when no text exists.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: according to the mufta bihi, ghusl is wajib in cases of doubt between mani and madhi. According to Abu Yusuf, no ghusl is required.
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view.

تفرقة غير ظاهرة الوجه، فالكل على الإطلاق إذ لا يظهر بينهما إفتراق .اهـ. قوله (دون) تذكر، ومميز) أما إذا تذكر أحدهما حلماً دون الآخر فعلى المتذكر فقط، أو وجدت علامة كونه منه أو منها فعلى صاحبها فقط، ومحله ما لم يكن الفراش نام عليه غيرهما قبلهما، أما إذا كان ذلك، والمني جاف فالظاهر عدم الوجوب على كل منهما كذا في البحر قوله : (بغلظ) متعلق بمميز والأول والثالث والخامس صفة مني الذكر والثاني والرابع والسادس صفة مني الأنثى.
And among them (is finding thin fluid) after awakening from sleep, and he does not recall a wet dream. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf, and by his statement Ibn Ayyub and Abu al-Layth adopted (the view) that it is madhi, and it is the analogically stronger view. And for the two (is) what was narrated that he (the Prophet) was asked about a man who finds wetness and does not recall a wet dream; he said: He should perform ghusl. And because sleep is a rest that arouses desire, and semen may become thin due to a cause, and precaution is necessary in matters of worship. And this is if his penis was not erect before sleep, because erection is a cause for madhi, so it is attributed to it. And if the husband and wife find between them water with no recollection and no distinguishing mark by thickness of leaf, whiteness, yellowness, length and width, then ghusl is obligatory on both in the correct view as a precaution. He said in al-Mi'raj: meaning an honor for men because intercourse with a circumcised woman is more pleasurable... and its time is among the issues in which the Imam refrained out of piety due to lack of text... His statement: (does not necessitate ghusl) meaning nor does it nullify wudu. His statement: (And among them is finding thin fluid after sleep) the summary of the issue of sleep is twelve aspects as in al-Bahr...
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the obligation of ghusl when one finds thin fluid (mā' raqīq) after sleep without recollection of a wet dream. The matn states that according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) ghusl is wajib, contrary to Abu Yusuf's earlier view which considered it madhi. The commentator (Tahtawi) explains that Ibn Ayyub and Abu al-Layth followed Abu Yusuf's view based on qiyas. The proof for the two is a hadith where the Prophet commanded ghusl for such a case, and the reasoning that sleep arouses desire and semen may thin due to a cause, necessitating precaution. However, if the penis was erect before sleep, the fluid is attributed to madhi. The passage then details the twelve possible scenarios of doubt and the rulings for each, including the consensus cases and the point of dispute. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that ghusl is wajib when there is doubt between semen and madhi and no recollection of a wet dream.

Key Terms
ماء رقيق (mā' raqīq): Thin fluid found after sleep, which may be either semen (mani) or madhi. The text discusses the ruling when its nature is unclear.
مذي (madhi): Pre-ejaculatory fluid, thin and sticky, that requires wudu but not ghusl.
احتلام (ihtilām): Wet dream, which confirms that the fluid is semen and necessitates ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), if a person wakes up and finds thin fluid but does not recall a wet dream, what is the ruling?
A) Ghusl is wajib.
B) Ghusl is not required; only wudu.
C) Nothing is required.
D) Only the clothing needs to be washed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the relied-upon position is that finding thin fluid after sleep without recollection of a dream obligates ghusl due to the hadith and precaution, unless one is certain it is madhi.

Remaining Obligatory Causes of Ghusl
Listing remaining obligatory causes of ghusl with brief proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four additional causes that make ghusl obligatory and explain the evidence and conditions for each according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Ghusl is obligatory for menstruation, postpartum bleeding, embracing Islam after janabah, and washing the deceased – each with specific conditions.

The previous lesson covered the primary causes of ghusl: seminal discharge, direct contact of circumcised parts, and dreaming about intercourse. This lesson completes the list with four additional obligatory causes: menstruation, postpartum bleeding, embracing Islam after a state of janabah, and washing the deceased. Each cause is supported by clear evidence from Quran or consensus, and each has its own conditions that must be met for the obligation to take effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause
Name the specific event or state that triggers ghusl: menstruation, postpartum bleeding, embracing Islam after janabah, or the death of a Muslim (requiring washing by the living).
These are in addition to the earlier causes like seminal emission.
2
State the ruling and its evidence
For each cause, determine the ruling (fard ayn or fard kifayah) and the textual or consensus proof. Menstruation and postpartum are based on the Quran ('nor are you allowed to have intercourse with them until they purify themselves' – understood as requiring ghusl after cessation). Embracing Islam after janabah relies on the principle that purification is a condition for prayer. Washing the deceased is a communal obligation from the Prophet's command.
The passage mentions 'by consensus' for postpartum; the Quranic verse is cited via the reference to the wudu verse.
3
Apply the condition
Note the specific condition for each cause: menstruation and postpartum bleeding obligate ghusl only after the bleeding stops (al-intiqa'). For embracing Islam, the state of janabah must have existed before Islam; after Islam, ghusl becomes obligatory because the person is now obligated to pray. For washing the deceased, the Muslim must not be a martyr or subject to a crime that removes the obligation.
The passage emphasizes 'after purification from their impurity by cessation' for hayd and nifas.
4
Distinguish fard ayn vs. fard kifayah
The first three causes (finding semen, menstruation, postpartum, embracing Islam) make ghusl fard ayn – individually obligatory on the person. Washing the deceased is fard kifayah – a communal obligation; if some Muslims perform it, the obligation is lifted from others.
This distinction is important for understanding responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ظنه منياً) منياً بعد إفاقته من سكر و بعد إفاقته من (إغماء) احتياطاً (و) يفترض (بحيض) للنص (ونفاس) بعد الطهر من نجاستهما بالانقطاع إجماعاً (و) يفترض الغسل بالموجبات (لو حصلت الأشياء المذكورة قبل الإسلام في الأصح لبقاء صفة الجنابة ونحوها بعد الإسلام ولا يمكن أداء المشروط من الصلاة ونحوها بزوال الجنابة وما في معناها إلا به فيفترض عليه لكونه مسلماً مكلفاً بالطهارة عند إرادة الصلاة ونحوها بآية الوضوء (ويفترض تغسيل الميت المسلم الذي لا جناية منه مسقطة لغسله ( كفاية) وسنذكر تمامه في محله إن شاء الله تعالى.
His statement: (he thinks it is semen) after recovering from intoxication and after recovering from unconsciousness, as a precaution. And ghusl is obligatory for menstruation due to the text, and for postpartum bleeding after purification from their impurity by cessation, by consensus. And ghusl is obligatory for the obligations if the mentioned things occurred before Islam in the correct view due to the persistence of the state of janabah and the like after Islam, and the performance of the conditional act like prayer and the like cannot be achieved by removal of janabah and what is similar to it except by it (ghusl), so it becomes obligatory upon him as a Muslim who is obligated for purification when intending prayer and the like by the verse of wudu. And it is obligatory to wash the deceased Muslim who does not have (any crime) that drops his washing, as a communal obligation. And we will mention its completion in its place if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage lists additional causes that make ghusl obligatory, continuing from the previous section. They include: finding fluid that one thinks is semen after recovering from intoxication or unconsciousness (as a precaution), menstruation (based on the Quranic text), postpartum bleeding (by consensus after the bleeding stops), embracing Islam, and washing the deceased Muslim (as a fard kifayah). The passage explains that if these causes occurred before embracing Islam, ghusl is still obligatory after Islam because the state of janabah persists and prayer is conditional upon purification. The verse of wudu (5:6) is cited as evidence for the obligation of purification for prayer. This section is a straightforward list for memorization.

Key Terms
حيض (ḥayḍ): Menstrual bleeding; its cessation necessitates ghusl by consensus.
نفاس (nifās): Postpartum bleeding; ghusl is obligatory after it stops, by consensus.
غسل الميت (ghusl al-mayyit): Washing the deceased Muslim body; it is a fard kifayah on the living.
إغماء (ighmāʾ): Unconsciousness; if a person finds suspected semen after regaining consciousness, ghusl is obligatory as a precaution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does ghusl become obligatory due to postpartum bleeding (nifās)?
A) At the start of the bleeding.
B) After the bleeding stops.
C) Only if the woman experiences a wet dream.
D) It is never obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Ghusl becomes obligatory for menstruation and postpartum only after the bleeding stops, for converts if they were in janabah before Islam, and for washing the deceased as a communal duty.

Madhi and Wadi: Fluids That Break Wudu but Not Ghusl
Introduction to a chapter listing fluids that do not require ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define madhi and wadi, distinguish them from semen, and state whether each requires ghusl or wudu according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Madhi and wadi nullify wudu but never require ghusl.

This passage opens a chapter listing ten things that do not necessitate ghusl. It begins with two common fluids: madhi (pre-ejaculatory fluid) and wadi (a thick cloudy fluid). Understanding their definitions and rulings is essential for correctly identifying when ghusl is obligatory versus when only wudu is needed. The text also addresses a subtle objection about wadi and urine, reinforcing the independent nullifying effect of wadi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two fluids
Locate the terms madhi and wadi in the source text. Note their Arabic names and the short descriptions provided.
Madhi is thin white; wadi is thick cloudy.
2
Examine the descriptions
Read the descriptions of each fluid carefully: when they appear, their physical properties, and any associated feelings (or lack thereof).
Note that madhi occurs during arousal without gushing; wadi often follows urine or heavy lifting.
3
Extract the ruling
From the phrase 'لا يغتسل منها' and the surrounding context, determine the legal effect on ghusl and wudu for each fluid.
Both nullify wudu but do not require ghusl.
4
Understand the objection and answer
The commentator raises a potential objection: since wadi often accompanies urine, why would wudu be needed separately? Follow the answer that wadi can appear without urine, so its nullification is independent.
This shows careful juristic reasoning to avoid redundancy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل عشرة أشياء لا يغتسل منها مذي بفتح الميم وسكون الذال المعجمة وكسرها وهو ماء أبيض رقيق يخرج عند شهوة لا بشهوة ولا دفق ولا يعقبه فتور وربما لا يحس به وهو أغلب في النساء من الرجال يحترز به عما لو كان مذياً فإنه لا غسل عليه قاله السيد عن شرح مثلاً مسكين. قوله : (وهو ماء أبيض كدر ثخين يشبه المني في الثخانة ويخالفه في الكدرة، ويخرج قطرة أو قطرتين عقب البول إذا كانت الطبيعة مستمسكة، وعند حمل شيء ثقيل وبعد الاغتسال من الجماع، وينقض الوضوء، فإن قيل : ما فائدة الوضوء من الودي، وقد وجب من البول قبله أجيب بأنه يخرج بدون البول كما ذكرنا فلا يرد وجوب
Chapter: Ten things from which ghusl is not performed: madhi – with fatḥa on the mīm and sukūn on the dhāl (and also kasra) – and it is a thin white fluid that emerges at the time of desire, not with desire (i.e., without intense lust), not with gushing, and is not followed by lethargy, and perhaps one does not feel it. It is more prevalent in women than in men. It is a precaution against what if it were actual madhi, there is no ghusl upon him. The sayyid said from the commentary of Miskin for example. His statement: (and it is a thick white cloudy fluid resembling semen in thickness but differing in cloudiness, and it comes out as a drop or two drops after urination when the nature is constipated, and when carrying something heavy, and after performing ghusl from intercourse; and it nullifies wudu. For if it is said: what is the benefit of wudu from wadi when wudu was already obligated due to urine before it? The answer is that it comes out without urine as we mentioned, so the objection does not arise.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage introduces the fasl on ten things that do not require ghusl. It begins with the definition of madhi (pre-ejaculatory fluid). Madhi is a thin white fluid that emerges during arousal but without gushing or subsequent lethargy. It is more common in women. The text notes that if fluid is uncertain, it is presumed madhi, so no ghusl is needed, only wudu. Then it describes wadi: a thick white cloudy fluid that resembles semen in thickness but is cloudier. It often comes out after urination, when constipated, or after ghusl from intercourse. Wadi also nullifies wudu but does not require ghusl. The commentator addresses an expected objection: since wadi often follows urine, wudu is already needed; but the answer is that wadi can appear independently, so it retains its own nullifying effect. This is a definition-ruling passage.

Key Terms
مذي (madhi): Pre-ejaculatory fluid: thin, white, emitted during arousal, with no gushing or lethargy; nullifies wudu but not ghusl.
ودي (wadi): Thick, white, cloudy fluid resembling semen but cloudier; emitted after urination or heavy lifting; nullifies wudu but not ghusl.
لا يغتسل منها (lā yughtasalu minhā): Ghusl is not performed due to these things; they are not causes of ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It requires ghusl.
B) It nullifies wudu, but does not require ghusl.
C) It has no effect on purity.
D) It nullifies both wudu and requires ghusl.

Key Takeaway
Madhi and wadi are two fluids that break wudu but never make ghusl obligatory.

Obligation of Ghusl for Menstruation, Postpartum, and Pre-Islam Janabah
States obligation conditions and proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three causes of Ghusl obligation in the Hanafi school: cessation of menstruation, cessation of postpartum bleeding, and the persistence of janabah from before embracing Islam, and explain why cessation is the condition rather than the flow.

Core Idea
Ghusl becomes obligatory when the blood stops, not when it starts; and pre-Islam impurity persists.

This passage from the Hashiya clarifies a key point in purification: the obligation of Ghusl for women after menstruation and postpartum is triggered by the cessation of blood, not the bleeding itself. Additionally, the text addresses a common question about a person who embraced Islam while in a state of major impurity (janabah): does that impurity remain and require Ghusl? The answer is yes, because the rulings of janabah persist even after becoming Muslim. Let's examine the reasoning and evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three causes mentioned
Read the passage and list the three situations that obligate Ghusl: menstruation (hayd), postpartum (nifas), and pre-Islam events (such as janabah).
2
Understand the condition for menstruation and postpartum
The passage states that Ghusl is obligatory 'by menstruation' but the hashya clarifies it means 'by its cessation'. This is a condition (shart), not a cause (sabab). The obligation arises when the blood stops, not when it flows.
The commentator stresses that cessation is the condition.
3
Analyze the rule for pre-Islam janabah
The text says that if these events (like sexual intercourse or ejaculation) occurred before embracing Islam, Ghusl is still obligatory after becoming Muslim. Why? Because the state of janabah persists until purification. The condition (prayer, etc.) cannot be fulfilled without removing janabah.
The proof: the verse of wudu implies purification is required for prayer, and janabah is a greater impurity.
4
Note the dalil
For menstruation, the dalil is the Quranic command in 2:222 '...and when they have purified themselves...' which implies wujub (obligation) per the usul principle that a command indicates obligation. For postpartum, the text says 'by consensus' (ijma). For pre-Islam janabah, the reasoning is that the rulings of janabah continue, so the Muslim must purify.
5
Distinguish madhiy
The hashya adds a note that madhiy (pre-ejaculatory fluid) does not require Ghusl, only wudu. This guards against a misunderstanding that all fluids require a full bath.
This distinction is important for daily practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة منياً بعد إفاقته من سكر و بعد إفاقته من (إغماء) احتياطاً (و) يفترض (بحيض) للنص (ونفاس) بعد الطهر من نجاستهما بالانقطاع إجماعاً (و) يفترض الغسل بالموجبات (لو حصلت الأشياء المذكورة قبل الإسلام في الأصح لبقاء صفة الجنابة ونحوها بعد الإسلام ولا يمكن أداء المشروط من الصلاة ونحوها بزوال الجنابة وما في معناها إلا به فيفترض عليه لكونه مسلماً مكلفاً بالطهارة عند إرادة الصلاة ونحوها بآية الوضوء يحترز به عما لو كان مذياً فإنه لا غسل عليه قاله السيد عن شرح [؟] مثلاً مسكين. قوله : ويفترض بحيض) أي بإنقطاعه لأنّ المعدود هنا كما تقدم شروط لا أسباب وإنما أضيف الوجوب إليها تسهيلاً، والشرط هو الإنقطاع لا الخروج قوله : (ونحوها) كتواري الحشفة، والحيض والنفاس والمراد بقاء الأحكام المترتبة. قوله : (ونحوها) كسجدة التلاوة، صلاة . قوله : (بزوال) الجنابة متعلق بالمشروط، وقوله وما في معناها أي الجنابة كالحيض والنفاس، وقد مر .
Book of Purification: (Ghusl is obligatory) from semen after awakening from intoxication and after awakening from unconsciousness as a precaution. (And) it is obligatory (by menstruation) due to the text, (and postpartum) after purification from their impurity by cessation by consensus. (And) Ghusl is obligatory by the causes (if the mentioned things occurred before Islam in the most correct view due to the persistence of the state of janabah and similar after Islam, and the conditioned act of prayer and similar cannot be fulfilled without removal of janabah and what is in its meaning, so it becomes obligatory upon him because he is a Muslim obligated to purification when intending prayer and similar by the verse of wudu. [The commentator says:] It guards against the case of madhiy, for there is no Ghusl upon it, as said by al-Sayyid from the commentary of [؟] miskin. His saying: 'and it is obligatory by menstruation' means by its cessation, because what is counted here as mentioned are conditions, not causes; rather, obligation is added to them for facilitation. The condition is cessation, not the discharge. His saying: 'and similar' like concealment of the glans, menstruation and postpartum, and the meaning is the persistence of the related rulings. His saying: 'and similar' like prostration of recitation, prayer. His saying: 'by removal' of janabah is related to the conditioned act, and his saying 'and what is in its meaning' means janabah like menstruation and postpartum, and it has passed.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof stating the obligation of Ghusl for menstruation and postpartum bleeding after the blood stops, and for events like janabah that occurred before embracing Islam. The hashya clarifies that the condition for obligation is the cessation of blood, not the flow itself. The ruling is based on textual evidence (Quran) for menstruation and consensus for postpartum. For pre-Islam events, the impurity persists, so Ghusl remains obligatory. The hashya also notes that madhiy does not require Ghusl, distinguishing it from semen.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Menstrual blood; the passage treats its cessation as the condition for Ghusl obligation.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding; Ghusl becomes obligatory after its cessation.
جنابة (janabah): State of major ritual impurity requiring Ghusl; persists even after embracing Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the semen itself remains on the body

Key Takeaway
Ghusl obligation for women arises from the cessation of blood, not its start, and for converts, any major impurity from before Islam remains requiring purification.

Washing the Deceased Muslim: A Communal Obligation
Ruling statement with exceptions and details on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing the deceased Muslim as fard kifayah and identify the exceptions and conditions for validity.

Core Idea
Washing the deceased is a communal duty (fard kifayah) with specific exceptions and conditions.

In this lesson, we examine the obligation to wash a deceased Muslim. The text establishes this as a communal duty (fard kifayah) but also identifies exceptions—such as martyrs and certain criminals—and discusses the requirement of intention. Understanding these nuances ensures correct application of this important ibadah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The text explicitly states that washing a deceased Muslim is obligatory (yuf'tarad) as a sufficiency (kifayah). This means if some members of the community perform it, the obligation is lifted from the rest.
Fard kifayah differs from fard 'ayn in that it is collective, not individual.
2
List the Exceptions
The text mentions two exceptions: (1) a person who died from a crime that drops the washing (e.g., a martyr), and (2) an ambiguous hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil). For the latter, there is a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school.
The martyr is exempted because his blood is considered pure.
3
Examine the Requirement of Intention
The text discusses whether intention (niyyah) is required for this ghusl. The preferred view is that intention is a condition (shart) to drop the obligation from the living washer, not to achieve purification of the deceased.
This is based on Fath al-Qadir, as cited by al-Tahtawi.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Variation on the Khuntha Mushkil
For an ambiguous hermaphrodite, two opinions exist: some say to perform tayammum (dry ablution) on the deceased, others say to wash them in their clothes. The text indicates the first opinion (tayammum) is better.
This variation is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويفترض تغسيل الميت المسلم الذي لا جناية منه مسقطة لغسله ( كفاية) وسنذكر تمامه في محله إن شاء الله تعالى . قوله : (الذي لا جناية منه كالبغي، ولو قال الذي لا وصف له يسقط غسله ليشمل الشهيد لكان أولى، ويستثنى من الميت أيضاً الخنثى المشكل فقيل : ييمم، وقيل: يغسل في ثيابه والأول أولى، وهل يشترط لهذا الغسل النية الظاهر أنها شرط لإسقاط الوجوب عن المكلف لا لتحصيل طهارته كما في فتح القدير .
And it is obligatory to wash the deceased Muslim who has no crime from him that drops his washing (as a sufficiency). We will mention its completion in its place, God willing. His saying: (who has no crime from him) like the fornicator? And if he said 'who has no description that drops his washing' to include the martyr, it would be better. And from the deceased also the ambiguous hermaphrodite is excepted; it is said: tayammum, and it is said: wash in his clothes, and the first is better. And is intention required for this washing? The apparent is that it is a condition to drop the obligation from the responsible person, not to achieve his purification, as in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement establishing the obligation of washing the deceased Muslim as a fard kifayah (communal obligation). The exception is a person who died due to a crime that drops his washing, such as a fornicator?? Actually, the hashya clarifies exceptions: those who died with a description that drops washing (like martyr) and the ambiguous hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil). It further discusses whether intention is required, concluding that it is a condition for dropping the obligation from the living, not for the deceased's purification.

Key Terms
فريق الله (fard kifayah): A communal obligation; if some perform it, the obligation is lifted from all.
خنثى مشكل (khuntha mushkil): An ambiguous hermaphrodite whose gender cannot be determined easily.
نية (niyyah): Intention, a condition for the validity of the washer's act to discharge obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on washing a deceased Muslim who died without a disqualifying crime?
A) Wajib on every individual
B) Fard kifayah
C) Sunnah mu'akkadah
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Washing a deceased Muslim is a fard kifayah with exceptions for martyrs and ambiguous hermaphrodites, and intention is required for the washer's obligation.

Madhiy and Wadiy: Exemptions from Ghusl
Lists the first two of ten things that do not require Ghusl, with definitions and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for madhiy and wadiy regarding Ghusl and wudu, and identify the evidence (ijma) for that ruling.

Core Idea
Madhiy and wadiy break wudu but do not require Ghusl – a consensus ruling.

In this passage, the author of the hashya explains the first two of ten items that do not necessitate Ghusl: madhiy (pre-ejaculatory fluid) and wadiy (prostatic fluid). The definitions clarify their distinct characteristics, and the passage concludes with the consensus of scholars that no Ghusl is required. Understanding this distinction is essential for proper taharah (purification) in daily worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is Ghusl for madhiy and wadiy. The ruling is that it is not required (ghayr wajib).
The text explicitly says 'لا يجب الغسل'.
2
Define the substances
Madhiy is thin white fluid from sexual arousal; wadiy is thick turbid fluid after urination. Know their properties to distinguish from semen.
Both are described in the hashya.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is ijma (consensus) of the scholars, stated at the end of the passage.
The Arabic: 'أجمع العلماء على أنه لا يجب الغسل بخروج المذي والودي'.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The consensus explicitly negates obligation. Since ijma is a primary source, it provides definitive evidence.
Ijma is qat'i in thubut and dalala.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
There is no disagreement; the ruling is muttafaq alayh across schools.
This strengthens the reliability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل عشرة أشياء لا يغتسل منها مذي بفتح الميم وسكون الذال المعجمة وكسرها وهو ماء أبيض رقيق يخرج عند شهوة لا بشهوة ولا دفق ولا يعقبه فتور وربما لا يحس نحو وجه وهو أغلب في النساء من الرجال يحترز به عما لو كان مذياً فإنه لا غسل عليه قاله السيد عن شرح مثلاً مسكين. قوله : (وكسرها) أي الذال مع تخفيف الياء، وهو أفصح كالأولى وتشديدها، والفعل ثلاثي مخفف، ومضعف ورباعي قوله : (وهو ماء أبيض كدر ثخين يشبه المني في الثخانة ويخالفه في الكدرة، ويخرج قطرة، أو قطرتين عقب البول إذا كانت الطبيعة مستمسكة، وعند حمل شيء ثقيل وبعد الاغتسال من الجماع، وينقض الوضوء، فإن قيل : ما فائدة الوضوء من الودي، وقد وجب من البول قبله أجيب بأنه يخرج بدون البول كما ذكرنا فلا يرد وجوب ويسمى في جانب النساء قذي بفتح القاف والذال المعجمة (و) منها (ودي) بإسكان الدال المهملة وتخفيف الياء، وهو ماء أبيض كدر ثخين لا رائحة له يعقب البول، وقد يسبقه أجمع العلماء على أنه لا يجب الغسل بخروج المذي والودي
Section: Ten things from which Ghusl is not performed: Madhiy – with fatḥ on the mīm and sukūn on the dhāl and also kasr – it is thin white fluid that emerges at the time of desire, not with desire nor with gushing nor is it followed by lethargy, and perhaps one does not feel it except from the face? It is more common in women than men. [The commentator says:] It guards against the case of madhiy, for there is no Ghusl from it, as said by al-Sayyid from the commentary of [؟] miskin. His saying: (and kasr on it) meaning the dhāl with tashdīd on the yā, and this is more eloquent like the first, and also tashdid, and the verb is triliteral without tashdid, and with tashdid, and quadriliteral. His saying: (It is a thick, turbid white fluid, resembling semen in thickness but differing in turbidity. It emerges as a drop or two after urination when the nature is constipated, and when carrying something heavy, and after Ghusl from intercourse. It nullifies wudu. If it is said: what is the benefit of wudu from wadi when wudu is already obligatory from urine before it? The answer is that it exits without urine as we mentioned, so the objection does not stand.) And it is called in women 'qudhī' with fatḥ on the qāf and the dhāl. (And from them) (wadiy) with sukūn on the dāl and tashdīd on the yā, and it is a thick, turbid white fluid with no odor, following urine and sometimes preceding it. The scholars are in consensus that Ghusl is not obligatory for the emission of madhiy and wadiy.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage opens a section listing ten items that do not necessitate Ghusl, starting with madhiy (pre-ejaculate) and wadiy (prostatic fluid). The hashya provides detailed definitions: madhiy is thin white fluid from sexual arousal, more common in women; wadiy is thick, turbid fluid that often follows urination. Both nullify wudu but not Ghusl. The passage concludes with the consensus of scholars that no Ghusl is required for these two fluids.

Key Terms
مذي (madhiy): Pre-ejaculatory fluid; thin white fluid emerging at sexual arousal; does not require Ghusl.
ودي (wadiy): Prostatic fluid or thick discharge after urination; does not require Ghusl.
قذي (qudhī): Female equivalent of wadiy; thick discharge after urination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on Ghusl for the emission of madhiy?
A) Ghusl is wajib
B) Ghusl is not required, but it nullifies wudu
C) Only wudu is required
D) Nothing is required

Key Takeaway
Madhiy and wadiy break wudu but do not require Ghusl, based on scholarly consensus.

Eight Cases Where Ghusl Is Not Obligatory: Hanafi Exemptions and Evidence
Listing and explaining eight exemptions from Ghusl with intra-madhhab differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight exemptions from Ghusl in the Hanafi school, state the condition that triggers Ghusl in each case, and explain the intra-Hanafi differences regarding the ihtilam case.

Core Idea
Ghusl is required only upon seeing water or confirmed emission; these eight cases show when it is not.

After establishing the general obligation of Ghusl for janabah, the text lists eight specific situations where Ghusl is not obligatory. These illustrate the legal principle that mere contact or arousal is insufficient—physical evidence of ejaculation or complete penetration is necessary. The passage also notes precautionary positions from various Hanafi imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first case: nocturnal emission without wetness
Determine the ruling and the proof from the hadith of Umm Sulaym.
2
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference
Muhammad's position requiring precautionary Ghusl vs. the majority view.
This is an important example of ikhtilaf within the school.
3
Move to childbirth without blood
Note the difference between Sahibayn (no Ghusl) and Abu Hanifa (precautionary Ghusl).
4
Continue through remaining cases
Insertion with barrier, enema, finger/object insertion (distinguish anal vs. vaginal), intercourse with animal/dead woman, and virgin without hymen break.
Pay attention to the chosen views and conditions.
5
Synthesize the common principle
Ghusl is only obligatory when there is either visible semen or complete penetration with intent.
This synthesizes the underlying rationale.
