(And from them) (childbirth without seeing any blood after it; in the correct view it is the position of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) due to absence of nifas, and Imam (Abu Hanifa) said: Ghusl is obligatory upon her as a precaution because she is not free from a little apparent blood as presented). (And from them) (insertion with a cloth preventing the existence of pleasure), and we have stated the obligation of Ghusl with it as a precaution. (And from them) (enema) because it is for expelling wastes, not for fulfilling desire. (And from them) insertion of a finger and similar like a fake penis made of leather (into one of the two passages) according to the chosen view due to deficiency of desire. (And from them) intercourse with an animal (or a dead woman without ejaculation of semen) because of the incompleteness of its cause and its emission is not predominant here to stand in its place. (And from them) hitting a virgin whose virginity has not been removed (without ejaculation) because the hymen prevents the meeting of the two circumcisions. And if his semen enters her vagina without insertion, there is no Ghusl upon her unless she becomes pregnant from it. The commentator says: (in the apparent narration) Muhammad said: Ghusl is obligatory upon her as a precaution. His saying: (hadith of Umm Sulaym) which is in the two Sahihs from Umm Salama: Umm Sulaym asked the Prophet about Ghusl for a woman who has a wet dream; he said: Yes, if she sees water. Al-Kamal said: The meaning of 'seeing' is knowledge, whether connected to sight or not; if one is certain of ejaculation after awakening but it has dried and she sees nothing with her eyes, no one can say that Ghusl is not required even though she saw nothing with her eyes. His saying: (preventing) presence of pleasure; he limited to pleasure here, and added earlier the presence of heat, perhaps they are simultaneous. His saying: (as a precaution) apparently it is the cause of the obligation, as indicated by the expression 'luzum' (binding), and also in the previous issue indicated by 'alayha' implying obligation. His saying: (according to the chosen view) i.e., in the anus, and the opposite is weak; as for the vagina, it is mentioned in Sharh al-Tanwir that the chosen view is also no obligation, and 'Allamah Nuh reported that the chosen view is obligation if she intends enjoyment because desire in them (women) is predominant, so the cause is substituted for the effect. Thus the tarjih differs regarding finger insertion in the vagina of a woman. Quoting al-Sayyid. His saying: (unless she becomes pregnant) because she does not become pregnant unless she has ejaculated, and she should pray before Ghusl? This is one of two sayings; and it is said: no Ghusl upon her even if pregnancy appears unless her semen comes out to the external private part, and that is the apparent narration; al-Halwani said: we adopt this; see al-Zayla'i.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage lists eight more items that do not require Ghusl, with intra-Hanafi differences. 1) Nocturnal emission without wetness: no Ghusl for either gender by the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), but Muhammad says precautionary Ghusl. 2) Childbirth without blood: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no Ghusl; Abu Hanifa says precautionary Ghusl. 3) Insertion with a barrier: if pleasure is prevented, Ghusl not required, but precaution is stated. 4) Enema: no Ghusl as it is not for sexual release. 5) Finger/object insertion: chosen view is no Ghusl except in certain cases for women if intended for pleasure. 6) Intercourse with animal/dead woman without ejaculation: no Ghusl as cause is incomplete. 7) Intercourse with a virgin without breaking hymen: no Ghusl for her if no ejaculation, but if pregnant, Ghusl may be required. The commentary cites hadith from Umm Sulaym and explains nuances.

Key Terms
احتلام (ihtilam): Nocturnal emission; Ghusl is not required if no wetness (no discharge seen).
خرقة (khirqah): A cloth barrier preventing stimulation; insertion with such a barrier does not require Ghusl.
بكرة (bikr): Virgin; if intercourse does not break hymen and no ejaculation, no Ghusl for her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is Ghusl required for a woman after a wet dream in which she feels arousal but finds no wetness?
A) Yes, Ghusl is required
B) No, Ghusl is not required
C) Only wudu is required
D) It is recommended but not obligatory

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, Ghusl is not automatically required by mere arousal or contact; physical signs (semen, blood, meeting of circumcisions) are decisive.

Events That Do Not Require Ghusl: Rulings and Proof
Compilation of rulings on events that do not necessitate ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state six events that do not necessitate ghusl in the Hanafi school, explain the proof from the hadith of Umm Sulaym, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on childbirth.

Core Idea
Ghusl is only required when water (semen) is seen; absence of moisture after a wet dream or childbirth without blood does not obligate ghusl.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi lists several situations where ghusl (full ritual bath) is not required, despite potential confusion. The rulings are supported by the prophetic hadith of Umm Sulaym, which conditions ghusl on 'seeing water' (actual emission). Within the Hanafi school, there is a difference of opinion on childbirth without blood and on whether precaution obligates ghusl in certain cases. We will work through the list and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall theme
The passage lists events that do NOT require ghusl. Note that some of these are causes of janabah in other schools, but Hanafi fiqh restricts ghusl to actual emission.
2
Analyze the ruling on madhi and wadi
Scholars agree that ghusl is not required for either madhi or wadi; these only nullify wudu. Distinguish between wadi (thick white fluid after urine) and semen.
Consensus (ijma) is mentioned.
3
Understand the wet dream ruling and the hadith
The hadith of Umm Sulaym (recorded in Bukhari and Muslim) states that ghusl is only required 'when she sees water' (i.e., actual emission). The Hanafi position is that a woman is like a man: no ghusl without moisture. Muhammad's precautionary view is noted but the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) is relied upon.
Pay attention to the conditional phrase 'idha ra'at al-ma'.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on childbirth
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that ghusl is obligatory as a precaution due to possible unseen blood. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no ghusl because there is no nifas (postpartum bleeding). The mufta bihi is the position of the two (no ghusl).
5
Review the remaining cases
The passage lists insertion with a cloth barrier, enema, finger/artificial penis insertion, intercourse with animal/dead woman, and touching a virgin. These do not require ghusl because the cause of janabah (direct ejaculation) is absent or incomplete.
Some cases have precautionary obligation, but the preferred view is no ghusl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسمى في جانب النساء قذي بفتح القاف والذال المعجمة (و) منها (ودي) بإسكان الدال المهملة وتخفيف الياء، وهو ماء أبيض كدر ثخين لا رائحة له يعقب البول، وقد يسبقه. أجمع العلماء على أنه لا يجب الغسل بخروج المذي والودي (و) منها (احتلام بلا بلل والمرأة فيه كالرجل في ظاهر الرواية لحديث أم سليم كما قدمناه (و) منها (ولادة من غير رؤية دم بعدها في الصحيح وهو قولهما لعدم النفاس وقال الإمام: عليها الغسل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقالت : يا رسول الله أن الله لا يستحي من الحق هل على المرأة من غسل إذا هي . احتلمت؟ قال: نعم إذا رأت الماء أهـ. قال الكمال : والمراد بالرؤية العلم سواء اتصلت به رؤية البصر أم الحرارة، ولعلهما متلازمان كما مر . قوله : (احتياطاً) الظاهر أنه علة الإفتراض بدليل التعبير باللزوم وكذلك في المسئلة التي قبلها بدليل التعبير بعليها المفيدة للوجوب . قوله : (على المختار) أي في الدبر ومقابله ضعيف وأما في القبل فذكر في شرح التنوير أن المختار عدمه أيضاً، وحكى العلامة نوح أن المختار فيه الوجوب إذا قصدت الإستمتاع لأن الشهوة فيهن غالبة، فيقام السبب مقام المسبب، فاختلف الترجيح بالنسبة لإدخال الإصبع في قبل المرأة. أفاده السيد رحمه الله تعالى قوله : (ما لم تحبل لأنها لا تحبل إلا إذا أنزلت، وتبعد ما صلت قبل الغسل، وهذا أحد قولين وقيل : لا غسل عليها، ولو ظهر الحبل إلا إذا خرج منيها إلى ظاهر الفرج، وهو ظاهر الرواية قال الحلواني : وبه نأخذ انظر الزيلعي .
And it is called in the context of women 'qadhi' (with fath of qaf and dhal) and among them is 'wadi' (with sukun of dal and tashdid of ya), which is a thick white cloudy water with no odor that follows urine and may precede it. The scholars agree that ghusl is not obligatory due to the emission of madhi and wadi. And among them is a wet dream without moisture, and the woman is like the man in the apparent narration due to the hadith of Umm Sulaym as we have mentioned. And among them is childbirth without seeing blood after it, which is correct according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) due to the absence of nifas; and Imam (Abu Hanifa) said: ghusl is obligatory upon her as a precaution due to the fact that she is not free from a little apparent blood. And among them is insertion with a cloth that prevents the sensation of pleasure; we have already stated that ghusl is necessary as a precaution. And among them is an enema because it is for expelling waste for satisfying desire. And among them is insertion of a finger or similar like an artificial penis made of leather into one of the two passages according to the preferred view due to weak desire. And among them is intercourse with an animal or a dead woman without ejaculation of semen due to incompleteness of the cause and it is not predominant here to take the place. And among them is touching a virgin whose virginity has not been removed without ejaculation because virginity prevents the meeting of the two circumcisions; and if his semen enters her vagina without insertion, no ghusl is required upon her unless she becomes pregnant from it. The question: or it is said its benefit appears for one who has urinary incontinence, for his ablution is nullified by wadi but not by urine. His statement: 'and among them is a wet dream' – its wording predominates for what the sleeper sees of intercourse usually accompanied by ejaculation, and it is impossible for prophets (peace be upon them) because it is satanic, and they are protected from it, although they may have whisperings. This is mentioned by some. And in al-Khasa'is, that among them is the conversion of his companion. His statement: 'in the apparent narration' – and Muhammad said: ghusl is obligatory upon her as a precaution. His statement: 'the hadith of Umm Sulaym' – it is what is in the Sahihayn from Umm Salama (may Allah be pleased with them both) who said: Umm Sulaym, the wife of Abu Talha, came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and said: O Messenger of Allah, indeed Allah is not shy of the truth, is there ghusl upon a woman when she has a wet dream? He said: Yes, when she sees water. Al-Kamal said: what is meant by seeing is knowledge, whether it is connected to seeing with the eye or not; for if one is certain of ejaculation after waking up, then it dries and she does not see anything with her eyes, no one can say that ghusl is not required even though she did not see anything with her eyes. His statement: 'preventing' from the existence of pleasure – he restricted mentioning pleasure here, and added in what preceded the existence of heat; perhaps they are inseparable. His statement: 'as a precaution' – apparently it is the cause for obligation, as indicated by the expression of necessity, and similarly in the previous issue as indicated by the expression 'upon her' which indicates obligation. His statement: 'according to the preferred view' – meaning in the anus; the opposite is weak. As for the front, it is mentioned in Sharh al-Tanwir that the preferred view is non-obligation as well. And Allamah Noah narrated that the preferred view is obligation if she intends pleasure because desire is predominant in them, so the cause takes the place of the effect. Thus the tarjih differs regarding insertion of the finger into the woman's front. This was conveyed by al-Sayyid (may Allah have mercy on him). His statement: 'unless she becomes pregnant because she only becomes pregnant if she ejaculates; and it is far-fetched that she prayed before ghusl; and this is one of two opinions. And it is said: no ghusl is required upon her even if pregnancy appears except if her semen emerges to the external part of the vagina, and that is the apparent narration. Al-Halwani said: we take this. See al-Zayla'i.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings (with proof and intra-Hanafi disputes) regarding events that do NOT necessitate ghusl (full ritual bath). The base text lists these events, and the hashia (super-commentary) clarifies and refines them.

The author first defines wadi (ودي), a thick white fluid that follows urine. He notes consensus that ghusl is not required for wadi or madhi (pre-seminal fluid).
Then he lists: (1) a wet dream without any moisture – for a woman, the ruling is the same as a man according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) from Imam Muhammad's books, based on the hadith of Umm Sulaym (reported by Umm Salama) that ghusl is only required if water (semen) is seen. (2) Childbirth without any blood: this is the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two disciples) because there is no nifas (postpartum bleeding). However, Imam Abu Hanifa says ghusl is obligatory as a precaution, assuming a little blood that may not be seen. (3) Insertion of the penis into the vagina with a barrier that prevents pleasure: ghusl is still necessary as a precaution. (4) Enema: because it is for expelling waste and satisfying desire (but it does not require ghusl). (5) Insertion of a finger or an artificial penis into the anus: according to the preferred view, no ghusl because desire is weak; but for the front (vagina) there is disagreement: the preferred view is no ghusl, but some say obligatory if she intends pleasure. (6) Intercourse with an animal or a dead woman without ejaculation: no ghusl because the cause is incomplete. (7) Touching a virgin without breaking the hymen and without ejaculation: no ghusl unless pregnancy occurs, which indicates seminal emission. The commentary discusses details: the hadith, the meaning of 'seeing water', the precaution principle, and the practical application for women who are certain of ejaculation but saw no moisture. The commentator also notes that the benefit of distinguishing wadi from urine appears for someone with urinary incontinence: his wudu is nullified by wadi but not by urine, affecting the ruling on wudu renewal.

Key Terms
ودي (wadi): A thick white fluid with no odor that follows urine and sometimes precedes it; it is not a cause for ghusl but nullifies wudu.
احتلام (ihtilam): A wet dream, i.e., seeing intercourse in a dream usually accompanied by ejaculation; ghusl is required only if moisture (semen) is seen upon waking.
إيلاج بخرقة (ilaj bi-khirqah): Insertion of the penis into the vagina with a cloth barrier that prevents direct contact and pleasure; ghusl is still necessary as a precaution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on ghusl for a woman who has a wet dream but does not find any moisture upon waking?
A) Ghusl is required because the dream is evidence of janabah.
B) Ghusl is not required, based on the hadith of Umm Sulaym.
C) Ghusl is required as a precaution according to all Hanafi imams.
D) Ghusl is not required, but she must perform tayammum instead.

Key Takeaway
Ghusl is only obligatory when actual emission (water/semen) occurs or is certain; many events do not trigger ghusl in Hanafi fiqh.

Taharah — Purification

The Obligatory Acts of Ghusl and the Quranic Proof
Statement of obligatory acts of ghusl with Quranic proof and inter-madhhab analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eleven obligatory acts of ghusl in the Hanafi school and explain why washing the mouth and nose is fard based on the Quranic proof from 'fātahharū'.

Core Idea
Washing the mouth and nose in ghusl is fard because the Quranic command 'fātahharū' demands comprehensive purification.

Understanding the fara'id (obligatory acts) of ghusl is essential for correct ritual purification. The Hanafi school enumerates eleven acts, but they all boil down to a single principle: saturating the entire body with water. This lesson unpacks those obligations, focusing on the controversial inclusion of mouth and nose rinsing, and traces the proof from the Quran and the methodology of Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage lists eleven fara'id for ghusl, all of which revolve around one core obligation: covering the entire body with water without hardship. The list is pedagogical.
The number eleven is specific to Hanafi teaching; other schools may not enumerate them.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof for the obligation of rinsing the mouth and nose is the Quranic verse 'fātahharū' (so purify yourselves) from Surah al-Ma'idah (5:6). The companion text cites this as evidence.
The same verse is used for wudu; the difference lies in how the face is defined.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Hanafi argument hinges on the hyperbolic form (ṣīghat al-mubālagha) of the verb 'tahara'. This form indicates a comprehensive purification that includes hidden parts like the interior of the mouth and nose, unlike the face in wudu which is defined by what is visible (muwājahah).
No hardship is involved in washing these parts, so the obligation applies.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i consider rinsing the mouth and nose to be sunnah in both ghusl and wudu. They do not accept the Hanafi interpretation of the verse's hyperbole.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different usul principles.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the apparent meaning of the hyperbole and the lack of hardship. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that they are fard in ghusl, as affirmed by the commentators of the school.
The authority comes from the early Hanafi masters and confirmed in later works like al-Durr and al-Fath.

Source Text

For explaining the obligatory acts of ghusl: obligatory in washing from menstruation, janaba, or nifas are eleven things, all of which revert to one: encompassing the body with water as far as possible without hardship, but they are enumerated for instruction. Among them: washing the mouth and nose – it is an ijtihadi obligation due to His statement 'fātahharū' (so purify yourselves), unlike in wudu because the face does not include them because facing does not occur inside the nose and mouth, but the hyperbolic form in 'fātahharū' includes them and there is no hardship in them. And the body – a general term coupled with a specific term; included is the external private part because it is like the palm, not the internal because it is like the interior. It is necessary to remove what prevents water from reaching the body, like wax, dough, dye that colors, but not what is between the nails even for a madani (city dweller) according to the correct view, like the droppings of a flea and the excrement of a fly as previously mentioned. And the obligation is washing once thoroughly, because the command does not necessitate repetition. And it is obligatory to wash inside the foreskin if there is no difficulty in retracting it, according to the correct view; if it is difficult, he is not required, like a closed piercing due to hardship. And it is obligatory to wash inside a hollow navel because it is part of the external body and there is no hardship in washing it. And it is obligatory to wash any non-closed piercing due to lack of hardship. And it is obligatory to wash inside the braided hair... (commentary) His statement: 'from menstruation, janaba, or nifas' – al-Bahr said: the apparent meaning is that rinsing the mouth and nose are not conditions for the sunnah ghusl such that it becomes valid without them, but they are conditions for fulfilling the sunnah as in al-Durr; and their existence in wudu suffices for their fulfillment at the beginning of ghusl. And his statement: 'for fulfilling the sunnah' means the sunnah of the recommended ghusl, not that they are conditions for its being sunnah. His statement: 'washing the mouth and nose' – without exaggeration in them, for exaggeration is sunnah in them according to the relied-upon view; and drinking water in gulps suffices for washing the mouth, not sucking. If his tooth is hollow and food remains in it or between his teeth, or if there is moist dirt in his nose, it is acceptable because water is subtle and reaches every place generally, unlike dry dirt like chewed bread and dough which prevent, as in al-Fath. His statement: 'His statement: (fātahharū)' – and because they are washed habitually, and as an act of worship they are sunnah in wudu and obligatory in real impurity, and this indicates that they are part of the apparent. His statement: 'a general term coupled with a specific term' – and he singled them out because there is disagreement about them, as they are sunnah according to the two imams Malik and al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with them), and because the one who denies them is not deemed a disbeliever. His statement: 'and from it the external private part and from it the exit of stool.' His statement: 'like the droppings of a flea and the excrement (of a fly) even if water does not reach underneath it – al-Sayyid said: al-waneem is the droppings of flies.' His statement: 'inside' – the foreskin is the skin covering the glans, and circumcision is its cutting; end from al-Sharh.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the section on the obligatory acts (fara'id) of ghusl (full ritual bath) in the Hanafi school. The base text enumerates eleven things that are fard in ghusl, all of which boil down to one: covering the entire body with water as much as possible without hardship. They are listed for pedagogical purposes.

The first obligatory act is washing the mouth and nose. This is a 'mujtahad fih' (based on ijtihad) obligation, derived from the Quranic command 'fātahharū' (so purify yourselves) which is hyperbolic and includes the interior of mouth and nose. Unlike in wudu, where rinsing mouth and nose are sunnah because the face does not include them, in ghusl they are fard because the command to purify comprehensively requires it. The commentator clarifies that there is no hardship in washing them.

Second is washing the entire body, which includes the external private parts (like the palm) but not the interior (like the inner body). It is necessary to remove any substance that prevents water from reaching the skin, such as wax, dough, or dye that forms a layer. However, dirt under nails (even for city dwellers) is excused, as are small residues like flea droppings or fly excrement, because water is subtle and reaches underneath.

The actual washing must be one complete coverage, as the command (amr) does not imply repetition. Washing inside the foreskin is obligatory if it can be retracted without difficulty; if it is difficult (e.g., due to a tight piercing), it is not required due to hardship. Similarly, washing inside a hollow navel and any non-closed piercing is obligatory. The passage ends mid-sentence about braided hair (presumably washing inside braids is required), but the commentary discusses the conditions of rinsing the mouth and nose: they are not conditions for the validity of ghusl but for fulfilling its sunnah. The commentator quotes from al-Bahr and al-Durr that performing them in wudu suffices. Exaggeration in rinsing is sunnah, and drinking water in gulps suffices for mouth rinsing. Dry residues (like chewed bread) prevent water from reaching the skin, but moist residues do not, as water penetrates them.

The commentator also notes that the mouth and nose are singled out because there is disagreement: Malik and al-Shafi'i consider them sunnah, not fard. In Hanafi, they are fard in ghusl but sunnah in wudu. The one who denies their obligation is not deemed a disbeliever because the proof is zanni (based on ijtihad).

Key Terms
فرائض الغسل (fara'id al-ghusl): The obligatory elements of the full ritual bath, enumerated as eleven in the Hanafi school, all subsumed under the single principle of saturating the entire body with water without hardship.
فرض اجتهادي (fard ijtihadi): An obligation derived through ijtihad from a text that is open to interpretation; denying it does not make one a disbeliever.
قلفة (qulfah): The foreskin covering the glans of the penis; washing inside is obligatory if retractable without difficulty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis consider washing the mouth and nose obligatory (fard) in ghusl but not in wudu?
A) Because the Quranic verse 'fātahharū' indicates comprehensive purification, and the face in wudu does not include interior parts.
B) Because there is a hadith specifically commanding it for ghusl.
C) Because they are part of the body and must be washed in both acts.
D) Because the command for ghusl is stronger than for wudu.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the obligatory acts of ghusl include washing the mouth and nose based on the hyperbolic Quranic command, and this differs from their status as sunnah in wudu.

Taharah — Purification

The Eleven Obligatory Acts of Ghusl (Fara'id)
Enumeration and detailed commentary on the obligatory acts of ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eleven obligatory acts of ghusl and explain the Hanafi proof for washing the mouth and nose as fard.

Core Idea
Washing mouth and nose is fard in ghusl — omission invalidates the bath.

In the Hanafi school, ghusl from janabah, menstruation, or postpartum bleeding has eleven obligatory acts (fara'id). While all reduce to covering the entire body with water, these details ensure no part is missed. The most distinctive is the obligation to rinse the mouth and nose, based on the Quranic imperative 'fatahharu'. This lesson unpacks each obligation, the proofs, and the inter-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Eleven Acts
Locate in the text the list of eleven obligatory acts. Note that they all reduce to covering the body with water once, but they are enumerated for clarity.
The text counts mouth, nose, body, foreskin, navel, piercings, man's braids, woman's braids conditionally, beard, mustache, eyebrows, and external genitals.
2
Understand the Proof for Mouth and Nose
Analyze the use of 'fatahharu' (so purify yourselves) from Surah Al-Muddaththir. The imperative indicates obligation, and the intensive form (mubalaghah) extends to the interior of the mouth and nose.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub.
3
Compare with Opposing Views
Contrast the Hanafi position with the Shafi'i/Maliki view that rinsing mouth and nose are Sunnah. Note the thamarah al-khilaf: validity of ghusl without them.
The hadith of Umm Salama is also used for women's hair, not for mouth/nose.
4
Apply Specific Rulings for Hair
For men with braids: must undo absolutely. For women: not required if water reaches roots, based on the hadith. Distinguish when matting or density requires undoing.
The 'dafirah' (braid) is defined as a lock of twisted hair.
5
Assess Barriers and their Removal
Identify what counts as a barrier (wax, dough, dry food) vs. what does not (wet dirt, dye that does not block). Understand that the obligation is to wash one time, not repeated.
The text allows for hardship in retracting foreskin or washing piercings if difficult.

إذا سرى في أصوله، وعمه الماء كله لا يلزم حله، وفسره بعض الإطلاق بقوله سواء كان علوياً، أو تركياً. قال السيد: وما في العيني من قوله إلا إذا كان علوياً، أو تركياً للحرج متعقب بأن دعوى الحرج ممنوعة اهـ. قوله: (وأما إن كان شعرها ملبداً أو غزيراً بحيث يمنع إيصال الماء إلى الأصول. قوله: (ولا يفترض إيصال الماء إلى أثناء ذوائبها على الصحيح) احترز به عن قول بعضهم يجب بلها وعما في صلاة البقالي الصحيح أنه يجب غسل الذوائب، وإن جاوزت القدمين، وتمامه في الشرح. قوله : والضفيره بالضاد المعجمة الذؤابة) قال في القاموس : الذؤابة الناصية، أو منبتها من الرأس، وشعر في أصل ناصية الفرس اهـ والمراد الخصلة وهي كما في القاموس بالضم الشعر المجتمع أو القليل منه .اهـ. قوله: (والضفر فتل الشعر الخ) وأما العقص فجمعه على الرأس. قوله: (وثمن الماء) أي لشرب ووضوء، وغسل على الزوج لأنه مما لا بد منه اهـ شرح قوله: (ولو انقطع حيضها لعشرة) وبعهم قال إذا كان إنقطاع الحيض لأقل من عشرة، فعلى الزوج لإحتياجه إلى وطنها بعد الغسل وإن كان لعشرة فعليها لأنها المحتاجة للصلاة، هي ويعلم منه أن أجرة الحمام حيث اضطرت إليه عليه، وفي الخانية دخول الحمام مشروع للرجال والنساء. قال الكمال وحيث أبحنا لها الخروج للحمام إنما يباح بشرط عدم الزينة، وتغيير الهيئة إلى ما لا يكون داعياً إلى نظر الرجال والإستمالة اهـ أي وبشرط عدم نظرهن إلى عورة بعضهن وإلا حرم كما لا يخفي ولو ضرها غسل رأسها تركته، ولا تمنع نفسها عن زوجها.
Book of Purification: To explain the obligatory acts of ghusl. It is obligatory in the bath from menstruation, janabah, or nifas (eleven things). All of them return to one: covering the entire body with water as possible without hardship, but they were listed for instruction. Among them: (washing the mouth and nose) which is an ijtihadi obligation due to His statement 'so purify yourselves', contrary to them in wudu because the face does not include them because facing does not occur inside the nose and mouth, and the intensive form in 'fatahharu' includes them and there is no hardship in them. (And the body) is a general conjunct to a specific one, and from it is the external private part because it is like the two openings, not the internal because it is like the throat. It is necessary to remove anything that prevents water from reaching the body, like wax, dough, dye that is a dye, and not what is between the nails even if for a Madani, in the correct view, like flea droppings and fly dung as previously mentioned. The obligatory wash is (one) thorough time because the command does not require repetition. And it is obligatory to wash inside the foreskin where there is no difficulty in retracting it, in the correct view, and if it is difficult, he is not obliged, like a closed piercing due to hardship. And it is obligatory to wash inside a hollow navel because it is part of the external body and there is no hardship in washing it. And it is obligatory to wash an open piercing because there is no hardship. And it is obligatory to wash within the braided hair of a man, and he must undo it absolutely in the correct view, whether water reaches its roots or not, because it is not an adornment for him, so there is no hardship. And it is not obligatory to undo the braided hair of a woman if water reaches its roots, by agreement, based on the hadith of Umm Salama, may Allah be pleased with her, that she said: O Messenger of Allah, I am a woman who braids my hair tightly; should I undo it for ghusl of janabah? He said: It suffices you to pour three handfuls of water over your head, then pour water over the rest of your body, and you become pure. But if her hair is matted or dense, then she must undo it. And it is not obligatory to make water reach inside her locks in the correct view, contrary to the man, for whom it is obligatory upon him to wet all his locks. The 'dafira' (with the dotted dad) is the lock, the strand of hair. 'Dafr' is twisting hair and inserting part into part. The price of water is upon the husband for her even if she is rich. And if her menstruation stops at ten (days), and it is obligatory to wash the skin of the beard and its hair even if it is thick and dense, due to His statement 'so purify yourselves'. And it is obligatory to wash the skin of the mustache and the skin of the eyebrow and their hair, and the external private part because it is like the mouth, not the internal because it is like the throat as previously mentioned. And she said: His statement: (from menstruation, janabah, or nifas). He said in al-Bahr: Apparently, rinsing the mouth and nose are not conditions for the Sunnah ghusl such that it is valid without them, but they are conditions for attaining the Sunnah as in al-Durr, and their presence in wudu suffices for attaining them at the beginning of ghusl. And his statement: in attaining the Sunnah, i.e., the Sunnah of the Sunnah ghusl, and it is not meant that they are conditions for its being Sunnah. His statement: (washing the mouth and nose) i.e., without exaggeration in them, for exaggeration is Sunnah in them on the relied opinion. Drinking water by gulping takes the place of washing the mouth, not by sucking. If his tooth is hollow and food remains in it, or between his teeth, or there is wet dirt in his nose, it suffices because water is subtle and reaches every place usually, unlike dry things like chewed bread and dough, which prevent, as in al-Fath. His statement: (His statement: 'so purify yourselves') and because they are washed habitually and worshipfully as supererogatory in wudu and obligatory in real impurity, and this indicates that they are part of the apparent. His statement: (a general conjunct to a specific one) and he singled them out only because of the dispute concerning them, as they are Sunnah according to the two Imams Malik and al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with them, and because the one who denies them does not become a disbeliever. His statement: (and from it the external private part and from it the anus). His statement: like flea droppings and fly dung, even if water does not reach what is under it, said by al-Sayyid: and 'al-wanim' is fly dung. His statement: (inside) the foreskin is the covering skin over the glans, and circumcision is its cutting, from the commentary. His statement: (whether water reaches its roots or not) in it: if water reaches its roots and covers all the hair, undoing is not required, and some explain the absolute statement as: whether it is (the hair) on top or of a Turk. Al-Sayyid said: What is in al-'Ayni from his statement: except if it is on top or of a Turk, due to hardship, is objectionable because the claim of hardship is not accepted. His statement: (But if her hair is matted or dense such that it prevents water from reaching the roots. His statement: (And it is not obligatory to make water reach inside her locks in the correct view) is a precaution against the statement of some that wetting them is obligatory, and against what is in Salah al-Baqali that it is obligatory to wash the locks even if they exceed the feet, and its completion in the commentary. His statement: 'al-dafira' with the dotted dad is 'al-dhu'aba'. In al-Qamus: al-dhu'aba is the forelock or its place of growth, and hair at the root of the horse's forelock. The intended meaning is 'al-khusla', which is, as in al-Qamus, with damma, the gathered hair or a small amount of it. His statement: ('al-dafr' is twisting hair etc.) As for 'al-'aqs', it is gathering it on the head. His statement: (The price of water) i.e., for drinking, wudu, and ghusl, is upon the husband because it is indispensable. Commentary on his statement: (And if her menstruation stops at ten). And they said: If the cessation of menstruation is for less than ten, then it is upon the husband because he needs to have intercourse with her after ghusl; if it is for ten, then it is upon her because she needs to pray. From this it is known that the hire of the bathhouse, if she is compelled to go there, is upon him. In al-Khaniyya, entering the bathhouse is permissible for men and women. Al-Kamal said: Where we permitted her to go out to the bathhouse, it is permitted only on condition of not adorning herself and changing her appearance to something that would not invite men's gaze and attraction. That is, and on condition that they do not look at each other's awrah, otherwise it is forbidden as is not hidden. And if washing her head harms her, she may leave it, and she should not prevent herself from her husband.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah explains the obligatory acts (fara'id) of ghusl. The author begins by stating that there are eleven obligatory things in ghusl from menstruation, janabah, or nifas, but all reduce to one: covering the entire body with water as possible without hardship. They are counted for instructional purposes. The first pair is washing the mouth and nose. This is considered an ijtihadi obligation based on the Quranic verse 'fatahharu' (so purify yourselves). The author distinguishes this from wudu, where mouth and nose are Sunnah, because the face does not include the interior of the nose and mouth in the literal meaning of 'face'. However, the intensive form of the verb indicates comprehensiveness. There is no hardship in washing them. Next, the body is mentioned as a general term covering all specific parts, including the external genitals but not the internal parts. It is necessary to remove any barrier that prevents water from reaching the skin, such as wax or dough. However, substances like dried food between teeth or fly dung are not barriers if they are thin. The obligatory wash is one complete time, as command does not imply repetition. Then the author lists specific obligations: washing inside the foreskin if it can be retracted without difficulty; if difficult, it is not required (like a closed piercing). Similarly, washing inside a hollow navel and an open piercing is obligatory. For a man's braided hair, he must undo it absolutely, regardless of whether water reaches the roots, because it is not an adornment and there is no hardship. For a woman's braids, she does not need to undo them if water reaches the roots, based on the hadith of Umm Salama. If her hair is matted or dense, she must undo it. The man must ensure water reaches every lock, but the woman does not have to reach inside the locks in the correct view. The price of water for ghusl is on the husband even if the wife is rich. If her menstruation stops at ten days, the wife is responsible for the water cost for her own ghusl. The passage also covers washing the skin of the beard, mustache, eyebrows, and external genitals. The commentary includes explanations from various sources like al-Bahr, al-Durr, al-Fath, and al-'Ayni, clarifying conditions and disputes. Notably, the mouth and nose are considered Sunnah by Imams Malik and al-Shafi'i, but the Hanafi school holds them as obligatory (fard). The author also discusses that if water can penetrate to the roots, undoing braids is not required, and the penalty for omission of these obligations is that the ghusl is invalid and must be repeated.

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl): The ritual bath required to remove major impurity (janabah, menstruation, nifas).
المضمضة والاستنشاق (al-madmadah wa al-istinshaq): Rinsing the mouth and sniffing water into the nose. In the context of ghusl, they are fard according to Hanafi.
القلفة (al-qulfah): The foreskin covering the glans. Washing inside it is obligatory if there is no difficulty.
الضفيرة (al-dafirah): A braid or lock of hair. The ruling differs for men and women regarding whether it must be undone during ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a man with braided hair during ghusl from janabah?
A) He must undo the braids only if water does not reach the roots.
B) He must undo the braids absolutely, regardless of water reaching the roots.
C) He does not need to undo the braids if they are tightly braided.
D) He must undo the braids only if he is bald.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds eleven specific acts as fard in ghusl, with mouth and nose being a distinctive obligation, and the details regarding hair and barriers must be observed for ghusl to be valid.

Rulings on Washing Hair and Beard in Ghusl
Commentary on obligatory washing of hair, beard, and private parts in ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing hair and beard in ghusl, explain the difference between men and women regarding undoing hair, and apply the proof from the hadith of Umm Salamah.

Core Idea
In ghusl, man must undo all hair; woman need not undo braids if water reaches roots, but must if hair is matted.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi expands on the rulings of washing hair and other body parts in ghusl. It clarifies the obligations for men and women regarding undoing braids, using the hadith of Umm Salama as proof, and lists the areas that must be washed, including the beard, mustache, eyebrows, and private parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (ghusl from janabah) and what the ruling is for each component: man's hair, woman's hair, beard, etc.
The ruling distinguishes between hair that is an adornment (woman) and hair that is not (man).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith of Umm Salama where the Prophet (PBUH) allowed her to pour three handfuls without undoing her braids.
The hadith is a Sunnah ahad, zanni al-thubut but qati al-dalala for the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith proves the ruling: the explicit permission indicates that undoing is not obligatory if water reaches the roots.
The imperative 'yazimuhu' for men is derived from general principles (no hardship), while the hadith provides an exception for women.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the differing view: some scholars (possibly Shafi'i) require water to reach inside women's locks, but the Hanafi correct view is that it is not obligatory.
The passage uses 'ala al-sahih' to indicate the preferred view.
5
Apply the ruling to practice
List the obligatory parts: skin and hair of beard, mustache, eyebrows, external private part. For women, undoing is not fard if water reaches roots; for men, undoing is fard even if water penetrates.
The price of water and bathhouse fee fall on the husband.

(The hair of a man): It is necessary for him to undo it (absolutely) according to the correct view, whether water penetrates its roots or not, because it is not an adornment for him, so there is no hardship in it. And it is not obligatory for a woman to undo her braided hair if water penetrates its roots, by agreement, due to the hadith of Umm Salama (may Allah be pleased with her) that she said, 'O Messenger of Allah, I am a woman who braids my hair tightly; shall I undo it for ghusl from janabah?' Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Maraqi al-Falah regarding the rulings of washing hair and other parts in ghusl. It begins with the ruling for a man's hair: it is obligatory to undo any braids or knots absolutely, regardless of whether water reaches the roots, because a man's hair is not considered adornment, so no hardship exists. For a woman, it is not obligatory to undo braided hair if water reaches the roots, based on the hadith of Umm Salama where the Prophet (peace be upon him) instructed her to pour three handfuls of water over her head without undoing her braids. However, if her hair is matted or thick such that water cannot reach the roots, undoing becomes necessary. Furthermore, for a woman, it is not obligatory (according to the correct view) to make water reach the inside of her locks (the braided strands), unlike a man for whom it is obligatory to wet all locks entirely. The commentary then defines technical terms: al-dafira is a lock of hair, al-dafr is the act of twisting and interweaving hair, and al-‘aqs is gathering hair on the head. Next, it discusses the price of water for ghusl and wudu: it is the husband's responsibility even if the wife is wealthy, because water is essential. Regarding menstruation cessation: if it stops before ten days, the husband must provide water for ghusl because he needs intimacy; if at ten days, the wife bears the cost because she needs to pray. The fee for a bathhouse, if needed, is also on the husband. The commentary notes that going to the bathhouse is permissible for women but with conditions: no adornment and no change of appearance that invites male attention, and no looking at others' awrah. If washing her head harms her, she may omit it, but she cannot refuse her husband intimacy. The passage then lists obligatory washes: the skin and hair of the beard (even if thick), the skin of the mustache, the eyebrows, and the external private part (because it is like the mouth; the internal is like the throat and not obligatory to wash). This is based on Quran 5:6 ('So purify yourselves').

Key Terms
الضفيرة (al-ḍafīrah): A lock of hair, braid, or forelock; specifically the gathered hair on the head.
الضفر (al-ḍafr): The act of twisting and interweaving strands of hair together.
العقص (al-‘aqṣ): Gathering hair upon the head (e.g., in a bun).
الذؤابة (al-dhu’ābah): The forelock or topknot of hair; also used for a horse's forelock.
بشرة اللحية (basharah al-liḥyah): The skin of the beard, which must be washed in ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a woman need to undo her braided hair for ghusl?
A) Always, regardless of water penetration
B) Only if her hair is matted or thick and prevents water from reaching the roots
C) Only if she is in a state of janabah
D) It is never necessary for a woman to undo her hair

Key Takeaway
In ghusl, a man must undo all hair; a woman need not undo braids if water reaches roots, but must if hair is matted; and washing the beard, mustache, eyebrows, and external private parts is obligatory.

The Twelve Sunan (Recommended Acts) of Ghusl
List and explanation of the twelve recommended acts in ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the twelve sunan of ghusl according to the Hanafi school and distinguish between those that are sunnah and those that are obligatory.

Core Idea
In ghusl, twelve acts are recommended; their omission does not invalidate the ghusl but forfeits additional reward.

This lesson examines the sunan (recommended acts) of ghusl as explained by al-Tahtawi. While the fard (obligatory) acts of ghusl are minimal—rinsing the mouth, nose, and washing the entire body—the sunan add completeness and follow the Prophet’s practice. The passage lists twelve such acts and addresses minor intra-Hanafi differences over wiping the head and delaying the feet.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal status of these acts
The passage explicitly calls these acts 'sunan' (recommended). Missing them does not invalidate ghusl, but loses reward. Note that they are not fard or wajib.
2
Enumerate the first five sunan
The passage mentions: (1) commencing with basmalah, (2) intention (niyyah), (3) washing hands up to the wrists, (4) washing any impurity on the body separately, (5) washing the private parts. Each is supported by hadith or reason.
Intention is sunnah to voice; the underlying intention is fard.
3
Examine the wudu before ghusl
The passage says to perform a complete wudu like for prayer, including triple washing and wiping the head. There is a difference: some say no wipe because water is poured, but the preferred view (al-awwal asahh) is to wipe, based on the hadith of Maymunah.
This applies to the wudu performed before the main ghusl.
4
Analyze the delay of washing the feet
The passage mentions delaying washing the feet if standing in a place where water collects, to avoid washing them in dirty water. There is ikhtilaf: some scholars (mashayikh) reject the delay based on Aisha's narration. The text does not clearly prefer one view.
The delay is a sunnah, not obligatory.

Chapter on the sunan (recommended acts) of ghusl: Twelve things are recommended in ghusl: First: commencing with the basmalah (saying 'Bismillah') due to the general hadith 'Every matter of importance...' And commencing with intention (niyyah) so that his action is an act of worship for which he is rewarded, like in wudu. And commencing with the basmalah accompanies the intention because basmalah is related to the tongue and intention to the heart. And both are done along with washing the hands up to the wrists at the beginning, following the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him). And it is recommended to wash any impurity that may be on his body separately at the beginning, so that he is assured of its removal before it spreads over his body. And likewise, washing his private parts, even if there is no impurity on them, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) did, so that he is assured that water reaches the part that contracts when standing and expands when sitting. Then he performs wudu like his wudu for prayer, washing each part three times and wiping the head according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). It is also said that he does not wipe it because he pours water over it, but the first is more correct because he (peace be upon him) performed wudu before ghusl like his wudu for prayer, and wudu includes washing and wiping. But he delays washing his feet if he is standing during ghusl in a place where water collects...
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section (fasl) on the sunan (recommended acts) of ghusl. Twelve sunan are listed: (1) starting with basmalah, (2) making intention (niyyah), (3) doing both along with washing the hands up to the wrists at the beginning (following the Prophet's practice), (4) washing any impurity on the body separately at the start to prevent spreading, (5) washing the private parts even if no impurity, (6) then performing a complete wudu like for prayer (washing each part three times, wiping the head according to the apparent narration; there is a difference of opinion whether to wipe the head since water is poured over it, but the first is more correct due to hadith evidence), (7) delaying washing the feet if standing in a place where water collects (there is ikhtilaf among mashayikh on this: some say not to delay because Aisha's narration did not mention delay). The commentary elaborates: the basmalah and intention are as in wudu; the basmalah is on the tongue but the intention is in the heart, yet the basmalah also involves the heart because the one who remembers must be conscious of the meaning. The hadith of Maymunah is cited: the Prophet (peace be upon him) washed his hands, then poured water on his private parts and washed them with his left hand, then rubbed his left hand on the ground, then performed wudu like for prayer, then poured three handfuls on his head, then washed the rest of his body, then moved and washed his feet. This shows the order. The delay of feet is based on this hadith, but some scholars argue against delay due to Aisha's narration. The relied-upon view is not explicitly given here, but the text mentions the ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
سنة (sunnah): A recommended act; if performed, reward is given; if omitted, no sin.
التسمية (al-tasmīyah): Saying 'Bismillah' (in the name of Allah) at the beginning of an act.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention in the heart to perform the act of worship for the sake of Allah.
الغسل (al-ghusl): The full-body ritual washing to remove major impurity (janabah, hayd, nifas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT among the sunan of ghusl?
A) Washing the hands up to the wrists at the beginning
B) Washing any impurity on the body separately
C) Washing the private parts
D) Washing the feet three times at the end of ghusl

Key Takeaway
The twelve sunan of ghusl are recommended acts that complete the ghusl; their omission does not break the validity of the ghusl but forfeits the full reward.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
The Twelve Sunnahs of Ghusl and Their Evidence
Detailed enumeration and commentary on the twelve sunnahs of ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the twelve sunnahs of ghusl, identify the primary evidence from the Sunnah, and explain the Hanafi tarjih on the disputed points of delaying foot washing and dalk.

Core Idea
The Prophet's ghusl practice defines twelve sunnahs—omitting a sunnah leaves the act valid yet incomplete.

Ghusl is a purifying ritual bath required after major impurity. While the fard (obligatory) elements are minimal—rinsing the mouth, nose, and washing the entire body—the Hanafi school enumerates twelve sunnahs that perfect the act. These sunnahs are derived from the detailed hadith of Maimuna (RA). This lesson walks through each sunnah and addresses two key intra-Hanafi disputes: whether to delay washing the feet and whether rubbing (dalk) is mandatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their ruling
The source lists twelve acts. Each is classified as sunnah muakkadah (emphasized recommended). The ruling level is sunnah, not fard or wajib.
Sunnah muakkadah means the act was regularly performed by the Prophet; omitting it is discouraged but does not invalidate ghusl.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The main evidence is the hadith of Maimuna (RA), narrated by al-Jama'ah (Bukhari, Muslim, etc.). It describes the Prophet's ghusl in detail.
The hadith is ahad (single-narrator), so its thubut is zanni, but its legal force as proof for sunnah is accepted.
3
Analyze the evidence for each sunnah
From the hadith, we derive the twelve sunnahs: tasmiyah, niyyah, washing hands, removing impurity first, washing private parts, performing wudu (including triple washing head and wiping), delaying feet, pouring water thrice, starting with head, right shoulder then left, and dalk.
The hadith explicitly mentions many of these; others (like tasmiyah) are supported by general hadith.
4
Examine the ikhtilaf on delaying foot washing
Some scholars (citing Aisha's hadith) do not delay, but the majority (including Abu Hanifa) prefer to delay based on Maimuna's hadith. The mufta bihi is to delay.
The hadith of Maimuna explicitly states: 'then he stepped aside and washed his feet.'
5
Examine the ikhtilaf on dalk (rubbing)
Dalk is sunnah according to Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn; only a rare narration from Abu Yusuf considers it wajib. The relied-upon position is sunnah.
The Quranic command 'tatahharu' is interpreted generally, not requiring rubbing.

Chapter: Book of Purity. Section: On the Sunnahs of Ghusl. It is Sunnah in ghusl twelve things: 1. Beginning with the tasmiyah (saying Bismillah) due to the generality of the hadith 'Every matter of importance...' 2. Beginning with the intention so that his action becomes an act of worship for which he is rewarded, like wudu; and beginning with the tasmiyah accompanies the intention because tasmiyah is on the tongue and intention is in the heart. 3. They (tasmiyah and intention) are performed with washing the hands up to the wrists at the start, as was the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him). 4. It is Sunnah to wash any impurity on his body separately at the beginning so that he is assured of its removal before it spreads over his body. 5. Likewise, washing his private parts even if there is no impurity, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) did, to be assured of water reaching the part that contracts when standing and expands when sitting. 6. Then he performs wudu like his wudu for prayer, and he triple-washes the head and wipes over the head in the apparent narration; it is said he does not wipe it because he pours water over it, but the first is more correct because the Prophet (peace be upon him) performed wudu before ghusl like his wudu for prayer, and wudu includes washing and wiping. 7. But he delays washing the feet if he is standing while performing ghusl in a place where water collects, because he needs to wash them a second time from the used water. 8. Then he pours water over his body three times, covering the entire body with each pour, and this is Sunnah due to the hadith. 9. If the person performing ghusl immerses himself in flowing water, or immerses in what is like flowing water, such as a large body of water (like ten by ten), and remains immersed for the duration of wudu and ghusl, or similarly in rain, even if only for wudu, then he has completed the Sunnah because this achieves the thoroughness like triple washing. 10. He begins in the state of pouring water over his head, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) did. 11. After that (the head), he washes his right shoulder then his left, due to the recommendation of starting with the right; this is the saying of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani. 12. And that he rubs all parts of his body in the first wash so that water covers his body in the last two washes; and rubbing is not obligatory in ghusl except in a narration from Abu Yusuf due to the specific wording 'purify yourselves' in contrast to wudu because it uses the word 'wash'. Then the commentary: 'The beginning with tasmiyah...' [The translation continues for the entire hashiya, but for brevity, the key points are covered.]
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal ruling from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi on the Maraqi al-Falah, enumerating the twelve sunnahs of ghusl (ritual bath). It combines the matn (base text) with the hashiya (super-commentary). The author first lists the sunnahs: beginning with tasmiyah and niyyah, washing hands, removing impurities separately, washing private parts, performing wudu-like for prayer, triple-washing the head, delaying washing the feet (if standing in water), pouring water thrice over the body (or immersion as substitute), starting with the head, then right then left shoulder, and finally dalk (rubbing) the body. The commentary clarifies each: e.g., tasmiyah is like in wudu; intention should accompany; washing hands before inserting into vessel; removing impurity before washing; private parts include both front and back; wudu includes all its sunan and faraid; the hadith of Maimuna (RA) is cited for the practice of the Prophet (PBUH). For the issue of delaying foot washing, there is a dispute: some (based on Aisha's hadith) do not delay, but the preferred view is to delay based on Maimuna's hadith. The commentary also discusses tathleeth (triple washing) and its conditions, immersion in large water or rain as fulfilling the sunnah, and the recommendation of dalk (rubbing) which is not obligatory except in a rare narration from Abu Yusuf. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah as it relies on well-known hadith and the core Hanafi texts.

Key Terms
التثليث (al-tathleeth): Triple washing; pouring water over the body three times, each time covering the entire body.
الدلك (al-dalk): Rubbing of the body with the hand while washing to ensure water reaches all parts.
الغسالة (al-ghusalah): The used water that has been used for washing and has dripped off the body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is the ruling on dalk (rubbing) in ghusl?
A) It is obligatory (fard) based on the Quranic command 'tatahharu'.
B) It is sunnah, not wajib, except in a narration from Abu Yusuf.
C) It is makruh to do dalk because it resembles innovations.
D) It is only recommended for those with impurities.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers the twelve acts of ghusl as sunnah muakkadah, with rubbing (dalk) being sunnah not wajib, and delaying foot washing is preferred when standing in accumulating water.

The Etiquettes of Ghusl: The Exception of Facing Qibla
Rulings on the adab of ghusl, stating they are like those of wudu except facing qibla.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the etiquettes (adab) of ghusl and identify the one exception regarding facing the qibla, along with the reason.

Core Idea
Ghusl's adab mirror wudu's, but never face the qibla while bathing due to the state of exposure.

This brief passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the recommended manners for ghusl. While most adab are identical to those of wudu, there is a critical exception: one should not face the qibla during ghusl. Let's explore why and how this ruling applies.

Guided Path
1
Recall the adab of wudu
List the sunnan acts for wudu (e.g., saying Bismillah, washing hands thrice before starting, rinsing mouth, etc.).
2
Identify the scope of the rule
The text says the adab of ghusl are like those of wudu. So all wudu adab apply to ghusl.
3
Spot the exception
The exception is that one should not face the qibla while performing ghusl.
The Arabic phrase: إلا أنه لا يستقبل القبلة حال اغتساله
4
Understand the rationale
The reason is that during ghusl, one is typically naked or partially uncovered, and facing the qibla in such a state is considered disrespectful (makruh tanzihi).
لأنه يكون معه (meaning in a state of exposure)
5
Apply the ruling
When performing ghusl, turn away from the qibla; if you need to make du'a, do so without facing it. The exception holds even for supplication.
The text adds 'بل ولو دعاء' indicating the ruling remains even during du'a.

Commentary
This passage is a brief ruling on the adab (recommended manners) of ghusl. It states that the adab of ghusl are identical to those of wudu, which were explained earlier in the text. The only exception is that facing the qibla is not recommended during ghusl because the person is typically naked or partially uncovered, and facing the qibla in such a state is discouraged. The commentary adds that even if one is making supplication (du'a), the rule holds—meaning one should not face the qibla while uttering du'a during ghusl. The text is incomplete, so further details are missing.

Key Terms
الآداب (al-adab): The recommended manners and etiquettes to be observed during the performance of ghusl.

Key Takeaway
The adab of ghusl equal those of wudu, except that facing the qibla is not recommended due to the state of exposure.

Taharah — Purification

Covering 'Awrah During Ghusl: Rulings and Etiquette
Ruling with proof on covering 'awrah during ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on covering the 'awrah during ghusl, apply the hadith proof, and identify the recommended and disliked actions associated with the bath.

Core Idea
Covering 'awrah during ghusl is recommended (mandub), not obligatory; unnecessary uncovering is makruh tanzih.

Ghusl is a ritual bath required after major impurity. Beyond its validity, there are etiquettes (adab) that enhance the act. The most significant of these is covering the 'awrah, which the Hanafi school considers highly recommended but not a condition of validity. This lesson explores the textual evidence, the scope of the ruling, and related practices such as speaking and supplication during ghusl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage addresses the etiquettes of ghusl. The central ruling: it is recommended (mandub) to cover the 'awrah during ghusl; uncovering unnecessarily is disliked (makruh tanzih). The text states 'if it is covered, there is no harm' and 'it is disliked to uncover the 'awrah'.
The ruling applies to both men and women, but the definition of 'awrah differs.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Abu Dawud: 'Indeed, Allah is Concealing, loves modesty and concealment; so when one of you bathes, let him conceal himself.' This is the primary proof for the recommendation.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), consistent with its use for a recommended ruling.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'falyastatir' (let him conceal) in Hanafi usul generally indicates obligation, but here it is understood to imply recommendation (istihbab) because the context shows that covering is not a condition for validity. Other evidence (like the permissibility of bathing without covering in extreme need) overrides the default obligation.
This exemplifies the Hanafi principle that a command can be diverted to recommendation when there is evidence of non-obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf about undressing in seclusion
The text reports a difference of opinion on whether one may undress completely in seclusion: some say it is sinful, others permissible for a short time, and some say no harm. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it is makruh tanzih.
The author does not explicitly give tarjih, but the first statement in the passage ('if covered, no harm' and 'yukrah') establishes the preferred view.
5
Apply the ruling to related cases
The text extends the ruling to women: she delays her ghusl among men; if she fears missing prayer, she performs tayammum and prays, but must repeat the prayer later. Also, speaking (including supplication) is disliked only when the 'awrah is uncovered; if covered, it is permissible.
The practical takeaway: always conceal as much as possible during ghusl, and avoid speaking when uncovered.

Regarding uncovering the 'awrah: if it is covered, there is no harm. It is recommended that one not speak any words while bathing, even supplications, because it is a place of filth. It is disliked to uncover the 'awrah. It is recommended to bathe in a place where no one can see him, for it is not permissible for anyone to look at his 'awrah due to the possibility of it becoming exposed during the state of washing or dressing, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Indeed, Allah is Concealing, loves modesty and concealment; so when one of you bathes, let him conceal himself.' Reported by Abu Dawud. If he finds no screen among men, he bathes and chooses what is more concealing. The woman among women is likewise, but among men she delays her bath. The sin is on the looker, not on the one who uncovers his garment to purify himself. It is said that one may undress to bathe alone, and he may undress his wife for intercourse if the house is small, about ten cubits. It is recommended to pray two rak'ahs as a supererogatory prayer after it, like after wudu, because it (ghusl) includes it. Disliked in ghusl is what is disliked in wudu, and additionally disliked is supplication, as previously mentioned. There is no fixed measure for the water used for purification in ghusl and wudu due to the differing conditions of people; one should observe a moderate amount, without extravagance or stinginess. Supplication: As for speech other than supplication, it is disliked while uncovering, as in the Sharh. As for supplication, it is due to what the author mentioned. His statement: 'It is disliked with uncovering of the 'awrah even if in a place where no one sees him.' His statement: 'It is recommended that he bathe' i.e., while his 'awrah is covered, as evidenced by his statement 'due to the possibility of its exposure' etc., and by what preceded. His statement: 'Indeed, Allah is Hayy' i.e., free from imperfections. His statement: 'He bathes and chooses what is more concealing' is what is in al-Wahbaniyyah and al-Qunyah; what is in Ibn Amir Hajj is that he delays it to be able to bathe without being seen. This applies equally to men and women, no difference whether they are among men or women. If he fears the time will expire, he performs tayammum and prays. Apparently, he must repeat (the prayer) because many scholars say: if the excuse for tayammum is due to a human factor, the repetition is not waived, even if tayammum was allowed. His statement: 'And among men she delays her bath' and likewise among men and women, and she should perform tayammum and pray due to her legal inability to access water, as in al-Durr. His statement: 'The sin is on the looker' i.e., if he is deliberate in a situation where uncovering the 'awrah is permissible. His statement: 'It is said that one may undress to bathe alone'—know that in al-Qunyah there is a difference of opinion regarding the permissibility of uncovering in seclusion. He said: undressing in a small bathroom, shortening his garment, or shaving his pubic area is sinful; it is said it is permissible for a short time; it is said there is no harm; it is said one may undress etc., up to what the author mentioned. His statement: 'About ten cubits' and in the Sharh 'five cubits.' Consider the basis of this specification; perhaps the basis for the first is that ten is considered large, as they estimated for water, so that the place, if of this size, is spacious—Allah knows best. His statement: 'Like wudu' rather ghusl is superior because it is wudu and more, and to that he alluded by his saying 'it includes it.'
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah explains the etiquettes of ghusl (ritual bath) with a focus on covering the 'awrah. The author begins by stating that if one's 'awrah is covered during ghusl, there is no harm; it is generally required to cover it, and doing so is recommended. It is also recommended to avoid speaking, even supplications, because the bathroom is a place of filth. However, speaking without supplication is disliked only when the 'awrah is exposed, as noted in the Sharh. The text then cites a hadith from Abu Dawud emphasizing concealment. For men, if no screen is available, one should bathe but choose the most concealing spot. For women among other women, the same rule applies, but among men, she should delay her bath until she can bathe in privacy. The sin for looking falls on the one who intentionally looks, not on the one who uncovers out of necessity for purification. The text also discusses a difference of opinion about undressing in seclusion. Some say it is permissible only for a short time, while others say it is sinful to uncover even in a small bathroom for shaving or similar acts. The recommended size of a private room is ten cubits according to one opinion, five according to another, with the rationale that a larger space provides better concealment. After ghusl, it is recommended to pray two rak'ahs, as after wudu, and the same rulings of disliked actions apply, with the addition that supplication is disliked during ghusl. There is no fixed quantity of water for purification; one should use a moderate amount without extravagance or stinginess. The commentary also addresses the case of a woman among men: if she fears missing the prayer, she should perform tayammum and pray, but must repeat the prayer later, as the excuse (lack of privacy) is due to a human factor.

Key Terms
عورة ('awrah): Parts of the body that must be covered in front of others; for men, navel to knee; for women, the entire body except face and hands.
ستير (sattir): One who conceals faults; a name of Allah emphasizing His attribute of concealment.
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution using pure earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling regarding supplications (du'a) during ghusl while the 'awrah is covered?
A) It is wajib (obligatory).
B) It is mustahabb (recommended).
C) It is makruh (disliked) even if the 'awrah is covered.
D) It is permissible and recommended to recite du'a.

Key Takeaway
Covering the 'awrah during ghusl is a highly recommended practice (mandub), supported by a clear hadith; unnecessary uncovering is disliked (makruh tanzih) but does not invalidate the ghusl.

Recommended Baths (Ghusl Masnun) for Friday, 'Eid, Ihram, and 'Arafah
Specification of four occasions for which ghusl is recommended (masnun) and related intra-madhhab disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four occasions for which ghusl is sunnah in the Hanafi school and explain the practical difference between Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's views on Friday ghusl.

Core Idea
Friday ghusl is sunnah for the prayer, not the day—timing and purity continuity matter for its full reward.

This lesson examines the four recommended baths (ghusl masnun) in the Hanafi school: for Friday, the two 'Eids, entering ihram, and standing at 'Arafah. We will also explore an internal Hanafi dispute regarding the correct time and purpose of Friday ghusl, and how this affects its reward.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four occasions and their ruling
List the four occasions for which ghusl is recommended (masnun) according to the Hanafi school. Note that the ruling is sunnah mu'akkadah, not wajib.
The text states: 'It is sunnah to bathe for four things.'
2
Examine the evidence for Friday ghusl
Analyze the hadith cited: 'Whoever among you comes to Friday, let him bathe.' Consider its status as evidence and how it is interpreted.
Hanafis use this hadith to show the link between ghusl and attending the prayer.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Identify the two positions: Abu Yusuf holds that Friday ghusl is sunnah for the prayer itself, while Muhammad and al-Hasan hold it is for the day of Friday.
This dispute affects the reward if a person bathes early then has a hadath.
4
Trace the practical consequence (thamarah)
Work through the scenario: if one bathes early on Friday, then has a minor hadath, then makes wudu before prayer. According to each view, does he attain the virtue of the sunnah ghusl?
Abu Yusuf: no virtue because the ghusl is for the prayer and the purity was broken. Muhammad: virtue remains because the ghusl is for the day.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The author states the correct opinion ('ala al-sahih') is that of Abu Yusuf. This is the relied-upon view for fatwa.
The tarjih is based on the hadith and the reasoning that ghusl is linked to attending the prayer.
