قوله : (سنة للصلاة في قول أبي يوسف ولليوم عند الحسن نقله القهستاني عن التحفة. قوله للحج أو العمرة) أو مانعة خلو تجوز الجمع . قوله : (ولهذا لا يتيمم) أي لا يتيمم مكانه بفقد الماء لأن غسل الإحرام للتنظيف لا للتطهير، فلا يسقط بالتيمم .
Section: It is sunnah to bathe for four things. According to the correct (opinion), because they are better than the time. And it is said it is for the day. Its consequence: if one has an event (nullifies wudu) after his bath, then performs wudu, he does not have its virtue (the virtue of the sunnah bath) according to the correct opinion, and he has the virtue according to the weak opinion. And in Mi'raj al-Diraya: if on Thursday or Friday night, he uses the siwak with respect to achieving the objective, which is removing odor. And from them (the four) is the prayer of the two 'Eids, because the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, used to bathe on the day of Fitr, Adha, and 'Arafah. And he said, peace be upon him: 'Whoever performs wudu on Friday, it is well and good, and whoever bathes, the bath is better.' And it is abrogating the apparent meaning of his saying: 'Friday bath is obligatory on every pubescent person.' And the bath is sunnah for the prayer according to the saying of Abu Yusuf, as in Friday. And it is sunnah for ihram for Hajj or 'Umrah due to his practice, and it is for cleanliness, not for purification. So a woman bathes even if she is menstruating or postpartum. And for this reason, one does not perform tayammum in its place due to lack of water. And it is sunnah for the pilgrim (at 'Arafah), not for others, and the pilgrim does it at 'Arafah. No, no tayammum. His statement: 'According to the correct opinion' is the saying of Abu Yusuf, and what is in the two Sahihs supports it: 'Whoever among you comes to Friday, let him bathe.' And in a narration of Ibn Hibban: 'Whoever comes to Friday from men and women, let him bathe.' And in a narration of al-Bayhaqi: 'And whoever does not come to it, there is no bath upon him.' End. His statement: 'And it is said it is for the day' is said by Muhammad to show its virtue over other days, due to his saying: 'The master of days is Friday.' And many attribute it to al-Hasan. And it is mentioned in al-Muhit that Muhammad is with al-Hasan. And in Ghayat al-Bayan from Sharh al-Tahawi that it is for both of them according to Abu Yusuf. His statement: 'Its consequence etc.' appears also for those upon whom Friday is not obligatory. As for the bath after the prayer, it is not considered (valid) by consensus, as in the Friday section of al-Muhit and al-Khaniyah. His statement: 'Uses siwak' – with the sunnah to achieve the objective. And he said in al-Nahr like al-Bahr: it seems that the sunnah is not achieved by this (if there is a delay) according to agreement. As for the view of Abu Yusuf, because the prayer is conditional upon it, and generally there occurs an event (hadath) between them in such a period of time. As for the view of al-Hasan, because one must be in a state of purity with the purification of the bath on the day, not before, and generally there occurs an event also. End, summarized. His statement: 'So it is well and good' – i.e., he has taken the sunnah, and how excellent this characteristic is. So the pronoun refers to something not mentioned, and this is permissible in well-known usage, as in His statement, Exalted, 'Until she concealed herself with the veil' [Quran 38:32]. His statement: 'It abrogates the apparent meaning of his saying etc.' It is also said: the meaning of 'obligatory' is 'emphatically recommended,' as one says, 'your right upon me is obligatory.' His statement: 'Sunnah for the prayer according to the saying of Abu Yusuf, and for the day according to al-Hasan' – al-Qahastani transmitted it from al-Tuhfah. His statement: 'for Hajj or 'Umrah' – 'or' is for exemption of conjunction, allowing the combination. His statement: 'And for this reason one does not perform tayammum' – i.e., one does not perform tayammum in its place due to lack of water, because the bath for ihram is for cleanliness, not for purification, so it is not dropped by tayammum.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya discusses the four occasions for which ghusl is sunnah (mustahabb). The main ruling: it is sunnah to bathe for Friday prayer, for the two 'Eid prayers, for entering the state of ihram for Hajj or 'Umrah, and for standing at 'Arafah on the day of 'Arafah. The text then explains an internal Hanafi dispute regarding Friday ghusl: is it sunnah for the Friday prayer itself (Abu Yusuf's view) or for the day of Friday (Muhammad and al-Hasan's view)? The correct opinion (al-sahih) according to the author is that of Abu Yusuf, based on hadith that command ghusl for those attending Friday prayer. The practical consequence of this difference is that if a person bathes early on Friday, then nullifies wudu, and then performs wudu before prayer, according to Abu Yusuf he does not attain the virtue of the sunnah bath because he had an event (hadath) that broke the ghusl's effect (since the ghusl is for the prayer and requires continuity). According to the other view, he would still have the virtue because the bath is for the day, not the prayer. The text also notes that ghusl for ihram is for cleanliness (tanzif) not for ritual purification (tatheer), so a menstruating woman may perform it, and if water is unavailable, tayammum is not performed because it is not a substitute for this type of ghusl. For 'Arafah, only the pilgrim (hajj) should bathe, not others. The commentary cites various sources (Sahihayn, Ibn Hibban, al-Bayhaqi) to support the sunnah status of Friday ghusl and clarifies that the word 'wajib' in some narrations means 'emphasized sunnah' (mu'akkad) not obligatory, and that this is an abrogation or interpretation.

Key Terms
غسل مسنون (ghusl masnun): A ritual bath that is recommended (sunnah) on specific occasions, not obligatory but highly encouraged.
تنظيف (tanzif): Cleaning for purposes of hygiene and adornment, as opposed to tatheer (ritual purification that removes hadath).
ناسخ (nasikh): Abrogating; the hadith that indicates Friday ghusl is sunnah abrogates the earlier command that implied obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, the sunnah ghusl for Friday is for:
A) The entire day of Friday
B) The Friday prayer
C) The morning of Friday
D) Both the day and the prayer

Key Takeaway
The four sunnah ghusls (Friday, 'Eid, Ihram, 'Arafah) are recommended, but the reward for Friday ghusl depends on its link to the prayer according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
Friday Ghusl: Sunnah Muakkadah for the Prayer
Ruling with proof on Friday ghusl, including intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Friday ghusl is sunnah muakkadah (not wajib) in the Hanafi school, identify the mufta bihi position, and state the practical consequence of the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Friday ghusl is sunnah for the prayer, not the day; its reward is lost if an event occurs before prayer.

Many Muslims assume Friday ghusl is obligatory, but the Hanafi school classifies it as a confirmed sunnah (sunnah muakkadah). The classical texts reveal a subtle intra-madhhab disagreement: does this ghusl belong to the day or to the prayer? The answer determines whether its reward survives if you break wudu before the prayer. This lesson unpacks the proof from hadith, the abrogation of the apparent command, and the authoritative view that settles the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states it is sunnah to perform ghusl for four things, starting with Friday. Note that the ruling is 'sunnah' not 'wajib'. Confirm that in Hanafi terminology, sunnah muakkadah is a confirmed but non-obligatory act.
Contrast with other schools that may call it mustahab.
2
Analyze the proof for Friday ghusl
The text cites the hadith: 'Whoever among you comes to Friday, let him perform ghusl.' Recognize that the imperative form (amr) normally indicates obligation in Hanafi usul, but here it is overridden by another hadith: 'Whoever performs wudu on Friday has done well, and whoever performs ghusl is better.' This second hadith shows that wudu is sufficient, proving ghusl is not obligatory.
The concept of abrogation (naskh) is applied to the apparent meaning of the first hadith.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
The text reports that according to Abu Yusuf, the ghusl is sunnah for the prayer, while according to Muhammad and al-Hasan, it is for the day itself. Identify each position and its basis: Abu Yusuf relies on the phrase 'comes to Friday' (i.e., for the prayer), while Muhammad cites the virtue of Friday as a day.
This dispute is within the Hanafi school, not between schools.
4
Examine the praktical consequence (thamarah)
The consequence appears when a person performs ghusl early on Friday, then experiences an event (breaks wudu or becomes junub) before the prayer, then performs wudu and attends the prayer. According to Abu Yusuf, the reward of the sunnah ghusl is lost because the ghusl was tied to the prayer and the event nullified it. According to Muhammad, the reward remains because the ghusl was for the day. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) view is that of Abu Yusuf.
This practical difference is the key outcome of the dispute.
5
Confirm the correct view and its evidence
The text endorses Abu Yusuf's view as 'al-sahih' (the correct one), supported by the explicit hadith that connects ghusl to attending the prayer, and by the consensus that ghusl after the prayer is not considered (by consensus). Note that the author also mentions that the hadith about obligation is abrogated.
The tarjih mechanism: the evidence is stronger for the prayer-based view.

Source Text

His saying: 'And it is said that it is for the day' was said by Muhammad to show its virtue over other days due to his saying: 'The master of days is Friday.' Many attributed it to al-Hasan. It is mentioned in al-Muhit that Muhammad (is) with al-Hasan, and in Ghayat al-Bayan from Sharh al-Tahawi that it is for both of them according to Abu Yusuf. It is said in al-Nahr like al-Bahr: it is appropriate that the sunnah is not attained by this in agreement. According to Abu Yusuf, because prayer is required and generally there is an event between them in such a duration, and according to al-Hasan, because he conditions that one be in a state of purification from the ghusl of the day and not before, and generally there is also an event. The pronoun refers to an unmentioned thing, which is permissible in the well-known usage as in His saying: 'until it is hidden by the veil.' His saying: 'for Hajj or Umrah' with 'or' for permissibility of combining. Similarly are all the sunnah and mandub ghusls. His saying: 'For the virtue of the time of standing' and to be closer to it, thus more effective in achieving the goal, as they said regarding Friday ghusl: it is better to be close to the time of going to it. However, this indicates only preference, not a condition for obtaining the sunnah. Al-Hidayah said: That these ghusls are sunnah is the most correct view, and it is said they are mustahab, evidenced by the fact that Muhammad called Friday ghusl in al-Asl 'good'. Al-Fath said: 'And that is the view.'
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi comments on the matn of Maraqi al-Falah regarding the four sunnah ghusls. It begins by stating the correct view (al-sahih) that ghusl is sunnah for Friday, citing hadith from the Sahihain. It then discusses a difference of opinion: whether the ghusl is for the day itself (for its virtue) or for the prayer. The consequence (thamarah) is seen in rulings for those not obligated to pray Jumu'ah. The passage also covers ghusl for Eid, citing the Prophet's practice. For Ihram, it notes that the ghusl is for cleanliness, not purification, so a menstruating woman may perform it. The final point is about ghusl for the pilgrim at Arafah, advising it after zawal to coincide with the time of standing (wuquf). The commentary then references al-Hidayah and al-Fath regarding whether these are sunnah or mustahab, concluding that the relied-upon view is that they are sunnah. It also explains that tayammum cannot substitute for this ghusl because it is for cleanliness rather than ritual purification, unlike purifying ghusl from janabah. The passage includes a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds the ghusl is for the prayer, while Muhammad and al-Hasan hold it is for the day itself. The correct view (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Yusuf, supported by hadith showing the ghusl is connected to attending the prayer.

Key Terms
السنن (al-sunan): Plural of sunnah; here refers to the four recommended ghusls that are classified as sunnah (not fard).
الاغتسال (al-ightisal): The act of washing the entire body with water; here it is a recommended (sunnah) act for specific occasions.
ثمرة (thamarah): The practical consequence or result of a legal difference; here it refers to how the difference of opinion about whether the ghusl is for the day or the prayer affects the ruling for someone who has an event after ghusl.
ناسخ (nasikh): An abrogating proof; here the hadith allowing wudu to suffice abrogates the apparent command for ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school, for what is the Friday ghusl sunnah?
A) For the day of Friday itself
B) For the Friday prayer
C) For the entire week
D) For both the day and the prayer

Key Takeaway
Friday ghusl is sunnah muakkadah for the prayer per the relied-upon Hanafi position; if an event occurs after the ghusl but before the prayer, the reward of the sunnah is lost.

Recommended (Mandub) Ghusls: Details and Proofs
Commentary on the list of sixteen recommended ghusls, with details on each

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling (mandub/fard) for each of the recommended ghusls and identify the conditions that change the ruling.

Core Idea
Most of the sixteen ghusls are mandub, but they become fard if the person is impure at the time of the occasion.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi explains the sixteen occasions for which ghusl is recommended (mandub) in the Hanafi school. While the default ruling is mandub, certain circumstances—such as being in a state of impurity when converting to Islam—make ghusl obligatory. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify each occasion and its default ruling
List the occasions: conversion, puberty by age, recovery from insanity/intoxication/fainting, cupping, washing dead body, night of Bara'ah, night of Qadr. Default ruling is mandub.
The passage mentions sixteen, but not all are given in detail.
2
Recognize the exceptions that change the ruling to fard
For conversion: if impure (janabah, menses, postpartum), ghusl becomes fard. For puberty: if signs appear, ghusl becomes fard.
The text explicitly states 'it is obligatory upon him' for impure convert.
3
Examine the dalil for cupping ghusl
The hadith from Abu Dawud shows the Prophet performed ghusl after cupping; this indicates recommendation but no command.
The hadith is ahad, so it is zanni al-thubut.
4
Understand the ikhtilaf regarding cupping and washing dead
Some scholars consider these ghusls wajib; Hanafis say mandub to exit disagreement.
The commentator says 'to exit from the disagreement'.
5
Apply the rule: Determine if ghusl is mandub or fard based on the individual's state
Check if the person is in a state of major impurity at the time of the occasion. If yes, fard; otherwise, mandub.
For puberty, if signs appear before 15, ghusl is fard regardless of age.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لمن أسلم طاهراً بذلك أمر من أسلم واحترز به عمن أسلم غير طاهر فإنه يفترض عليه الغسل على المعتمد كما تقدم. قوله : ولمن بلغ (بالسن) احترز به عن بلوغ الصبي بالإحتلام والإحبال والإنزال وعن بلوغ الصبية بالإحتلام ،والحيض، والحبل فإنه لا بد من الغسل فيها . قوله: (وهو خمس عشرة سنة على المفتي (به وهو قولهما ورواية عن الإمام إذ العلامة تظهر في هذه المدة غالباً فجعلوا المدة علامة في حق من لم تظهر له العلامة وأدنى مدة يعتبر فيها ظهور العلامة إثنتا عشر سنة في حقه وتسع سنين في حقها فإذا بلغا هذا السن، وأقرا بالبلوغ كانا حكماً لأن ذلك مما يعرف من جهتهما . قوله : ( ولمن أفاق الخ) لعله للشكر على نعمة الأفاقة. قوله : (وعند الفراغ من (حجامة لما ورد أنه كان يغتسل من أربع منها الحجامة رواه أبو داود. قوله : (خروجاً للخلاف الأولى ما قاله السيد خروجاً من خلاف القائل بلزوم الغسل منهما قوله : ( وندب في ليله (براءة) سميت بذلك لأن الله تعالى يكتب لكل مؤمن براءة من النار لتوفية ما عليه من الحقوق، ولما فيها من البراءة من الذنوب بغفرانها، قاله العمروسي . قوله: (يقيناً) بأن يكون بطريق الكشف مثلاً قوله : (أو (علماً كذا هو فيما شرح عليه السيد أيضاً، والمناسب لمقابلة اليقين أن يقول : أو ظناً بأن يتبع الإمارة الواردة تعيينها، وهي كونها ليلة بلجة لا حارة، ولا باردة إلى غير ذلك مما ذكروه والذي فيما رأيته من الشرح، أو عملاً باتباع ما ورد والمعنى أن لرؤية إما باليقين أو بالعمل بما ورد من الإمارات. قوله : (لإحيائها) يحتمل إرتباطه بالغسل أي إنما ندب لإحيائها وفيه أن الأحياء مطلوب آخر ليس له تعلق بالغين
His saying: '(Ghusl is recommended) for one who becomes Muslim while in a state of purity' - this commands the one who becomes Muslim. He restricts it to those who are not impure, because if one becomes Muslim while impure (from janabah, menses, postpartum), then ghusl is obligatory upon him, as established previously. His saying: 'And for one who reaches puberty by age' - he restricts it to maturity by age, excluding maturation by wet dream, impregnation, or ejaculation for a boy, and by wet dream, menses, or pregnancy for a girl, for in those cases ghusl is unavoidable. His saying: 'And it is fifteen years according to the mufta bihi view' - this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and a narration from the Imam (Abu Hanifa), because signs of puberty generally appear within this period. They made this age a sign for those for whom no sign appears. The minimum age for consideration of a sign is twelve years for a boy and nine years for a girl. If they reach this age and claim puberty, they are legally considered pubescent because it is known from them. His saying: 'And for one who recovers (from insanity, intoxication, or fainting)' - perhaps it is for gratitude for the blessing of recovery. His saying: 'And upon completion of cupping' - because it is narrated that he used to perform ghusl for four things including cupping, reported by Abu Dawud. His saying: 'To exit from the disagreement' - what al-Sayyid said is better: to exit from the disagreement of those who say ghusl is necessary from both (cupping and washing a dead body). His saying: 'And it is recommended on the night of Bara'ah' - it is so named because Allah writes for every believer a decree of freedom from the Fire to fulfill what is due from him, and because of the freedom from sins by their forgiveness, as said by al-Amrusi. His saying: 'with certainty' - meaning by way of unveiling, for example. His saying: 'or knowledge' - as it is in the commentary of al-Sayyid as well. The appropriate opposite of certainty would be 'or conjecture' by following the signs mentioned for its determination, namely that it is a bright night, neither hot nor cold, and other signs they mentioned. In what I saw of the commentary: 'or action by following what is reported'. The meaning is that seeing it is either with certainty or by acting upon the reported signs. His saying: 'To revive it' - it may be connected to the ghusl, meaning that ghusl was recommended to revive it, but the revival itself is another desired act not related to the ghusl.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This section of the hashia covers the mandub (recommended) ghusls. It begins with ghusl for a convert to Islam. If the person converts while in a state of purity (not junub, etc.), ghusl is recommended (mandub) for cleanliness. But if they convert while impure (janabah, menstruation, postpartum), ghusl becomes obligatory (fard) to remove that state. Next is ghusl upon reaching puberty by age: the mufta bihi view is 15 years for both sexes. This applies when no other signs of puberty (like wet dreams) appear. If signs appear, ghusl is obligatory. For those who claim puberty at an earlier age (12 for boys, 9 for girls), their claim is accepted. Then ghusl for recovery from insanity, intoxication, or fainting, likely as thanksgiving. Then after cupping and after washing a dead body, to avoid disagreement with those who consider these ghusls obligatory. The commentary notes that some scholars hold these to be necessary. Then ghusl on the night of Bara'ah (mid-Sha'ban) because of its great virtue: Allah frees people from the Fire and forgives sins. Finally, ghusl on Laylat al-Qadr, but only if one is certain of the night (by spiritual unveiling) or follows the reported signs (e.g., a bright night, moderate temperature). The ghusl is recommended to revive the night, but reviving it itself is a separate act.

Key Terms
المندوب (al-mandub): A recommended act that is not sunnah muakkadah; performing it brings reward but omitting it does not incur sin. In this text, it refers to ghusls that are not from the four sunnah ghusls.
البلوغ (al-bulugh): Legal maturity; in the context of ghusl, it is the age at which one is required to perform ghusl if no other signs appear. The mufta bihi age is 15 lunar years for both boys and girls.
براءة (bara'ah): The night of mid-Sha'ban, a virtuous night in which Allah writes decrees and frees people from Hellfire.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling of ghusl for a person who converts to Islam while in a state of janabah?
A) Mandub (recommended)
B) Makruh (disliked)
C) Fard (obligatory)
D) Sunnah muakkadah

Key Takeaway
The majority of the sixteen ghusls are mandub, but the ruling can shift to fard when the person is in a state of major impurity at the time of the occasion.

Recommended Occasions for Ghusl (Sunnah/Mandub)
Listing and explaining recommended occasions for ghusl with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling (sunnah) for the recommended occasions for ghusl and explain the evidence (dalil) for one example (cupping).

Core Idea
The Hanafi school extends the sunnah ghusl to numerous occasions, emphasizing comprehensive purification for acts of worship.

In this passage, al-Tahtawi explains the occasions where ghusl is recommended (mandub) and clarifies that the correct view is that they are sunnah. You will learn the key occasions, the evidence from the Sunnah, and an important intra-Hanafi difference regarding the age of puberty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hukm level
Determine whether the text classifies these ghusl as fard, wajib, sunnah, or mandub. Note that the base text uses 'mandub' but the commentary elevates them to 'sunnah'.
Hanafi distinction: sunnah is more confirmed than mustahabb.
2
List the occasions
Go through each occasion mentioned in the passage and note the category (sunnah/mandub). Pay attention to specific conditions like 'li-man aslama tahiran'.
The list is approximately sixteen, but the text says it can exceed that.
3
Examine the dalil
Identify the proof cited for ghusl after cupping. How does the Hanafi school derive sunnah from the hadith in Abu Dawud?
The hadith shows the Prophet's action, indicating recommendation, not obligation.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
On the age of puberty, Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ. Which is the mufta bihi and why?
The relied-upon view is 15 years for both males and females.
5
Understand the principle
Why does the base text use 'mandub' but the commentary say 'sunnah'? What does this tell us about Hanafi terminology?
This illustrates the evolution from matn to sharh and the consolidation of the school.

His statement: (with seeking forgiveness etc.) is a depiction of the request or the ba is causative. Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah discusses the various occasions where ghusl (ritual bath) is recommended (sunnah or mandub). The author first notes that the earlier section covered the sunnah ghusl (like Friday, etc.), and now he moves to the mandub (recommended) ones. The text lists approximately sixteen occasions, though it notes there are more. The key occasions include: 1) For a person who becomes Muslim while already in a state of purity (i.e., not junub), for cleanliness from the past state; 2) For one who reaches puberty by age – the relied-upon (mufta bihi) age is fifteen years for both boys and girls; there is an intra-Hanafi difference as Abu Hanifa had a different narration, but the fatwa follows the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; 3) For one who recovers from insanity, intoxication, or fainting, possibly as gratitude; 4) After cupping and after washing a dead body, to avoid the opinion of those who say it is necessary; a hadith (Abu Dawud) mentions that the Prophet (peace be upon him) did ghusl from four things including cupping; 5) On the night of Bara'ah (mid-Sha'ban) and Laylat al-Qadr (if one sees it with certainty or by following signs) to revive these nights; 6) For entering Madinah, to honor the Prophet's city; 7) For standing at Muzdalifah; 8) Upon entering Mecca; 9) For tawaf al-qudum (arrival tawaf) and tawaf al-ziyarah (visit tawaf); 10) For the prayers of solar and lunar eclipses; 11) For the prayer of seeking rain (istisqa'); 12) For prayer during times of fear (faza'), darkness, or severe wind; 13) For the repentant person; 14) For the one returning from travel; and 15) For the mustahadah (woman with chronic uterine bleeding) – though here the text is unclear whether ghusl is required or recommended. The commentary clarifies several points: that the sunnah status of these ghusl is the correct view (some earlier Hanafi sources called them mustahabb), that one ghusl can suffice for multiple occasions with intention (e.g., on a day with Muzdalifah, jamarat, and tawaf), and that the best of the earth according to the Hanafis is Mecca absolutely, but Madinah holds special virtue, especially the Prophet's grave. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi tendency to extend the category of sunnah ghusl beyond the obligatory (janabah, menstruation, postpartum) to various spiritual and physical occasions, emphasizing complete purification for acts of worship.

Key Terms
مندوب (mandub): An act recommended but not obligatory; its omission is not sinful, but doing it brings reward. The text uses it interchangeably with sunnah in some contexts.
غسل (ghusl): A full-body ritual bath with specific method: water reaches every hair and skin, with intention and washing the whole body.
مفتى به (mufta bihi): The relied-upon legal ruling for fatwa. Here, for puberty age, it is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: 15 years.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school encourages ghusl on many spiritual and physical occasions, establishing them as sunnah to promote comprehensive purification.

Recommended Baths (Ghusl) for Various Occasions
Enumeration of recommended ghusl occasions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the recommended occasions for ghusl in the Hanafi school and explain the rationale behind each.

Core Idea
Ghusl is recommended (mandub) for specific acts of worship, travel, calamities, and purification needs—not obligatory.

The Hanafi school designates certain situations as occasions (mandub) for performing full ritual washing (ghusl). These range from entering sacred cities to natural phenomena and personal events. This passage from Maraqi al-Falah enumerates these occasions with brief juristic reasons, helping you distinguish recommended baths from obligatory ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal category
Observe that the text uses 'yandabu' (it is recommended), placing these ghusl in the category of mandub (sunnah ghayr muakkadah). Recognize that this is below wajib but above mubah.
Mandub in Hanafi terminology is a non-obligatory act that earns reward.
2
List the occasions in order
Read through the Arabic and translation to compile all the occasions mentioned. Group them by theme: worship (e.g., tawaf, standing at Muzdalifah), travel (entering Madinah, returning from travel), calamities (eclipse, fear, darkness, severe wind), and personal states (repentance, irregular bleeding, execution, impurity).
The hashya adds brief rationale for each.
3
Understand the rationale
For each occasion, note the reason given in the commentary: reverence for sacred places, following the Prophet's practice, seeking Allah's mercy, or precautionary purity. This helps internalize the wisdom behind the recommendation.
E.g., standing at Muzdalifah is linked to the Prophet's prayer for forgiveness.
4
Distinguish from obligatory ghusl
Compare with obligatory ghusl (janabah, hayd, nifas). These are recommended, not required. Use the principle: omitting a mandub does not invalidate worship but missing out on reward.
The Hanafi school is specific about this distinction.
5
Apply the ruling in practice
When encountering any of these situations, one may choose to perform ghusl as a recommended act. Understand the flexibility: it is not binding but highly encouraged.
This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on flexibility in non-obligatory matters.

For entering the city of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to venerate its sanctity and his arrival in the presence of the Chosen One (peace be upon him); and it is recommended for standing at Muzdalifah because it is the second gathering and the place of answering the prayer of the lord of the two worlds for forgiveness of blood and injustices for his nation on the morning of the Day of Sacrifice after the break of dawn because by it enters the time of standing at Muzdalifah and he leaves just before sunrise. Upon entering Makkah, may Allah honor it, for Tawaf and Tawaf al-Ziyarah, so he performs the tawaf with the most complete of the two purifications and performs the veneration of the sanctity of the noble House; and it is recommended to pray the solar and lunar eclipse prayers to perform the sunnah of their prayers; and for the prayer for rain to seek the sending down of rain as mercy for creation through seeking forgiveness and supplication and prayer with the most complete of the two purifications; and for the prayer of one in fear of something frightening, seeking refuge with Allah Almighty and His generosity to remove distress from him; and from darkness that occurs by day and from severe wind by night or day because Allah destroyed by it those who transgressed like the people of 'Ad, so the purified one seeks refuge with Him; and it is recommended for the repentant from sin, for the one returning from travel, for the woman with irregular bleeding when her bleeding stops, for the one whose execution is intended, for throwing the pebbles, and for one whom impurity has affected and its location is hidden, so he washes his entire body, and likewise his entire garment as a precaution.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration from the matn of Maraqi al-Falah of occasions on which ghusl is recommended (mandub). The hashya elaborates on each, providing brief juristic rationale. For instance, standing at Muzdalifah is linked to the Prophet's prayer for the community; entering Makkah and Madinah emphasizes reverence; prayers for eclipses and rain are performed with complete purification; fear and calamities prompt turning to Allah; and practical cases like returning from travel, bleeding cessation, and execution reflect care for spiritual and physical purity. The final case of unknown impurity demands thorough washing as an ihtiyat (precaution). All these are considered mustahabb (recommended) in the Hanafi school, not obligatory.

Key Terms
غسل (ghusl): Full ritual washing of the entire body with intention, covering all limbs.
ندب (nadb): Recommended act; also called mustahabb or sunnah ghayr muakkadah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) As a recommended act (mandub)
C) Obligatory before each throw
D) Only if the pilgrim is in a state of janabah

Key Takeaway
Ghusl is recommended (mandub) for a variety of occasions; knowing the list helps a Muslim seek extra reward and purification without confusing it with obligations.

Inner Purity as a Condition for Outer Purification's Benefit
Admonition on necessity of inner purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that inner purity (sincerity and avoidance of vices) is a necessary condition for outer purification to be spiritually beneficial in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Outer purification without inner purity yields no spiritual benefit.

This lesson explores a profound admonition from the Hanafi tradition: ritual purification (wudu, ghusl) is not sufficient for spiritual reward unless accompanied by inner purification of the heart. The text defines inner purity as sincerity (ikhlas) and freedom from grudges, deceit, hatred, and envy. Understanding this condition is essential for acts of worship to be truly accepted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Claim
Read the opening statement: 'Outer purification does not benefit except with inner purification.' Note that this is a condition (shart) for the spiritual benefit, not necessarily for legal validity.
2
Define Inner Purification
Break down the components: sincerity (ikhlas) and avoidance of moral vices (grudges, deceit, hatred, envy). Understand that these are not merely recommended but essential for the act to be beneficial.
The text quotes from lexical works to define each vice precisely.
3
Analyze the Heart's Purification
Examine the requirement to purify the heart from attachment to worldly things and worship Allah solely for His essence, not for reward or fear.
This is described as a state of being a 'unique servant' of the Unique Owner.
4
Apply to Worship
Consider how this condition applies to daily acts like wudu and prayer. If one performs them with correct outward form but harbors inner vices, the spiritual benefit is nullified.
This is a spiritual condition, not legal invalidation.

Source Text

A great admonition: Outer purification does not benefit except with inner purification through sincerity and avoiding grudges, deceit, hatred, envy, and purifying the heart from anything other than the two worlds so that he worships Him for His essence, not for a cause, being in need of Him, and He bestows favor by fulfilling his necessities due to compassion, so you become a unique servant to the unique Owner, nothing other than Him enslaves you, and not God. For cleanliness. He said: concerning the mustahada... He said: concerning the one to be killed... He said: concerning the one afflicted by impurity... He said: 'Outer purification does not benefit' meaning that which is required in some acts of worship; the meaning is that it does not fully benefit, as it is undeniable that its existence is not like its non-existence. He said: 'with sincerity' is a depiction of inner purification. He said: 'and being free' meaning keeping away from grudges. He said in al-Qamus: 'al-ghalil' is rancor like 'al-ghill' with kasra and 'al-dighn'. He said: in the root of h-q-d: 'haqida' meaning he held enmity in his heart and awaited its opportunity... and from this it is known that 'al-ghill' and 'al-hiqd' are the same. He said: in the root of gh-sh-sh: 'ghashshahu' means he did not sincere advice, or he showed other than what he conceals... so deceit in some interpretations returns to what preceded. As for envy, may Allah protect us from it, it is known. He said: 'and purifying the heart' is a conjunct to sincerity, meaning he purifies it by cutting ties from the whole creation and what souls aspire to, so he intends only Allah, worshiping Him because He deserves worship, obeying His command, observing His majesty and grandeur, not desiring Paradise nor fearing Hell. From the commentary. He said: 'being in need' meaning showing his need to Him by asking for his religious and worldly needs, displaying poverty and necessity to the Master Rich beyond everything after purifying his tongue from idle talk, let alone lies, backbiting, slander, and calumny, and adorning it with glorification, saying there is no god but Allah, tasbih, and recitation of the Quran, perhaps he will be characterized by some qualities of fulfilling covenants, preserving boundaries, being pleased with what exists, and patience for what is missed. He said: 'servitude is in the commentary'. He said: 'by favor' meaning beneficence, not obligation upon Him. He said: 'the necessities' meaning because of them. He said: 'compassionately' with fatha on the 'ayn meaning mercy and tenderness, and with kasr the side. He said: 'so you become a unique servant' meaning not shared, from the words of al-Hajj, may Allah benefit us by him: among the signs of the gnostic is that he is free from the affairs of both worlds, occupied with Allah alone. He said: it is not for one who sees anyone or mentions anyone to say he knows the One from whom the ones appear. He said: whoever fears something other than Allah or hopes for another, the doors of everything are closed upon him, and fear is empowered over him, and he is veiled by seventy veils, the easiest of which is doubt. He said: 'and does not claim ownership' the sin and ta are extra, or the prohibition of seeking inclination is more emphatic than the prohibition of inclination itself. He said:
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This is a concluding admonition (tanbih) emphasizing that outer purification (taharah zahirah) is not spiritually effective unless accompanied by inner purification (taharah batinah). The hashya defines inner purity as sincerity (ikhlas) and avoidance of grudges (ghill), deceit (ghishsh), hatred (hiqd), and envy (hasad). It then lexically defines these terms, quoting from al-Qamus. The heart must be purified from attachment to anything other than Allah, worshiping Him for His essence, not for reward or fear. The servant's complete reliance on Allah, supplication in need, and purification of the tongue are also highlighted. The commentary concludes with a profound statement from a pious scholar (al-Hajj) about the state of a true gnostic ('arif) who is detached from both worlds and solely focused on Allah. The final notes clarify that the phrase 'wa la yastamlik' emphasizes avoiding even seeking ownership or inclination towards other than Allah.

Key Terms
الطهارة الباطنة (al-taharah al-batinah): Inner purity consisting of sincerity (ikhlas) and avoidance of moral vices like grudges, deceit, hatred, and envy.
إخلاص (ikhlas): Sincerity of intention, worshiping Allah solely for His sake without ulterior motives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Necessity of Inner Purity for Outer Purification
Spiritual condition for acceptance of outward purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why external purification (taharah zahirah) alone is insufficient without inner purity (taharah batinah) and identify the components of inner purification required according to this Hanafi text.

Core Idea
External purification benefits only when the heart is purified from sins and devoted solely to Allah.

In the chapter on purity (kitab al-taharah), the Hanafi scholar al-Tahtawi, commenting on Maraqi al-Falah, emphasizes a critical spiritual dimension: the outer acts of purification (wudu, ghusl, tayammum) are not truly beneficial unless the heart is also purified. This lesson unpacks that principle, identifying the elements of inner purification—sincerity, avoidance of rancor, deceit, spite, envy, and detachment from worldly attachments—and the goal of worshipping Allah for His essence alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Locate the key claim: 'La tanfa' al-taharah al-zahirah illa al-taharah al-batinah' (External purification does not benefit except internal purification). This is the core ruling of the passage.
The ruling is not about validity but about true benefit (naf').
2
List the Requirements of Inner Purification
Find the specific elements mentioned: sincerity (ikhlas), distancing from rancor (ghill), deceit (ghish), spite (hiqd), envy (hasad), and purifying the heart from everything other than Allah.
These are not physical but spiritual conditions.
3
Understand the Ultimate Objective
The passage states that one should worship Allah for His essence (li dhatihi) and not for any cause (la li 'illah). This means worship should be pure, not motivated by fear or reward.
This is a high spiritual station (maqam al-ihsan).
4
Recognize the Outcome
The result is becoming an individual slave to Allah alone, not enslaved by anything else. The phrase 'la yasta'rika shay'un min al-ashya siwahu' emphasizes absolute servitude to Allah.
This ties inner purity to complete tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تنفع الطهارة الظاهرة إلا الطهارة الباطنة بالإخلاص والنزاهة عن الغسل والغش والحدق والحسد وتطهير القلب عما سوى من الكونين فيعبده لذاته لا لعلة مفتقراً إليه وهو يتفضل بالمن بقضاء حوائجه المضطر بها عطفاً عليه فتكون عبداً فرداً للمالك لأحد الفرد لا يسترقك شيء من الأشياء سواه ولا الله للنظافة.
The external purification does not benefit except the internal purification with sincerity and distancing from rancor, deceit, spite, and envy, and purification of the heart from what is other than the two worlds, so that he worships Him for His essence not for a cause, being in need of Him, and He bestows favor by granting his necessary needs out of compassion towards him, so you become an individual slave to the King, to the One, the Individual, nothing of things enslaves you besides Him, and Allah for cleanliness.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on purity (kitab al-tahara) in Maraqi al-Falah states a key principle: external purification (like wudu and ghusl) is not truly beneficial unless accompanied by internal purification (tahara batina). Internal purification includes sincerity (ikhlas), avoiding hidden rancor (ghill), deceit (ghish), spite (hiqd), and envy (hasad), and purifying the heart from all attachments other than Allah. The ultimate goal is to worship Allah solely for His essence, not for any cause such as paradise or fear of hell, while recognizing one's utter dependence on Him. The passage emphasizes that a true servant is not enslaved by anything other than Allah.

Key Terms
الطهارة الظاهرة (al-taharah al-zahirah): External purification, i.e., ritual purity through water (wudu, ghusl) or earth (tayammum).
الطهارة الباطنة (al-taharah al-batinah): Inner purification of the heart from sins, bad character, and attaching to other than Allah.
الإخلاص (al-ikhlas): Sincerity in worship, doing deeds solely for Allah's sake without any worldly motive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Performing it three times
B) Accompaniment by internal purification with sincerity and heart purification
C) Reciting specific duas
D) Performing it in a specific order

Key Takeaway
Outer purity is valid without inner purity, but its true spiritual benefit depends on sincerity and a heart cleansed from vices and worldly attachments.

Definition and Ruling of Tayammum
Definition of tayammum linguistically and legally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of tayammum in the Hanafi school and identify intention as a necessary condition.

Core Idea
Tayammum is wiping face and hands with pure earth; intention is its condition.

This passage from Maraqi al-Falah marks the beginning of the chapter on tayammum. It distinguishes the linguistic meaning of 'tayammum' from its legal meaning in the Hanafi school. Understanding this definition is foundational for applying the rules of dry ablution correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning
Recognize that the word 'tayammum' in Arabic literally means 'intention' in an absolute sense, while 'hajj' literally means intention to a great place.
This linguistic foundation helps explain why intention (qasd) remains central in the legal ruling.
2
Contrast with legal meaning
Note that legally, tayammum is an action: wiping the face and hands with pure earth (sa'id mutahhar).
The legal definition specifies the body parts and the medium.
3
Recognize intention as a condition
The author explicitly states that intention (qasd) is a condition (shart) for tayammum, as it constitutes the niyyah.
Intention is not part of the definition but a prerequisite for validity.
4
Take note of cause and other conditions
The passage mentions that tayammum has a cause (sabab) and a condition (shart); further elaboration is found in later texts.
The cause is typically the absence of water or inability to use it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التيمم هو من خصائص هذه الأمة وهو لغة القصد مطلقاً، والحج لغة القصد إلى معظم، وشرعاً مسح الوجه واليدين عن صعيد مطهر، والقصد شرط له لأنه النية وله سبب وشرط
Chapter of Tayammum: It is from the special characteristics of this nation. Linguistically, it is absolute intention. Pilgrimage (Hajj) linguistically is intention to a great place. Legally, it is wiping the face and hands from pure earth. Intention is a condition for it because it is the intention, and it has a cause and a condition.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on tayammum (dry ablution) in Maraqi al-Falah. It states that tayammum is a unique concession (rukhsah) for the Muslim Ummah. The linguistic meaning of tayammum is 'intention' in an absolute sense, while hajj (pilgrimage) linguistically means 'intention to a great place'. The legal (shar'i) definition of tayammum is wiping the face and hands with pure earth (sa'id tahir). Intention (niyyah) is a condition for its validity; it also has a cause (sabab) - usually the absence of water or inability to use it - and conditions (shurut) for its performance.

Key Terms
التيمم (al-tayammum): The legal substitute for wudu or ghusl using pure earth when water is unavailable or harmful.
الصعيد (al-sa'id): Pure earth or soil, including dust, sand, stone, etc., that is clean and can be used for tayammum.
القصد (al-qasd): Intention; in the context of tayammum, it refers to the intention to perform tayammum for purification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Intention to perform worship
B) Wiping the whole body with earth
C) Wiping the face and hands with pure earth
D) A substitute for hajj

Key Takeaway
Tayammum is legally defined as wiping the face and hands with pure earth, and intention is a necessary condition for its validity.

Tayammum: Definition and Legal Aspects
Opening of tayammum chapter with definition and four legal aspects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of tayammum and identify its four key aspects: hukm, rukn, sifah, and kayfiyyah.

Core Idea
Tayammum is wiping face and hands with pure earth, fard for prayer, with intention as a condition.

This lesson opens the chapter on tayammum, a special purification for this Ummah. We explore its linguistic and legal definitions, then break down its ruling (hukm), pillar (rukn), description (sifah), and modality (kayfiyyah). Understanding these four aspects is essential for correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the linguistic definition
Identify that 'tayammum' linguistically means 'intending' (qasd) absolutely, while 'hajj' linguistically means 'intending' to the Sacred House. Note how the author distinguishes the two.
The linguistic root helps connect the term to intention (niyyah) in the legal definition.
2
Extract the legal definition
The legal definition is 'wiping the face and the two hands from pure earth.' Note that 'from' (عن) indicates the wiping is occasioned by touching pure earth. Intention is a condition, not a pillar, contrary to some definitions.
The author prefers this definition over one that includes intending pure earth as a pillar.
3
Identify the four legal aspects
The passage lists: (1) hukm (ruling): tayammum makes permissible what was prohibited; (2) rukn (pillar): comprehensive wiping of the limb; (3) sifah (description): it is fard for prayer absolutely, and mustahabb for entering the mosque with hadath; (4) kayfiyyah (modality): wipe the right hand with the left, and turn it to ensure complete coverage.
These four aspects mirror the structure of many Hanafi fiqh texts.
4
Note the condition of pure earth
The earth must be 'mutahhir' (purifying). Impure earth (najis) cannot be used even if dry. This excludes surfaces that nullify the purity condition.
This condition is derived from the definition itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التيمم هو من خصائص هذه الأمة وهو لغة القصد مطلقاً، والحج لغة القصد إلى معظم، وشرعاً مسح الوجه واليدين عن صعيد مطهر، والقصد شرط له لأنه النية وله سبب وشرط، قال الكمال: هذا هو الحق، فهذا هو التعريف أولى من قول بعضهم في تعريفه قصد الصعيد الطاهر، واستعماله بصفة مخصوصة فإنه جعل القصد ركناً. قوله : ( عن صعيد) أي الناشئ هذا المسح عن صعيد أي مس صعيد. قوله : (مطهر) احترز به عن الأرض إذا تنجست وجفت فإنه لا يتيمم عليها. قوله : (وشرط) كشرط أصله إلا فيما ستعلمه. قوله : (وحكم) هو حل ما كان ممتنعاً قبله في الدنيا، والثواب في الآخرة كأصله أيضاً. قوله : (وركن) هو المسح المستوعب للمعل. قوله: (وصفة) هو فرض للصلاة مطلقاً، ويندب لدخول المسجد محدثاً كما ستعلمه، ويجب فيما يجب فيه الوضوء. قوله : ( وكيفية) هي مسح اليمني باليسرى، وقلبه مستوعباً.
Chapter of Tayammum. It is a speciality of this Ummah. Linguistically, it is 'qasd' (intending) absolutely. Linguistically, Hajj is 'qasd' to the Most Sacred. Legally, it is wiping the face and the two hands from pure ground. Intention is a condition for it because it is the intention, and it has a cause and condition. Al-Kamal said: This is the truth, and this definition is better than the statement of some in its definition: 'intending pure earth and using it in a specific manner' because he made the intention a pillar. His statement: 'from pure ground' means the wiping arises from ground, i.e., touching ground. His statement: 'pure (mutahhir)' excludes the earth if it becomes najis and dries, for one does not perform tayammum on it. His statement: 'and condition' means like the conditions of its origin (wudhu) except as you will learn. His statement: 'and ruling' is that it makes permissible what was prohibited before it in this world, and reward in the Hereafter, also like its origin. His statement: 'and pillar' is the comprehensive wiping of the [limb?]. His statement: 'and description' is that it is fard for prayer absolutely, and recommended for entering the mosque while in a state of hadath as you will learn, and obligatory when wudhu is obligatory. His statement: 'and modality' is wiping the right with the left, and [turning?] it comprehensively.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on tayammum, first stating that tayammum is a special concession for this Ummah. Then it gives the linguistic and legal definitions. Linguistically, tayammum means 'intending' absolutely, while hajj means 'intending' to the Sacred House. Legally, tayammum is wiping the face and both hands with pure earth. The text then clarifies that the ruling of tayammum is permissibility: it makes permissible what was previously forbidden due to lack of water (e.g., prayer). The reward in the Hereafter is like that of wudhu. The pillar (rukn) is the complete wiping of the area (likely the face and hands). The description (sifah) states that tayammum is fard for prayer absolutely; it is also recommended for entering the mosque when one is in a state of minor impurity (hadath), and it becomes obligatory when wudhu is obligatory (e.g., for prayer). The modality (kayfiyyah) is to wipe the right hand with the left, and to wipe the entire area thoroughly.

Key Terms
تيمم (tayammum): Linguistically: intending; legally: wiping the face and hands with pure earth as a substitute for wudhu or ghusl.
صعيد (sa'id): Pure earth (ground) that is not mixed with najasah; the material used for tayammum.
قصد (qasd): Intention; here it is a condition for tayammum because tayammum requires niyyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Mandub (recommended)
C) Fard (obligatory) absolutely
D) Mubah (permissible)

Key Takeaway
Tayammum is wiping face and hands with pure earth, with intention as a condition, and is fard for prayer when water is unavailable.

Conditions of Tayammum: Intention and Its Rules
Lists conditions of tayammum and details on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eight conditions of tayammum, explain the role of intention, and apply the Hanafi ruling that a general intention of purification suffices for a junub.

Core Idea
For tayammum, the intention of purification from any hadath suffices; specifying janaba is not required.

Intention is the first condition of tayammum because earth is not naturally purifying; it requires intention to become so. This lesson explains the definition, timing, and conditions of intention, and clarifies a key Hanafi principle: a general intention to purify suffices even for a junub. Understanding this prevents the common mistake of requiring a specific intention for major impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eight conditions of tayammum
The passage states there are eight conditions, but only the first (intention) is detailed. Note the structure: the text first lists the conditions then elaborates on intention.
The other seven are not enumerated in this passage.
2
Examine the condition of intention
Understand why intention is needed (earth is impure), its reality (binding of heart), its time (at striking ground or wiping), and its three own conditions (Islam, tamyiz, knowledge).
Intention is distinct from knowledge; it is an additional mental act.
3
Analyze the special condition for tayammum's intention
The intention must be for prayer; three valid intentions are given: purification from hadath, istibahah (making prayer permissible), and raf' (removal) of hadath.
These three are functionally equivalent.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi position on specification
Note that specifying janaba is not required; a general intention of purification suffices. This is the mufta bihi position, supported by a sound narration from Ibn Samā'a.
Opposing view (Imam Muhammad in one narration) requires specification but is marjuh.
5
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a junub intends wudhu with tayammum, according to Hanafi position it is valid for janaba; according to opposing view, it would be invalid.
This affects daily practice for those in states of major impurity.

Source Text

Its cause is like that of its origin (wudhu): intending what is not permissible except with it. Its conditions he presented with his statement: 'Tayammum is valid with eight conditions. The first of them is intention, because earth is impure (multawwith) and does not become purifying except with intention, while water was created as purifying.' The reality of intention legally is the binding of the heart to perform the action with certainty. Its time is when he strikes his hand on that with which he performs tayammum, or when he wipes his limbs with the dust that touched them. Intention in itself has conditions for its validity, which he explained with his statement: 'The conditions for the validity of intention are three: Islam, so that the action becomes a cause for reward (and the disbeliever is deprived of it); secondly, discernment (tamyiz) to understand what he is saying; thirdly, knowledge of what he intends, to know the reality of the intended object.' And intention is a meaning beyond the knowledge that precedes it. Intention for tayammum has a specific condition, which he explained with his statement: 'It is required for the validity of tayammum, so that it becomes a key for prayer, that one of three things be intended: either the intention of purification from the hadath that exists in him (and it is not required to specify janaba from hadath, so the intention of purification suffices, because it was legislated for prayer and was made a condition for its validity and permissibility, so its intention was the intention of permitting prayer; therefore he said: or the intention of making prayer permissible (ibahah), because its permissibility is by lifting the hadath), so it is valid with absolute intention, and with the intention of lifting... His statement: 'because its permissibility is by lifting the hadath' is a justification for the validity of intention in tayammum.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage details the conditions of tayammum. It begins by stating that the cause (sabab) of tayammum is the same as its origin (wudhu): the desire to perform an act that is not permissible without purification (e.g., prayer). It then lists the eight conditions, but only elaborates on the first: intention (niyyah). Intention is required because earth is inherently impure (multawwith) and only becomes purifying through intention, whereas water was created as purifying. The reality of intention is the heart's resolve to perform the action with certainty. Its time is at the moment of striking the ground or wiping the limbs. Then the text discusses the three conditions for the validity of intention in general: Islam (so the act is rewarded), discernment (tamyiz: ability to understand), and knowledge of what one intends (to know the intended object). Intention is distinct from knowledge; it is an additional mental act. Finally, the text specifies a special condition for intention in tayammum: it must be for prayer, and three intentions are valid: (1) intending purification from the hadath (impurity) present; (2) intending making prayer permissible (istibahah); or (3) intending removal (raf') of the hadath. Importantly, specifying janaba (major impurity) from hadath is not required; if a junub intends wudhu, it suffices for janaba as well, according to a narration from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that the intention of purification alone is sufficient.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention: the heart's resolve to perform the action (tayammum) with certainty. It is a condition for tayammum's validity.
حدث (hadath): A state of ritual impurity (minor or major) that prevents prayer.
استباحة (istibahah): Making something permissible; here, intending tayammum to permit prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following intentions is NOT required to be specified for tayammum to be valid?
A) Intention of purification from hadath
B) Intention to make prayer permissible
C) Intention to remove janaba specifically
D) Intention of raising hadath

Key Takeaway
Intention is essential for tayammum, but a general intention to purify from impurity is sufficient; specifying janaba is not required.

The Intention (Niyyah) in Tayammum: Conditions and Valid Forms
Explaining the conditions and specifics of intention for tayammum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three valid intentions for tayammum and apply them to practical scenarios, and identify why intending tayammum alone is insufficient.

Core Idea
Intention for tayammum must be for purification, permissibility of prayer, or a specific worship — not the act of tayammum itself.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi delves into the precise requirements for intention (niyyah) in tayammum. Understanding these conditions is crucial because an incorrect or incomplete intention invalidates the entire purification, rendering any subsequent prayer void. We will break down the three valid categories of intention and clarify common pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Define intention and its legal status
Identify the definition of niyyah (resolve of the heart) and note that it is a condition (shart) for tayammum, not a pillar (rukn). Understand why intention is required: earth is not inherently purifying.
Niyyah is a condition that makes tayammum valid; without it, the act is like mere play with dust.
2
List the three general conditions of intention
For any intention to be valid, three prerequisites must be met: Islam (to merit reward), tamyiz (discernment to understand the act), and knowledge of what is intended.
These are universal for all acts of worship, not specific to tayammum.
3
Identify the three specific intentions for tayammum
The text states that for tayammum to be valid, the intention must be one of three: (1) purification from hadath, (2) permissibility of an act requiring purification (e.g., prayer), or (3) a specific worship that requires purification and is not valid without it.
Note that intention of tayammum alone (without linking to any of these) does not suffice.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi nuance about specification
Examine the narration where a junub person intends wudu with tayammum. The dominant view is that general intention suffices; this narration confirms that even a mistaken specification (intending wudu) is acceptable because it implies removal of hadath.
This shows flexibility: the intention does not need to name the exact type of hadath.
5
Apply the rules to practical scenarios
Test your understanding with scenarios: a person intends tayammum to read Quran while in minor hadath (invalid because reading Quran does not require purification), or a junub intends tayammum to pray without specifying janabah (valid as general purification).
The key is linking intention to purification, permissibility, or an act that demands purity.

Intention is a meaning beyond the knowledge that precedes it. Hence he said: or intention of permitting prayer, because its permissibility is by removing hadath, so it is valid with absolute intention, and with intention of removing hadath because tayammum removes it like wudu. But if he confines the intention to something, it must be specific, which he explained in the third condition: or intention of a specific worship that is not obligatory as part of something else by way of subordination, having been legislated originally as an approach to Allah, and also not valid without purification. So the intended is either prayer or a part of prayer in itself, like saying: I intend tayammum for prayer, or for funeral prayer, or prostration of recitation, or for reciting the Quran while he is junub, or she intended it for reciting after her menses or postpartum, because each of these requires purification and is worship. So the mutayammim does not pray if he intends tayammum alone, i.e. isolated without considering any of the above, or he intended tayammum for reciting the Quran while he has minor hadath and is not junub, and likewise a woman if she intended it for reciting while not obligated to purify from menses and postpartum because it is permissible for the one with minor hadath to recite, not the junub. If we scrutinize, we find both previous intentions refer back to the intention of removing hadath, because the intention of purification refers back to the intention of permissibility, and that refers back to the intention of removal, so reflect. His statement: 'it is valid with absolute intention' is a branch of his statement 'as for intention of purification', and it is not meant by absolute intention the intention of tayammum, for the author later stated that it is not valid with its intention. His statement: 'and with intention of removing hadath' is a branch of his statement 'because its permissibility is by removing hadath', and it is necessary to add our statement 'and it is valid with its intention'. His statement: 'But if he confines the intention to something' is an exception to an implied: 'this when he is absolute in intention', and it encompasses three scenarios: picture of intention of purification, picture of intention of permitting prayer, and picture of intention of removing hadath. His statement: 'which is not obligatory' etc., like prayer, unlike touching, for it became obligatory by way of subordination for recitation, and in itself it is not worship, nor is it originally approached. His statement: 'not valid without purification' i.e. or not permissible, to include reciting the Quran for one who is junub. His statement: 'in itself' i.e. considering its essence, and what is meant is that it is part in general, even if it actualizes non-part for another reason like prostration. His statement: 'or for funeral prayer' if he included it in the generality of prayer, so he says: so the intended is either prayer, even funeral prayer would be better because it is prayer in some respect. His statement: 'or prostration of recitation' - this and what follows are examples of the part of prayer in general. His statement: 'and it is worship' i.e. intended worship that is not valid without purification. His statement: 'so he does not pray with it' is a branch of the requirement of one of these three things. His statement: 'and he was not junub' is an explicit statement of the necessary. His statement: 'and she was not obligated to purify' i.e. she only has minor hadath. His statement: 'for the permissibility of the one with minor hadath to recite' i.e. it is intended worship but it is permissible without purification, so the third condition is lost. Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (hashiya) on the conditions of intention in tayammum from the matn of Maraqi al-Falah. The author explains that intention is the first of eight conditions for valid tayammum, because earth (unlike water) is not inherently purifying; it requires intention to become so. Intention is defined as the resolve of the heart to perform the action with certainty. Its time is when striking the hands on the earth or wiping the limbs. For the intention to be valid, three general conditions are needed: Islam (for the act to merit reward), discrimination (tamyīz, i.e., understanding what is being said), and knowledge of what one intends. Then the passage focuses on the specific condition for tayammum intention: it must be one of three things: (1) intention of purification from the existing hadath (whether minor or major, without needing to specify janabah), (2) intention of permitting an act that requires purification (like prayer), or (3) intention of a specific worship that requires purification, such as prayer, funeral prayer, prostration of recitation, or reciting the Quran while in a state of janabah. The intention of tayammum alone (without linking to any of these) does not suffice. Interestingly, the text mentions a narration from Ibn Sam'ah through Muhammad that if a junub person intends wudu with tayammum, it suffices for janabah – an intra-Hanafi nuance. The commentary also clarifies that all valid intentions ultimately refer back to the intention of removing hadath. The author warns against confusing 'absolute intention' (i.e., intending purification or permissibility) with intention of tayammum itself, which does not suffice.

Key Terms
النية (al-niyyah): The resolve of the heart to perform the action with certainty; in the context of tayammum, it is a condition that must be one of three forms: purification, permissibility, or specific worship.
التيمم (al-tayammum): Dry purification using pure earth when water is unavailable or harmful; its conditions include eight, first of which is intention.
الحدث (al-hadath): State of impurity that prevents prayer; includes minor (hadath asghar) and major (hadath akbar, e.g., janabah). The intention for tayammum must be to remove this state.
الاستباحة (al-istibaha): The permissibility (of prayer) that is achieved by removing hadath; one valid intention for tayammum is to intend istibaha al-salat (making prayer permissible).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person in minor hadath intends tayammum 'to read Quran'. Is this intention valid according to the passage?
A) Yes, because reading Quran is a worship that requires purification.
B) No, because reading Quran is permissible without purification for minor hadath.
C) Yes, because any intention linked to a worship is valid.
D) No, because one must intend purification from hadath specifically.

Key Takeaway
For tayammum, intention must be for purification, permissibility of an act requiring purity, or a specific worship that demands purification; intending tayammum alone is insufficient.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
